MODULE 90.3: EVENT-DRIVEN AND DISTRESSED STRATEGIES
LO 90.c: Describe the mechanics and risks of event-driven strategies such as
merger arbitrage.
Event-driven strategies focus on specific corporate events that create pricing
discrepancies and market inefficiencies. Event-driven investment opportunities can arise from
the following categories.
```
Strategic (hard catalysts) opportunities:
```
Risk arbitrage
Evaluating a firm’s strategic alternatives and taking a position
Potential spin-offs of divisions or business units
Taking positions in likely takeover targets
Proxy contests or activist shareholder campaigns
Stub trades, which isolate and profit from the discount between a holding company’s share
price and the value of its underlying assets
Financial opportunities:
Anticipating price movements based on credit re-ratings or liquidity events
Changes in accounting methods
Financial recapitalizations
Primary offerings of debt or equity
Bankruptcy reorganizations
Operational opportunities:
Synergies from mergers
Corporate turnaround or restructuring opportunities
Senior management changes
Legal/regulatory opportunities:
Taking positions based on litigation outcomes
Taking positions based on regulatory changes
Taking positions based on anticipated legislation
Technical opportunities:
Broken risk arbitrage situations
Secondary offerings of equity or equity-linked notes
Activist Strategy
An activist strategy involves taking an equity or derivative position in a company and then
seeking to influence management or the board of directors to pursue initiatives that the
activist believes will increase the share price. Activist investors may solicit proxy votes to
pressure the board. They may also advocate for changes in corporate governance, spin-offs
of business units, cost reductions, share repurchases, higher dividends, increased leverage, or
other actions intended to enhance shareholder value.
Merger Arbitrage
The goal of merger arbitrage, also known as risk arbitrage, is to profit from the spread
between the acquirer’s offer price and the target company’s stock price following the
announcement of an acquisition, purchase, or merger.
In cases where the acquiring firm plans to pay with its own stock in ashare-for-share
merger, the arbitrageur may short the acquirer’s stock while simultaneously purchasing the
target’s stock. The position is then unwound at the time of the merger, ideally at a profit.
In a merger arbitrage strategy, if the deal closes, the arbitrageur may benefit from the
```
following:
```
The arbitrage spread, which is the difference between the target firm’s stock price at the
announcement of the acquisition and the final bid price at closing
Dividends received on the target firm’s stock
Interest earned on the proceeds from the short position, net of borrowing costs and any
dividends owed on the shorted acquirer’s stock
The possibility of an increased bid, particularly when competing bidders emerge
However, there is also downside risk to this strategy. If the deal fails to close, then the
following could occur:
The target’s stock price typically falls back to its pre-announcement level, or possibly even
lower
The acquirer’s stock price may rise, which creates losses on the short position
It is important to recognize that in a merger arbitrage strategy the losses from a failed deal
can far exceed the gains earned when a deal is successfully completed.
```
EXAMPLE: Merger arbitrage
```
```
Arielle Way, Inc. (AWI), has offered to acquire Cockrell Industries (CI) for two shares of
```
AWI stock for each share of CI. Before the announcement, AWI was trading at $52 per
share and CI at $74 per share, so the offer represents roughly a 40% premium. Neither
firm pays dividends, and the transaction is expected to close within two months. Following
the announcement, AWI trades at $50 per share and CI at $95 per share. Determine the
appropriate merger arbitrage trade and the expected profit per CI share if the deal closes
as proposed.
```
Answer:
```
The merger arbitrage trade is as follows:
Buy 100 shares of CI at $95.
Sell short 200 shares of AWI at $50.
Anticipated result:
When the transaction closes, the short position will close, resulting in a profit of $5 per
CI share.
If competing bids emerge, CI’s share price may rise further while AWI’s share price may
decline, potentially increasing profits.
The annualized expected return on a merger arbitrage is computed as follows:
Many long-only investors, such as mutual funds, are not attracted to the expected returns
from buying the target’s stock because the potential gain, which is the difference between
the acquisition price and the post-announcement price, is relatively small and there is
uncertainty about whether the deal will close. Hedge funds, however, can apply specialized
expertise in legal and antitrust matters to better assess the likelihood of a successful merger.
In addition, they can use the accompanying short position to generate incremental return.
```
EXAMPLE: Expected return on merger arbitrage
```
Global Semiconductors makes a tender offer to the shareholders of Local Semiconductors at
$12 per share. Before the acquisition announcement, Local’s stock was trading at $9 per
share, and it immediately rose to $10.50 after the offer. The deal is expected to close in six
months, with an estimated 90% probability of completion. Calculate the expected
annualized return from entering a merger arbitrage position in Local.
```
Answer:
```
```
C = 0.90; G = $12 – $10.50 = $1.50; L = $10.50 – $9 = $1.50; P = $10.50; Y = 6 / 12 =
```
0.50
Distressed Securities Strategy
LO 90.d: Explain the mechanics of a distressed securities strategy, and describe
challenges, risks and considerations that may arise when implementing this
strategy.
Overview and Return Opportunities
A distressed securities strategy involves taking speculative positions in the securities of
firms that are either entering, in, or emerging from, bankruptcy. These situations can offer a
range of return opportunities. Examples of potential returns from distressed securities
strategies include the following:
Investors may buy shares in a company and hold them until it emerges from bankruptcy.
They may also purchase non-investment-grade bonds that are attractively priced because
many institutional investors and pension funds are prohibited from owning such debt.
Banks may choose to sell portions of their distressed loans to clean up their balance
sheets, creating opportunities for investors to purchase this debt.
Trade claims can also be attractive, as the original holders often lack the expertise or the
desire to remain involved throughout the bankruptcy process.
In other words, investors may be able to acquire these distressed securities or claims at
substantial discounts to their risk-adjusted values.
Ways to profit from distressed securities include the following:
Investors can buy and hold the securities of a firm entering restructuring in anticipation
that their value will increase once the firm emerges from the process.
Investors can analyze the pricing of senior and junior positions within a firm’s capital
structure and look for mispricings among the issuer’s various securities. For example,
during restructuring, senior securities may appreciate relative to junior securities. To exploit
this mispricing, an investor can purchase the senior securities and short the junior
securities before prices adjust.
```
Investor Approaches (Active vs. Passive)
```
```
Investors in distressed securities may be either active or passive. Active investors may (1)
```
```
attempt to influence the restructuring process by participating on a creditor committee, (2)
```
```
supervise the workout process (which may include out-of-court restructurings or in-court
```
```
bankruptcy proceedings) to protect their interests, or (3) engage in legal proceedings when
```
doing so could improve their position. Passive investors, by contrast, choose not to get
involved and prefer distressed investments that are less complex and less time intensive to
analyze and monitor.
Challenges, Risks, and Key Considerations
Distressed investing involves significant uncertainty because these firms often have unstable
cash flows, limited liquidity, and complex capital structures. Valuation can be difficult,
recovery estimates may be unreliable, and the restructuring or bankruptcy process can take
much longer than expected. Distressed securities are frequently illiquid, which makes it
challenging to adjust or exit positions without affecting prices.
In addition, outcomes depend heavily on legal and regulatory factors, including court rulings,
creditor negotiations, debtor-in-possession financing, and possible changes in bankruptcy law.
Competition from other distressed investors, unexpected deterioration in business conditions,
or the issuance of new senior debt can further erode recovery values. As a result, distressed
investing requires specialized expertise in financial analysis, legal processes, and corporate
restructuring.
```
EXAMPLE: Distressed investing
```
```
Naples Ocean Views (NOV), a real estate holding company, may be forced to file for
```
bankruptcy. The firm has $1 billion of 5% debt maturing in 10 years, which is currently
trading at a steep discount of $30 per $100 of face value. Bankruptcy is expected within
two years. NOV’s stock is trading at $3 per share. In the event of liquidation, the bonds are
estimated to be worth $35 per $100 of face value. Assume an investor follows the
distressed strategy of buying one NOV bond with $1,000 face value at $300 and shorting
100 shares of NOV stock at $3. If the firm is liquidated in two years, calculate the gains
on the bond position and the equity short position.
```
Answer:
```
```
Gain on bond position: Receive coupon payments of 5% on $1,000 for two years ($50 per
```
```
year, total $100), plus $350 in liquidation value, for total inflows of $450. Net gain on the
```
```
bond = $450 – $300 = $150.
```
Gain on equity short position: Short sale proceeds are $300, and the stock becomes
worthless, so the investor does not need to buy back the shares. Net gain on the equity
```
short = $300.
```
Total gain = $150 + $300 = $450
MODULE QUIZ 90.3
1. Novelty Healthcare has received a tender offer from a larger competitor. The offer terms specify a
price of $30 per share. Before the announcement, Novelty’s stock traded at $25, and it rose to
$29 after the offer was made public. Analysts at Primus Fund estimate that the deal has a 75%
probability of closing and expect completion within three months. What is the annualized
expected return from a merger arbitrage position in Novelty’s stock?
A. −3.45%.
B. −3.33%.
C. 0.25%.
D. 11.25%.
2. Which of the following scenarios best reflects the core risk faced by investors in distressed
securities?
A. A restructuring process takes longer than expected.
B. Senior secured creditors receive the lowest recoveries.
C. Bankruptcy guarantees predictable outcomes for all creditors.
D. High trading volume in distressed debt limits position building.
KEY CONCEPTS
LO 90.a
Equity long/short strategies take long and short positions in related securities to exploit
mispricings while reducing market risk. Returns come from alpha on long and short positions,
interest rebates on short sale proceeds, and interest earned on required liquidity buffers. Key
costs include margin financing on short positions, stock borrowing fees, and transaction
costs.
Dedicated short or short-biased strategies focus on identifying overvalued securities and
profiting from price declines. Managers rely on skills such as detecting aggressive accounting
practices and legal red flags. These strategies carry significant risk because rising markets
work against short positions and can trigger margin calls, short squeezes, and forced
covering.
Nonhedged equity strategies are long-only approaches that rely on fundamental analysis and
may use leverage or derivatives to enhance returns. Unlike long/short strategies, they retain
full market exposure.
Global macro strategies make large, directional, and often leveraged bets across asset classes
including equities, fixed income, currencies, and commodities based on expectations of
macroeconomic and geopolitical trends. Managers often use derivatives to express these
views.
Emerging markets strategies invest in the corporate or sovereign securities of developing
countries. These strategies seek higher returns but involve added risks related to political
conditions, liquidity constraints, and local economic factors.
LO 90.b
Fixed-income arbitrage is a long/short approach that seeks to exploit pricing inefficiencies
among fixed-income securities, such as yield divergences or liquidity-driven mispricing
between similar bonds.
Convertible arbitrage involves buying a convertible bond and shorting the underlying stock to
neutralize equity exposure. Returns come from the bond’s income, interest earned on short
sale proceeds, and the ability to monetize volatility through dynamic delta hedging.
Relative value arbitrage looks for pricing discrepancies across related assets. A common
example is pairs trading, where an investor buys one security and shorts another with a
historically strong price relationship, expecting convergence. Another example involves
exploiting mispricings between index futures and their underlying constituents.
LO 90.c
Event-driven strategies seek to profit from pricing inefficiencies created by specific corporate
events such as mergers, acquisitions, restructurings, recapitalizations, or other strategic
catalysts.
Activist investing involves taking an equity or derivative position in a firm and attempting to
influence management or the board to implement changes that the activist believes will
increase shareholder value. Success depends on whether management adopts the proposed
```
initiatives and whether the share price responds positively; failure occurs when no meaningful
```
changes are made.
```
Merger arbitrage (risk arbitrage) typically involves buying a target company’s stock and, in
```
share-for-share deals, shorting the acquirer’s stock. The strategy seeks to capture the spread
between the target’s post-announcement price and the offer price. Profits are realized if the
deal closes as expected, whereas substantial losses can occur if the deal falls through and the
target’s stock price drops sharply.
LO 90.d
Distressed investing involves purchasing the deeply discounted debt of firms entering, in, or
emerging from, bankruptcy, and sometimes shorting the equity if it is expected to lose most
of its value. Profits arise when the recovery value of the debt exceeds the purchase price or
from mispricings among different levels of the firm’s capital structure.
Investors may be active or passive. Active investors participate in creditor committees or
influence restructuring decisions, while passive investors avoid involvement and focus on
simpler situations requiring less legal or operational expertise.
Distressed strategies face notable risks, including valuation uncertainty, illiquidity, long and
unpredictable restructuring timelines, and legal or regulatory complexity. Outcomes depend
heavily on court decisions, creditor negotiations, and recovery priority.

The following is a review of the Risk Management and Investment Management principles designed to address the learning
objectives set forth by GARP®. Cross-reference to GARP assigned reading—Stowell and Stowell, Chapter 14.
READING 91
RISK, REGULATION, AND ORGANIZATIONAL
STRUCTURE
Study Session 14
EXAM FOCUS
This reading focuses on the risk, regulation, and organizational structure of hedge funds. For
the exam, understand how risk can be assessed at both the portfolio level and the investment
level. Also, recognize the hedge fund incremental risks introduced through leverage,
regulation, short selling, transparency, and varying risk tolerances. These risks connect
directly to broader systemic risk and risks within the banking sector. Be aware of the
suggested approaches to mitigate hedge fund risks. From a regulatory perspective,
understand the reporting requirements and jurisdictional differences, including the available
exemptions. In addition, keep in mind the three high-level areas that raise regulatory
```
concern: investor protection, market integrity, and financial stability. Finally, understand how
```
and why hedge funds are typically structured as limited partnerships.
MODULE 91.1: HEDGE FUND RISKS AND SYSTEMIC RISK
Risks in Hedge Fund Investing
LO 91.a: Describe different risks faced by investors in hedge funds.
One way to think about the risks of hedge fund investing is to categorize them as either
portfolio-level or investment-level risks. Portfolio-level risks include benchmarking issues,
```
survivorship bias, and unrelated business taxable income (UBTI). In a hedge fund context,
```
UBTI can arise when debt-financed income becomes taxable for investors who are otherwise
```
tax exempt; investing through an offshore hedge fund can help mitigate this risk.
```
Investment-level risks include counterparty risk, conflicts of interest, key person risk,
strategy failure, style drift, regulatory changes, and correlation spikes. Figure 91.1
summarizes the types of investment-level risks.
Figure 91.1: Investment-Level Risks in Hedge Fund Investing
Another way to categorize hedge fund risks is through five incremental risks that are
particularly pronounced in hedge funds: leverage, regulation, short selling, transparency, and
risk tolerance.
Leverage
Hedge funds frequently amplify outcomes through leverage, which can take the form of on-
```
balance-sheet leverage (direct borrowing) or off-balance-sheet leverage (e.g., derivatives).
```
Figure 91.2 presents leverage data by strategy, based on 2020 findings from the U.S.
Department of the Treasury’s Office of Financial Research.
Figure 91.2: Hedge Fund Leverage by Strategy
```
When returns are positive, leverage is a powerful ally; when returns are negative, it becomes
```
a difficult adversary, amplifying losses just as it amplifies gains. Leverage can also increase a
hedge fund’s sensitivity to liquidity shocks. This concept will be discussed in more detail later
in this reading. It is important to note that most hedge fund leverage is collateralized with
```
cash or assets held by the fund; true uncollateralized borrowing is rare.
```
Regulation
Potential hedge fund investors must conduct their own due diligence because hedge funds
operate with significantly less regulatory oversight than open-end mutual funds. Under the
```
“private adviser exemption” in the Investment Advisers Act of 1940 (Advisers Act), a
```
hedge fund manager is exempt from registration if the manager advises fewer than 15
```
clients. The Global Financial Crisis (GFC) of 2007–2009 reminded markets that hedge funds
```
are indirectly regulated through their highly regulated counterparties, particularly banks.
Following the crisis, the Dodd-Frank Act of 2010 required all hedge fund advisers
managing at least $150 million in assets to register with the Securities and Exchange
```
Commission (SEC), maintain extensive records, make those records available to the SEC,
```
```
appoint a chief compliance officer (CCO), and undergo periodic SEC examinations.
```
Hedge fund regulations vary significantly across international markets. In 2010, the European
```
Union (EU) introduced the Alternative Investment Fund Managers Directive (AIFMD),
```
which established comprehensive regulations for hedge funds and private equity funds for the
```
first time. The directive requires mandatory registration and imposes limits on leverage; it
```
also sets detailed reporting, disclosure, and marketing rules for both EU-based and non-EU-
```
based funds. In 2011, the European Securities and Markets Authority (ESMA) was
```
created to interpret AIFMD provisions, although enforcement remains the responsibility of
individual member states.
In 2010, Singapore implemented regulations requiring hedge funds with more than $250
```
million in assets to register with the Monetary Authority of Singapore (MAS). Regulated
```
hedge funds must provide both quarterly unaudited reports and annual audited reports to
investors and the MAS. They are also required to obtain a capital markets services license
from the MAS.
In Hong Kong, hedge funds are regulated under the Securities and Futures Ordinance
```
(SFO), which defineshedge fund activities to include dealing in securities, leveraged foreign
```
exchange trading, and futures contracts. Hedge funds must obtain a license that corresponds
to the specific business activities they engage in. The SFO also outlines recommended
reporting and disclosure practices and places limits on marketing efforts to potential clients.
China has a distinctive regulatory framework for hedge funds, with funds classified as either
government supported or private, where the private segment is still in an early stage of
development. The primary regulator is the China Securities Regulatory Commission
```
(CSRC). The CSRC prohibits the use of leverage by Chinese hedge funds and restricts short
```
selling to only 50 securities listed on the CSI 300 Index. In addition, brokers are not allowed
to use client shares to facilitate short sales, which makes shorting extremely expensive, at
roughly 10% per year. While the CSRC imposes strict limitations on hedge funds, the China
```
Insurance Regulatory Commission (CIRC) has encouraged insurance companies to use
```
their own capital to establish asset management firms that can engage in hedge fund–like
investment strategies.
Short Selling
Short selling is a common hedge fund strategy. It involves borrowing shares, posting
collateral, adding additional collateral as required, and eventually repaying the borrowed
```
shares when the position is closed. While long positions have limited downside (because
```
```
financial assets cannot have negative values), short positions have theoretically unlimited
```
downside risk because a stock’s price has no upper bound. A short squeeze occurs when
the price of a heavily shorted stock rises rapidly, forcing short sellers to buy shares to close
their positions. This forced buying can further accelerate the stock’s price increase. A well-
```
known example is the 2021 short squeeze in GameStop (ticker: GME).
```
Transparency
Many hedge funds attempt to arbitrage perceived inefficiencies in financial markets. When a
trade becomes crowded with too many participants, the profit opportunity can quickly
disappear. As a result, hedge funds are often highly secretive about their specific strategies,
which makes it more difficult for investors to monitor risks and evaluate strategy alignment.
In addition, exit gates and other liquidity limitations can restrict investors who are concerned
about strategy shifts.
Risk Tolerance
Hedge fund managers generally have a higher risk tolerance than most open-end mutual
fund managers, and their compensation structures often incentivize them to take on
additional risk. They also have access to a wider range of investment opportunities, including
newer and more innovative strategies. In addition, they frequently use derivatives, which can
help mitigate risk but also introduce complexities that make risk assessment more difficult.
Impact of Hedge Funds on Systemic Risk
LO 91.b: Explain how the activities of hedge funds and exposure to hedge funds
can increase systemic risk.
Systemic Risk From Hedge Fund Activities
Systemic risk is the threat of disruption to the broader financial system or economy. Hedge
funds can contribute to systemic risk in two key ways. First, the failure of a hedge fund’s
strategy can trigger contagion if it is forced to unwind positions at fire sale prices. Second,
hedge funds can impose large losses on financial institutions that lend to hedge funds or act
as their counterparties.
```
The 1998 failure of Long-Term Capital Management (LTCM) underscored the
```
importance of monitoring liquidity and leverage. It also demonstrated that assets that
typically behave independently can become highly correlated and move in the same direction
during periods of extreme market stress.
The 2006 failure of Amaranth Advisors raised questions about the systemic risk posed by
hedge funds. The Bank of England concluded that key intermediaries contributed far more to
systemic risk than hedge funds, and that hedge funds may even facilitate risk transfer in
ways that reduce it. This view, however, is not universal. Critics argue that hedge fund
leverage and the use of complex, nonlinear strategies can increase systemic risk.
It is clear that the relationship between hedge funds and their bank counterparties can create
systemic risk. This was evident during the GFC, when lenders forced hedge funds to post
additional collateral by unwinding positions. The resulting fire sales contributed to contagion
and significant losses in asset classes that were otherwise uncorrelated. These effects are
amplified by leverage and by computer-driven selling. Margin calls from counterparties,
investor redemptions, and declining risk appetite during periods of market stress can all
increase selling pressure. The situation can worsen further when funds of hedge funds are
involved, as their redemptions cascade to the underlying hedge funds and create a waterfall
of additional selling.
Consider the example in Figure 91.3. A hedge fund owns $100 of stock financed with 25%
margin, which corresponds to 3x leverage. If the stock price falls by 10%, leverage rises to
5x because the loan amount does not change. To bring leverage back to 3x, the hedge fund
must sell $30 of the stock. This forced selling can create a downward spiral in which
increasingly large amounts of the position must be liquidated to meet leverage requirements
as the asset price declines. The assets being sold may have no fundamental reason to be sold
to this extent, but the leverage constraints make the selling unavoidable.
Figure 91.3: Contagion Risk From Forced Hedge Fund Selling Under Leverage
Regarding Scenario A, a hedge fund holds $100 of stock financed with 25% margin, which corresponds to
3.0x leverage. The position is funded with a $75 loan and $25 of equity.
Regarding Scenario B, the stock price declines by 10%, reducing the position’s value to $90. Because the
loan amount remains unchanged at $75, equity falls to $15 and leverage increases to 5.0x.
Regarding Scenario C, to bring leverage back to the target level of 3.0x, the hedge fund must sell $30 of
stock. After the sale, the remaining stock is valued at $60, supported by a $45 loan and $15 of equity.
Systemic Risk From Bank Exposure to Hedge Funds
Banks face three primary types of risk when they engage with hedge funds:
1. Revenue risk. Banks earn significant fee and service income from hedge funds through
trading, clearing, custody, securities lending, financing, and reporting activities. The failure
of multiple hedge funds could materially reduce this revenue stream.
2. Credit risk. Margin loans are secured by collateral, but the value of the underlying assets
can decline more quickly than anticipated, potentially leaving the bank undersecured.
3. Counterparty risk. Banks face exposure through derivatives transactions with hedge
```
funds, such as credit default swaps (CDSs) and other over-the-counter (OTC) contracts.
```
In 2021, the default of Archegos Capital highlighted the risks that highly leveraged
investment entities can pose to banks. Although Archegos operated as a family office rather
than a hedge fund, its use of total return swaps created exposures that closely resembled
those associated with hedge fund leverage. When Archegos collapsed, Credit Suisse incurred
losses of about $5 billion, and other banks collectively lost an additional $5 billion. The failure
sent a clear warning signal through the banking system, prompting banks to require hedge
funds to reduce leverage in recognition of the potential systemic risk to the sector.
There are three main approaches to mitigating the impact of hedge funds on systemic risk:
```
(1) more conservative lending practices by banks, (2) lower leverage and greater
```
```
diversification within hedge fund portfolios, and (3) reasonable regulation. Regarding the
```
third approach, excessively strict regulation of hedge funds and private equity firms could
have unintended negative effects on financial markets by reducing the liquidity that these
investors provide. Hedge funds often act as investors of last resort, taking on risks such as
distressed securities that traditional investors are unwilling to assume.
MODULE QUIZ 91.1
1. Hedge funds encounter the most significant restrictions on short selling in which of the following
jurisdictions?
A. China.
B. Singapore.
C. Hong Kong.
D. The European Union.
2. Which of the following actions is least likely to reduce systemic risk associated with hedge funds?
A. Strict new regulations.
B. Lower leverage in hedge fund portfolios.
C. More conservative lending practices by banks.
D. Greater diversification in hedge fund portfolios.
MODULE 91.2: REGULATION AND ORGANIZATIONAL STRUCTURE
Regulatory Approaches to Hedge Funds
LO 91.c: Summarize different approaches taken by regulators to mitigate risks
related to hedge funds.
U.S. Regulatory Framework
```
The Investment Company Act of 1940 (’40 Act) requires all mutual funds to register
```
with the SEC, which entails regular reporting and imposes limits on leverage, short selling,
and performance fees. These restrictions would be impractical for hedge funds, but they can
avoid registration under two key exemptions:
1. Section 3(c)(1) exemption. A hedge fund qualifies for this exemption if it has fewer
than 100 investors.
2. Section 3(c)(7) exemption. A hedge fund may also be exempt if it is limited to
qualified purchasers, defined as individuals with more than $5 million in investment
assets. However, funds relying on this exemption must register under the Securities
```
Exchange Act of 1934 (’34 Act) if they have 500 or more investors.
```
```
Managers of hedge funds are exempt from the Investment Advisers Act of 1940 (Advisers
```
```
Act) if they advise fewer than 15 funds, a provision known as the private adviser
```
exemption. Many hedge fund firms structure themselves to stay below this threshold. In
addition, hedge funds avoid triggering requirements under the Employee Retirement Income
```
Security Act of 1974 (ERISA) by limiting benefit plan participation to less than 25% of total
```
fund assets.
```
Understanding Regulation D (Reg D) is also important. Hedge funds market themselves
```
through private placements rather than public offerings, which means they do not advertise
to the general public. Under Reg D, hedge funds may accept only accredited investors,
defined as individuals with a net worth of at least $2.5 million or annual income of $250,000
in each of the prior two years with a reasonable expectation of maintaining that level.
However, Rule 506 of Reg D does permit up to 35 nonaccredited investors.
The United States and the United Kingdom have generally maintained a favorable regulatory
environment for hedge funds, although both have taken a strict view on side letters that
could advantage certain investors over others. In the United States, regulatory interest has
come in waves. The SEC attempted to require hedge fund registration through a 2004
rulemaking, but the U.S. Court of Appeals for the District of Columbia overturned the rule in
2006, and the SEC has not successfully mandated registration since that decision. In 2007,
the President’s Working Group on Financial Markets rejected broad new regulation and
instead encouraged the industry to adopt voluntary guidelines. However, the massive fraud
associated with Bernie Madoff and the turmoil of the GFC renewed scrutiny, ultimately leading
to the inclusion of hedge funds under the Dodd-Frank Wall Street Reform and Consumer
Protection Act of 2010.
Title IV of the Dodd-Frank Act, known as the Private Fund Investment Advisers Registration
Act, eliminates the private adviser exemption from the Advisers Act. As a result, the former
“15-client” threshold is no longer relevant, and hedge fund managers are now required to
register with the SEC. Title IV also mandates that hedge funds submit reports to both the
```
SEC and the Financial Stability Oversight Council (FSOC), enabling regulators to monitor
```
```
assets under management (AUM), leverage, counterparty exposures, and investment
```
strategies. In addition, it introduced Form PF, which requires detailed operational and
portfolio information to be filed annually by advisers with at least $150 million in AUM and
quarterly by those with more than $1.5 billion.
International Regulatory Approaches
Europe has taken a much more restrictive approach to hedge fund oversight than the United
States and the United Kingdom. Hedge funds managing more than €100 million in AUM face
extensive reporting requirements covering leverage, investment strategies, risk exposures,
and marketing activities. EU-domiciled hedge funds benefit from the EU Passport, which
makes it somewhat easier to manage and market funds across member states. European
regulators have also imposed disclosures on net short positions, restrictions on naked short
selling, mandatory central clearing for derivatives transactions, comprehensive reporting of all
derivatives trades, and measures intended to slow high-frequency trading.
Oversight still varies by country: Portugal closely regulates derivatives markets, France places
greater emphasis on limiting leverage, and Germany has taken steps to make it more difficult
for banks to lend to certain types of highly leveraged investment vehicles, including hedge
funds.
Regulatory Objectives and Industry Self-Regulation
At a high level, regulators are focused on three main concerns:
1. Investor protection. Regulators want to ensure that hedge funds are not defrauding
investors. This risk is mitigated by restricting participation to investors who are presumed
to be sophisticated enough to evaluate hedge fund risks.
2. Market integrity. Regulators monitor whether hedge funds are trading on insider
information, using the same rules and enforcement mechanisms applied across financial
markets.
3. Financial stability. Regulators seek to prevent hedge funds from destabilizing markets.
This concern is addressed by limiting leverage and requiring timely and accurate posting of
collateral.
Some have advocated for greater self-regulation within the hedge fund industry. The
Alternative Investment Management Association has contributed to this effort by publishing
itsGuide to Sound Practices for Hedge Fund Valuation. Public scrutiny also increases when
hedge fund managers, such as the Man Group in London or firms like Blackstone in the
United States, become publicly traded. This added transparency provides investors with more
information and indirect access to the hedge fund industry.
Hedge Fund Organizational Structure
LO 91.d: Describe a typical organizational structure for a hedge fund and explain
motivations for adopting this structure.
Organizational and Structural Design
Hedge funds strategically choose their domicile to minimize taxes and reduce regulatory
oversight. In the United States, Delaware is a common choice because of its favorable legal
```
environment. Offshore funds are most frequently located in the Cayman Islands (55%), the
```
```
British Virgin Islands (15%), and Bermuda (10%). While the choice of domicile can reduce
```
taxes at the fund level, managers still pay taxes on the fees they earn, and investors remain
subject to taxation on their investment gains in their home jurisdictions.
The legal entity used to form a hedge fund is typically a limited partnership. The hedge
fund manager serves as the general partner, while the investors are the limited partners.
This structure is usually organized as an open-ended partnership, allowing the fund to issue
new shares to incoming investors and redeem shares for those wishing to exit, subject to any
lockups and gates specified in the partnership agreement.
A master-feeder structure is commonly used to avoid multiple layers of taxation and to
allow tax-exempt investors to participate in a tax-efficient manner. The master fund is where
all capital is pooled and all trading activity occurs. It is funded by multiple feeder vehicles,
each designed for different groups of investors. Typically, one feeder fund is established for
onshore investors and another for offshore investors. For a U.S.-domiciled hedge fund, the
offshore feeder is generally used for non-U.S. investors and for U.S. tax-exempt investors,
such as pension funds.
To operate this structure effectively, hedge funds require a broad range of specialized
support. They need accountants, lawyers, auditors, administrators, and independent
```
valuation professionals to determine the fund’s net asset value (NAV). They also rely on a
```
prime broker to provide lending, counterparty services, trade execution, clearing, and
settlement.
Tax Motivations and Regulatory Considerations
Hedge fund managers have a strong tax incentive to adopt the partnership structure. The
typical 2-and-20 fee structure model consists of a 2% management fee and a 20%
performance fee. Management fees are taxed as ordinary income, while performance fees,
also known as carried interest, may be taxed at the lower capital gains rate if the
underlying assets that generated the gains were held for at least one year. This so-called
carried interest loophole allows performance fees to be taxed as capital gains instead of
ordinary income, which reduces the manager’s tax liability by roughly half.
Before 2008, hedge fund managers were also able to defer taxation on their management
fees. Under Internal Revenue Code Section 457, they could choose to postpone receiving
these fees until a later tax year, effectively deciding that the income was not needed
immediately, and they could manage the timing of their tax obligations. This loophole was
eliminated in 2008.
Attempts have been made to eliminate the carried interest loophole. One effort failed in 2008,
and another failed in 2021 under the Biden Administration. For now, the carried interest
provision remains in place, but it could change in the future. This is an area that hedge fund
managers continue to monitor closely.
MODULE QUIZ 91.2
1. Which of the following regulations requires U.S.-based hedge fund managers to register with the
SEC?
A. Regulation D.
B. Title IV of the Dodd-Frank Act.
C. Investment Advisers Act of 1940.
D. Alternative Investment Fund Managers Directive.
2. Which of the following is most likely an incentive for hedge funds to be structured as limited
partnerships?
A. Management fees are taxed at capital gains rates.
B. Performance fees can be taxed at capital gains rates.
C. All fees paid to the general partner are treated as capital gains.
D. Management fees can be deferred indefinitely for tax purposes.
KEY CONCEPTS
LO 91.a
Hedge fund risks can be viewed at both the portfolio level and the investment level. Portfolio-
level risks include benchmarking issues, survivorship bias, UBTI exposure, and the effects of
leverage on overall performance and volatility. Investment-level risks relate to how a specific
fund is managed and include counterparty risk, conflicts of interest, key person dependence,
strategy failure, style drift, regulatory changes, and correlation spikes during periods of
market stress.
```
Another way to categorize hedge fund risks is through five incremental risks: (1) the use of
```
```
leverage, (2) lighter and more variable regulation compared to mutual funds, (3) the risks
```
```
and unlimited loss potential associated with short selling, (4) limited transparency that makes
```
```
monitoring difficult, and (5) generally higher manager risk tolerance supported by incentive-
```
based compensation. Regulatory requirements differ significantly across jurisdictions,
affecting reporting, leverage limits, short-selling rules, and investor protections.
LO 91.b
Hedge funds can contribute to systemic risk through contagion and through spillover effects
on the banking sector. Contagion can occur when hedge funds facing margin calls are forced
to sell assets, creating downward pressure that may extend to otherwise uncorrelated
markets. Banks are also exposed to hedge funds through revenue risk, credit risk on margin
loans, and counterparty risk from derivatives transactions.
Systemic risk linked to hedge funds can be mitigated by more conservative lending standards
at banks, lower leverage and greater diversification within hedge fund portfolios, and
reasonable regulation. However, overly strict regulation can reduce market liquidity and may
unintentionally heighten systemic risk.
LO 91.c
Hedge funds avoid the limitations of the Investment Company Act of 1940 through two main
```
exemptions:
```
```
Section 3(c)(1), for funds with fewer than 100 investors
```
```
Section 3(c)(7), for funds limited to “qualified purchasers” with more than $5 million in
```
investment assets
Regulation D is also important because hedge funds rely on private placements, restricting
marketing to “accredited investors.” These are defined as individuals with at least $2.5 million
in net worth or $250,000 in annual income, although Rule 506 permits up to 35 non-
accredited investors. The United States and the United Kingdom maintain relatively favorable
regulatory environments for hedge funds, whereas Europe applies more extensive rules,
reporting requirements, and jurisdiction-specific limitations.
In the aftermath of the GFC and the Bernie Madoff scandal, the Dodd-Frank Act expanded
oversight of hedge funds by eliminating the private adviser exemption under the Investment
Advisers Act of 1940, thereby requiring hedge fund managers to register with the SEC. Dodd-
Frank also introduced reporting requirements to both the SEC and the Financial Stability
```
Oversight Council (FSOC), with reporting frequency tied to assets under management (AUM).
```
```
At a high level, regulators focus on three primary concerns: (1) protecting investors from
```
```
fraud, (2) preventing insider trading, and (3) safeguarding financial stability by ensuring that
```
hedge funds do not destabilize markets.
LO 91.d
Hedge funds are typically organized as open-ended limited partnerships, with the hedge fund
manager serving as the general partner and investors serving as the limited partners. This
structure allows new investors to enter and existing investors to redeem shares, subject to
lockups and gates specified in the partnership agreement. Hedge funds often use a master-
feeder structure, in which capital from multiple feeder funds—commonly, one for onshore
investors and another for offshore investors—is pooled into a central master fund where all
trading activity takes place.
The limited partnership structure provides liability protection and is designed to minimize both
regulatory oversight and taxation. Under the common 2-and-20 fee model, management fees
are taxed as ordinary income, while performance fees, known as carried interest, may be
taxed at the lower capital gains rate if the underlying assets have been held for at least one
year. This treatment significantly reduces the manager’s tax burden. Efforts to change this tax
landscape have been limited. The ability to defer management fees for tax purposes was
eliminated in 2008, and attempts in both 2008 and 2021 to close the carried interest loophole
were unsuccessful.

SEC registration if they advised fewer than 15 funds. Regulation D governs private

```
Managers Directive is an EU regulatory framework. (LO 91.c)
```

Section 457, a provision eliminated in 2008. Performance fees, however, may be taxed
at the lower capital gains rate if the underlying assets that generated the gains were
held for at least one year. This favorable tax treatment, known as the carried interest
```
loophole, is a key incentive for hedge funds to use the limited partnership structure. (LO
```
