The following is a review of the Risk Management and Investment Management principles designed to address the learning
```
objectives set forth by GARP®. Cross-reference to GARP assigned reading—International Monetary Fund (IMF).
```
READING 92
THE RISE AND RISKS OF PRIVATE CREDIT
Study Session 14
EXAM FOCUS
This reading examines the emergence of private credit as a significant asset class. Private
credit has seen tremendous growth in recent years beyond its original objective as an
alternative financing source for medium-sized companies. However, there are vulnerabilities
and possible negative impacts from corporate private credit on overall financial stability. For
the exam, be able to describe the main characteristics of private credit. Also, be familiar with
the growth and return profiles of private credit. In addition, understand the main sources of
risk pertaining to private credit, which include credit, liquidity, leverage, valuation,
interconnectedness, and conduct. Finally, be able to provide policy recommendations to help
mitigate those risks.
MODULE 92.1: PRIVATE CREDIT CHARACTERISTICS
LO 92.a: Describe characteristics of private credit, including its typical investors
and borrowers, and compare private credit to other types of loans and fixed-
income instruments.
Private credit is nonbank corporate credit from bilateral agreements, excluding public
securities and commercial banks. For the past three decades, private credit mainly consisted
of asset managers obtaining funding from institutional investors through a closed-end fund
structure and then using those funds to lend to middle-market companies. In short, private
```
credit services a niche market for those mid-sized companies (i.e., annual revenues between
```
```
$100 million and $1 billion) that have borrowing requirements more than what most single
```
commercial banks can reasonably provide but are also not sufficiently large to raise funds
through a public offering.
```
Private credit loans are usually established directly between lenders (asset managers) and
```
```
borrowers. Total costs (e.g., interest payments) are most likely greater than those for
```
```
traditional bank loans; however, private credit allows for much greater customization of
```
```
lending terms (e.g., deferred interest payments) and greater use of covenants to protect
```
```
lenders (e.g., limits on dividend distributions, share buybacks, asset sales). Relationship
```
management with private credit is often better compared to bank loans and public debt,
which provides greater flexibility when working with delinquent loans, thereby increasing
repayments and reducing defaults.
Private Credit Investors and Borrowers
Typical investors in private credit are institutional investors that have a long investment
timeframe. Investors include pension funds, insurance companies, sovereign wealth funds,
and family offices. Such investors would often operate under a closed-end fund structure,
similar to that used in private equity. Other possible structures include business development
```
companies (BDCs) and collateralized loan obligations (CLOs).
```
```
Typical borrowers in private credit are small and mid-sized companies (e.g., information
```
```
technology, healthcare). Borrowing is often done on a floating-rate basis with more collateral
```
requirements and covenants than borrowers would likely encounter with a bank, and more
tailored loan terms suited to the borrower’s business operations. Private credit borrowers are
generally considered a bigger risk than high-yield bond or leveraged-loan issuers. Private
credit borrowers tend to have weak earnings and/or high leverage and, therefore, do not
often qualify for traditional bank financing. However, they do tend to have private equity
backers and strong relationships with their lenders, thereby reducing both liquidity and
solvency risks.
Private Credit vs. Other Types of Loans
Alternatives to private credit include traditional bank loans and the issuance of public debt. In
addition, broadly syndicated loans may be provided by multiple lenders to one borrower if
the amount and/or risk involved with the loan is deemed too high for one lender alone. A
```
leveraged loan is a broadly syndicated loan for a corporate entity that is highly leveraged;
```
therefore, this type of loan will likely be noninvestment grade and have a high interest rate.
LO 92.b: Explain the return profile and growth profile of the private credit asset
class, and compare the historical returns of private credit to those of other asset
classes.
Private Credit Growth
Private credit has seen tremendous growth in the years following the 2007–2009 financial
crisis. During an extended period of low interest rates, private credit became an increasingly
attractive alternative form of financing for borrowers. With stricter regulatory requirements
for financial institutions, insurance companies tended to enter the private credit lending
market as the capital charges and risk requirements were less onerous than those for
commercial banks. If such regulations continue to increase, there could be more movement
toward private credit.
With banks increasingly abandoning loans to riskier mid-sized companies, private credit
appears to have become a popular alternative with astounding growth that has reached
```
approximately $2.1 trillion in loans and undeployed capital (in 2023). As a comparison, this
```
amount is not much less than that of the global high-yield bond market. There is noticeable
```
private credit growth in Europe and Asia; however, it is mainly managed in North America.
```
Although the original focus of private credit was and remains the mid-sized borrower, private
credit lenders have expanded their customer base to include larger corporate entities that
normally would have borrowed via syndicated loans or public bond issuances. The tailored
nature of the borrowings plus the reduced disclosures and costs compared to public bond
issuances are likely reasons for the shift.
In the United States and Asia, private credit has seen annual growth rates of about 20% for
the past five years. Europe has seen similar growth rates, at an average of 17% over the
same period. As of June 2023, in the United States, Europe, and Asia, private credit consists
of about 7%, 1.6%, and 0.2% of total corporate credit, respectively.
In short, the growth in private credit mirrors the growth in private equity. Also, a substantial
amount of private credit involves the borrower being backed by a private equity firm.
Private Credit Returns
```
Regarding returns in recent years (since 2020), private equity has significantly outperformed
```
private credit, while private credit has had similar performance as natural resources and
somewhat better performance than real estate. Private credit has significantly outperformed
the S&P 500 and venture capital.
For about 10 years after the financial crisis, private credit outperformed private equity and
real estate, while private equity and real estate returns were similar. However, there was a
significant change around 2020 when private equity returns jumped dramatically ahead of
private credit. In addition, it is worth noting that private credit underperformed natural
```
resources from about 2008 to 2016; however, since then, their returns have been similar.
```
MODULE QUIZ 92.1
1. To minimize liquidity risk, which of the following investment structures would most likely be
utilized as an intermediary between borrowers and end investors in a private credit structure?
A. Closed-end fund.
B. Open-ended fund.
C. Collateralized loan obligation (CLO).
D. Business development company (BDC).
2. In recent years, which of the following asset classes has outperformed private credit?
A. Real estate.
B. Private equity.
C. Venture capital.
D. Natural resources.
MODULE 92.2: PRIVATE CREDIT RISKS
LO 92.c: Describe and assess the risks and vulnerabilities related to private credit,
and explain how private credit can pose risks to financial stability.
Risks to Financial Stability
There has been a change in credit provided by safe and regulated entities to less transparent
and less regulated private credit firms. Bank loans are heavily regulated, and public bonds are
subject to extensive disclosures. In contrast, private credit has very few regulations, with
nonrated and nontraded underlying loans that do not have standardized terms. The lack of
transparency in private credit makes it challenging to spot significant risks on a timely basis.
Currently, there is insufficient information available to perform a thorough analysis of the
impact of private credit on financial stability. The risks involved with private credit are
widespread and may have major systemic impacts, but such risks are not clearly understood.
Further, the sudden and rapid rise of private credit has not yet seen a serious market
correction and, therefore, private credit has not been properly vetted to ascertain its long-
term stability.
For now, private credit risks seem to be moderate with reasonable matching of asset and
liability durations and relatively low leverage. Liquidity and interconnectedness risks appear to
be low. At the same time, it is necessary to keep things in check given the sudden rise in
private credit in recent years. For example, given the competition for business, there is
always the risk of reduced underwriting standards for private credit, which would increase
expected losses and potentially nullify any risk management tools. With the increased supply
of private credit, there may be reduced risk premiums and less onerous covenants imposed in
an effort by firms to continue to generate revenues from lending.
Going forward, private credit has potential systemic risks given its rapid growth, relative lack
of transparency, and low oversight. Possible systemic risks include the following:
Borrowers tend to be small and highly leveraged. Given that private credit loans are mainly
floating-rate loans, any rate increases would hurt borrowers and possibly lead to a large
```
number of defaults (i.e.,credit risk), thereby reducing lender profitability.
```
Given the relative lack of knowledge and transparency behind the underlying assets,
```
investors with significant holdings of private credit in their investment portfolios (e.g.,
```
```
insurance companies and pension funds) may suffer large losses in a sudden economic
```
downturn.
As private credit funds expand into a wider group of public investors, less liquid private
credit funds may experienceliquidity risks when mass groups of investors suddenly attempt
to withdraw their investments at the same time.
Interconnectedness risks exist due to the labyrinth of leverage with numerous participants
```
(e.g., borrowers, funds, investors), each engaging in leverage transactions. For example,
```
should a given fund be subject to a large capital call in a stressed market, any default or
shortfall could have a domino effect on other participants, thereby causing a reduction of
funding across the entire economy.
Valuation risks exist with private credit funds. Given that this asset class does not yet have
sufficient mechanisms for price discovery, there is minimal or no regulatory monitoring, and
the lack of borrower transparency does not allow for an objective and thorough
determination of exposures. For example, unrealized losses might be deferred as long as
possible by fund managers, and when they eventually need to be realized, it may all occur
at once, which could trigger a wave of defaults.
Financial stability risks occur when various groups within the financial sector all hold private
```
credit (e.g., pension funds, private equity firms, insurance companies). The general lack of
```
information does not allow regulators to properly examine the risks in these groups to
determine any spillover effects.
```
Conduct risks arise due to more (unsophisticated) investors investing in private credit.
```
Unfortunately, the risks of private credit and/or the constraints on withdrawing funds may
not be properly explained to such investors.
Credit Risks
Private credit borrowers are highly sensitive to upward movements in interest rates, given
that virtually all loans are floating rate. In such cases, borrowers are often given temporary
relief when their unpaid interest is simply added to the outstanding debt to be repaid later.
The lender faces default risk if the borrower continues to generate insufficient cash flow to
make interest payments and reduce the debt.
At the same time, private credit losses have historically been lower than those for high-yield
bonds and similar to those for leveraged loans. The backing of private equity firms has
certainly reduced defaults. Additionally, the fact that most private credit loans require
collateral has also reduced the amount of credit losses.
There remain some questions regarding the cyclical nature of private credit. Some evidence
indicates that private credit funding continues to be available during stressed markets when
```
regular funding sources become severely restricted (e.g., March 2020). At the same time, the
```
amount of private credit funding available does have a positive relation to equity market
returns. Also, there is data to support a positive correlation between more onerous bank
lending standards and the reduction in new private credit lending.
Liquidity Risks
To reduce the potential cash flow mismatch between inflows and outflows due to illiquid
```
underlying assets (e.g., private corporate loans), private credit funds impose numerous
```
restrictions such as long-term lockups to reduce cash outflows to investors. However, the
lending activities of private credit funds would increase cash outflows and liquidity risks.
```
Additionally, the increased use of semiliquid evergreen fund structures (whereby the fund
```
```
continually accepts additional capital and makes new investments) also increases liquidity
```
risk.
Given the nature of the underlying assets, private credit CLOs and closed-end funds are not
likely to permit fund withdrawals, which greatly mitigates liquidity risk. On the other hand,
semiliquid funds do allow investor redemptions at specified times only, thereby potentially
attracting additional investors beyond institutional investors, such as individual investors, with
the prospect of some liquidity. Other redemption restrictions may involve maximum amounts
```
that may be withdrawn (i.e., gates) or a deferral of payment following a redemption request
```
```
(i.e., suspension). By limiting the number of redemption periods, the fund is better able to
```
```
control liquidity risk; however, in practice, attracting new individual investors through
```
semiliquid structures may require more frequent redemption periods, thereby increasing
liquidity risk.
Another form of liquidity risk may arise when private credit funds provide lines of credit to
borrowers. Should there be a sudden and unexpected draw on credit lines by many
borrowers at the same time, the private credit funds may require additional cash flow. This
might necessitate funding through investors’ capital calls, thereby shifting the liquidity risk to
those investors.
Leverage Risks
Although private credit funds may seem to utilize minimal leverage compared to banks, there
are numerous levels of leverage within the overall system. Leverage exists beyond just the
fund level, as investors, funds, and borrowers may also use leverage. Due to the lack of
relevant information, it is almost impossible to determine the collective impact of leverage on
overall financial stability.
Private credit investors that employ leverage typically include pension funds and insurance
companies. This leverage subjects them to potential credit downgrades and defaults.
Additionally, when such investors must satisfy margin and collateral calls during stressed
markets, their sheer size may have negative spillover effects on other market participants.
```
Leverage within a fund may be achieved throughspecial purpose vehicles (SPVs) and more
```
involved structures, including collateralized fund obligations, which involve the fund’s limited
partner interests being moved to an SPV to reduce the fund’s cash outflows and allow for
more investors.
Private credit borrowers are often highly leveraged because in addition to borrowing from
private credit funds, they also essentially borrow from the private equity firms that back
them.
Overall, leverage can potentially mask reporting deficiencies, which creates a significant risk
of large losses and negative impacts on other markets during periods when funding is
reduced or withdrawn. Specifically, borrower defaults will lead to fund losses and losses to
investors. Capital call requirements may require substantial funding, which may eventually
lead to the drying up of funding sources in the financial system as all lenders seek to reduce
lending activities at the same time.
Private credit funds utilize leverage in an effort to magnify returns for their investors. As
```
shown in Figure 92.1, this can be accomplished in three main forms: (1) closed-end funds,
```
```
(2) business development companies (BDCs), and (3) middle-market collateralized loan
```
```
obligations (CLOs).
```
Figure 92.1: Leverage in Private Credit Funds
There is very little data on leverage utilization by closed-end funds. However, it is known that
most closed-end private credit funds do not utilize leverage. BDCs appear to have more
diversified debt sources given that they issue unsecured debt. Although BDCs are regulated
and are subject to leverage caps, their leverage has gradually increased over the years.
```
Private credit CLOs are structured in a manner so that investors (e.g., insurance companies,
```
```
pension funds, hedge funds, and banks) may invest in the appropriate tranche(s) to suit their
```
risk tolerance.
```
Private credit funds could be subject to minimum loan-to-value ratios; therefore, during
```
```
stressed markets when collateral (asset) values fall, private credit funds may be subject to
```
onerous collateral calls. As mentioned previously, when private credit funds provide their
borrowers with revolving credit lines, the risk of sudden and unexpected draws on credit lines
by many borrowers at the same time may require substantial additional funds.
Valuation Risks
Private credit valuations have limited usefulness because there are few or no comparable
transactions, infrequent valuations, and no secondary markets to corroborate valuations. In
an economic downturn, stale valuations may ultimately contribute to a mass rush by investors
to withdraw from private credit funds. However, as discussed previously, redemption
limitations may effectively eliminate such liquidity risk.
```
Because private credit assets (e.g., long-term loans) are illiquid, it is difficult to obtain
```
accurate valuations. Such loans are specifically designed for both parties, which makes it
problematic to find comparable transactions. Market estimates and external valuators have
been used, but their assessments may still be too subjective and influenced by certain biases.
Overall, neither the required accounting standards nor the regulations for private credit funds
mandate how valuations should be performed. For the valuations that exist, there is no
consistent methodology that would allow for meaningful comparisons.
One study on private credit valuation concluded that private credit asset prices are less
sensitive to shocks than high-yield bond and leveraged-loan asset prices. However, private
credit assets are subject to additional valuation discounts, which offsets the lower price
sensitivity. The valuation discount is increased during economic downturns. Compared to
public debt, private credit loan value changes are lower and less responsive, which means
that prices and net asset value may be significantly different for several quarters, resulting in
stale valuations.
Stale valuations have the potential for overvaluation, which could allow more astute investors
to withdraw funds prior to any valuation write-downs. However, given the numerous
redemption restrictions, any runoff risks are substantially minimized. Also, private credit
valuations are fraught with estimation error and a lack of reliable data. Therefore, having
more frequent valuations is not necessarily useful and may even lead to greater market
volatility. In fact, having more frequent valuations may cause more focus to be placed on
short-term results, thereby negating the longer-term investment benefits of private credit.
On the other hand, stale valuations have some distinct disadvantages, as infrequent
valuations prevent investors from accurately assessing risks and making effective investment
decisions. Also, stale valuations may suggest higher returns than actual returns for the
current period. This is beneficial for managers trying to attract new investors, but it is
obviously misleading to investors. In addition, stale valuations may overstate asset values
and, therefore, result in overpayment of manager fees. Given the potential for deferred
losses, stale valuations will likely understate losses, which means investors may inaccurately
assess their investment risk.
Interconnectedness Risks
In terms of interconnections, private credit funds are connected with private equity firms,
banks, and institutional investors. A significant number of private credit managers are private
equity managers. Also, many of the firms that borrow in the private credit market are backed
or “guaranteed” by private equity sponsors, which significantly reduces credit risk. The close
connection between private equity and private credit means that weaknesses in one may
affect the other. In addition, the risk of conflicts of interest increases given the numerous
connections among managers.
```
Banks do not appear to have excessive exposure to private credit firm lending (under 1% of
```
```
total assets), and the exposure is further limited in that the lending is done on a secured
```
basis. Pension funds and insurance companies are the primary investors in private credit, but
their exposures are limited to about 3% of assets under management.
Pension funds and insurance companies continue to hold greater amounts of illiquid assets,
and private credit is helping considerably in that regard. At the same time, both types of
institutions make extensive use of derivatives. Because pension funds have significant
```
investments in private equity and private credit (both of which are illiquid), pension funds
```
have greater liquidity risk when faced with potential margin and collateral calls resulting from
their derivatives positions.
Private equity–influenced life insurance companies can provide private equity firms with a
reliable stream of cash flows from premiums collected that can be invested in illiquid assets
established and operated by the private equity firms themselves. These companies have
greater holdings of illiquid assets compared to other life insurance companies, but this does
not translate to stronger solvency capital ratios. In fact, private equity–influenced life
insurance companies have weaker-than-average solvency capital ratios, which could quickly
wipe out their regulatory capital should there be a sudden increase in defaults. The situation
would be made worse by the fact that the underlying illiquid assets, such as asset-backed
securities, are inherently leveraged.
Private credit funds may impose sudden capital calls on investors, which increases liquidity
risk. It is possible that pension funds and insurance companies have uncalled capital
commitments that exceed their current investments in private credit. Private credit assets are
sometimes included in defined-contribution pension plans, for example. Defined-contribution
plans allow beneficiaries to switch between investment options in the short term. However,
given the long-term nature of investing in private credit funds, there is a conflict in terms of
timing that may also create some liquidity risk for the private credit industry.
Underwriting Standards
The sudden and quick rise of private credit has resulted in much greater competition among
banks and public bond markets for larger corporate borrowers. Sometimes banks and other
lenders have worked together with private credit firms to provide larger loans. Unfortunately,
```
this has resulted in diminished underwriting standards (e.g., loans priced too low or not
```
```
commensurate with risk levels) and less strict covenants by private credit lenders. Should an
```
adverse economic event occur with high default rates, banks and other lenders may face
large losses.
Policies for Mitigating Private Credit Risks
LO 92.d: Assess potential policy recommendations that could help mitigate the
risks associated with private credit.
Because the massive increase in private credit could threaten financial stability, it is necessary
to have stronger controls in place to regulate it. Therefore, greater oversight and regulation
are required globally. As a result, the United States and the European Union have specifically
expanded their regulations to deal with the systemic risks, along with jurisdictions in Asia.
The focus of the regulations is on conflicts, conduct, valuation, and disclosures.
The most effective methods for managing private credit risks involve boosting the regulations
and supervisory activities on both a cross-sectoral and cross-border basis. This would require
authorities from different jurisdictions to work together to ensure a full understanding of the
risks and impacts of private credit and its possible collateral effects on other asset classes and
systemic institutions.
```
Policy recommendations should address the following key risks: (1) credit, (2) liquidity, (3)
```
```
leverage, (4) valuation, (5) interconnectedness, and (6) conduct.
```
Credit Risks
For insurance companies and pension funds, no requirements exist to monitor the
creditworthiness of the underlying loan assets. Thus, there is substantial reliance on internal
valuations and external credit rating agencies, which may not provide much confidence.
Funds that have a substantial amount of private credit should focus on more rigorous
```
processes (similar to those employed by banks) to monitor the performance of private credit
```
in structured products and direct lending, for example. Further, underwriting policies and
procedures as well as credit risks require careful scrutiny given the possibility of significant
negative systemic impacts.
Liquidity Risks
To date, clear efforts have been made to address concerns with liquidity mismatches.
However, the existence of open-ended structures as well as generous redemption policies in
many private credit funds do pose some liquidity mismatch risks, especially for funds that
consist of illiquid assets. The potential of many unsophisticated investors attempting to
withdraw funds in reaction to stressed economic conditions is a liquidity risk that needs to be
addressed.
Possible key remedies to limit the outflow of funds include regulators allowing for only closed-
end funds, allowing for more infrequent redemptions, and mandating more notice for
redemptions and more time for settlements. Another remedy could include more onerous
rules to mandate the use of liquidity management techniques and stress testing where
exposure to substantial liquidity mismatches exists. Finally, in an effort to educate investors,
there should be requirements to ensure that disclosures regarding investment risks and
restrictions on redemptions are thorough and understandable.
Leverage Risks
In general, there is not enough information available to properly determine the extent of
leverage employed in private credit. For example, within funds, what is reported may omit or
```
obscure the interconnected and numerous sources of leverage (e.g., SPVs, feeder/master
```
```
funds). Other data omissions and reporting inconsistencies across different countries and
```
sectors prevent a holistic view of oversight over total leverage.
Private credit firms borrow funds from banks to lend to other borrowers. In this regard, it
should be required that private credit firms demonstrate to their banks that they have strong
liquidity management processes in place. Such processes could include stress testing should
funding sources become constrained, stress testing of write-downs on leveraged assets, and
stress testing of large and abrupt uses of credit by the firms’ borrowers.
To overcome data deficiencies with leverage reporting, more reporting requirements could be
implemented both within and outside of borders, together with enhanced cooperation
between regulators. Leverage caps may need to be imposed by regulators when it is
determined that leverage levels are too high and could lead to negative systemic effects.
Valuation Risks
Regulations currently do not consider asset valuation, as the onus tends to be on the
institutional investors themselves to determine their own valuations. The lack of regulations
may result in significant differences in valuations for a given asset, depending on the
valuation method used.
The obvious solution here is greater oversight by regulators of the valuation methods used by
each of the entities. When valuations are significant and/or prone to error, more frequent
external valuations may need to be imposed. Additionally, audits of valuations should be
performed to spot deficiencies in valuation methods and internal controls so that they can be
corrected promptly. When it comes to valuations, the key risk lies with asset write-downs of
illiquid and semiliquid assets. Such losses must be accurately determined and recognized in
the appropriate period.
Interconnectedness Risks
Depending on the differences in the strictness of regulations across countries and sectors,
```
more private credit risk may be taken in countries and sectors with more lax rules (i.e.,
```
```
regulatory arbitrage). In fact, regulatory arbitrage may be contributing to substantial holdings
```
of private credit by insurance companies and pension funds. The many interconnections
between the two institutions and their potentially significant systemic effects should be a
warning sign to regulators.
Similar to leverage risks, the lack of data on interconnected risks may require cross-border
cooperation between regulators to ensure proper oversight, especially when the risks are
more concentrated. On a more local level, regulators within the different sectors also need to
cooperate to determine what additional information is required, learn more about the
interconnected risks, and reduce any contagion risks.
```
Regulatory arbitrage attempts to find the most favorable (least onerous) regulations and may
```
occur both locally and internationally. Therefore, the presence of concentrated risks may
necessitate the harmonization of the risk assessment processes across sectors and borders to
reduce or eliminate regulatory arbitrage.
Conduct Risks
With greater numbers of unsophisticated investors in private credit and the potential for
```
conflicts of interest (where the private credit fund is involved on both sides of the
```
```
transaction), it is necessary to consider conduct risks of the fund in order to avoid conflicts.
```
For example, private transactions may be unfair to one side, as such transactions do not have
a built-in control of market pricing.
Conduct supervisors must determine what conduct risks exist and recommend increased
disclosure when there is a conflict of interest. For example, allowing for more frequent
redemptions by investors could cause managers to manipulate valuations to discourage
redemptions or to make the redemptions conditional upon investing the redeemed funds in
other investments controlled by the manager. Supervisors should consider the investors’ best
```
interests first (e.g., are sales practices fair, and is investment suitability for clients sufficiently
```
```
addressed?). Controls must be put in place to ensure that retail investors adequately
```
understand the credit and liquidity risks as well as the restrictions on redemptions.
MODULE QUIZ 92.2
1. Historically speaking, which of the following asset classes has experienced the highest level of
losses?
A. Leveraged loans.
B. High-yield bonds.
C. Private credit loans.
D. Broadly syndicated loans.
2. In the context of vulnerabilities and potential risks to financial stability in corporate private credit,
which of the following risks is currently of greatest concern?
A. Credit risk.
B. Conduct risk.
C. Liquidity risk.
D. Interconnectedness risk.
3. Which of the following statements regarding policy recommendations on private credit risks is
most accurate?
A. The provision of more frequent redemptions to investors within private credit fund
investments gives rise to potential conduct risks.
B. Given that private credit risks are largely mitigated, authorities need to be careful not to take
an overly regulatory approach that would limit the growth of this emerging asset class.
C. The existence of regulatory arbitrage across borders and sectors has helped in mitigating
excessive concentration of private credit risk in certain jurisdictions and sectors.
D. To address private credit asset valuation risks on a cost-effective basis, regulators should
consider mandating either independent external valuations or strengthening managers’
internal governance mechanisms on valuation procedures.
KEY CONCEPTS
LO 92.a
For the past three decades, private credit mainly consisted of asset managers obtaining
funding from institutional investors through a closed-end fund structure and then using those
funds to lend to middle-market companies. Private credit loans are usually established directly
between lenders and borrowers. Total costs are most likely greater than those for traditional
```
bank loans; however, private credit allows for much greater customization of lending terms
```
and greater use of covenants to protect lenders. Relationship management in private credit is
often better compared to bank loans and public debt.
Typical investors are institutional investors that have a long investment timeframe, such as
pension funds, insurance companies, sovereign wealth funds, and family offices. Typical
borrowers are small and mid-sized companies, borrowing often on a floating-rate basis.
Private credit borrowers would generally be considered a bigger risk than high-yield bond or
leveraged-loan issuers. These borrowers tend to have weak earnings and/or high leverage
and, therefore, often do not qualify for traditional bank financing.
Other types of loans and fixed-income investments include broadly syndicated loans that may
be provided by multiple lenders to one borrower if the amount and/or risk involved with the
loan is deemed too high for one lender alone. A leveraged loan is a broadly syndicated loan
```
for a corporate entity that is highly leveraged; therefore, a leveraged loan will likely be
```
noninvestment grade and have a high interest rate.
LO 92.b
Private credit has seen tremendous growth in the years following the 2007 to 2009 financial
crisis, with banks increasingly abandoning loans to riskier mid-sized companies. Given stricter
regulatory requirements for financial institutions, there could be more movement toward
private credit. Although the original focus of private credit was and remains the mid-sized
borrower, private credit lenders have expanded their customer base to include larger
corporate entities that normally would have borrowed via syndicated loans or public bond
issuances. The tailored nature of the borrowings plus the reduced disclosures and costs
compared to public bond issuances are likely reasons for the shift.
In recent years, private equity has significantly outperformed private credit, while private
credit has had similar performance as natural resources and somewhat better performance
than real estate. Private credit has significantly outperformed the S&P 500 and venture
capital. For about 10 years after the financial crisis, private credit outperformed private equity
and real estate, while private equity and real estate returns were similar during the same
period.
LO 92.c
Private credit has clear potential systemic risks given its rapid growth, relative lack of
transparency, and low oversight. Financial stability risks occur because various groups within
```
the financial sector all hold private credit (e.g., pension funds, private equity firms, insurance
```
```
companies). The general lack of information does not allow regulators to properly examine
```
the risks in the financial sector groups to determine any spillover effects.
The risks and vulnerabilities related to private credit are summarized as follows:
Credit risks. Borrowers tend to be small and highly leveraged. Given that private credit
loans are mainly floating rate, rate increases would hurt borrowers and possibly lead to a
large number of defaults, thereby reducing lender profitability.
Liquidity risks. As private credit funds expand into a wider group of public investors, less
liquid private credit funds may experience liquidity risks when mass groups of investors
suddenly attempt to withdraw their investments at the same time.
Leverage risks. Many layers of leverage exist, but they are masked by reporting
deficiencies. This opens up significant risks of large losses and negative impacts on other
markets during periods when funding is reduced or withdrawn. Specifically, borrower
defaults will lead to fund losses and losses to the investors. Capital call requirements may
require substantial funding, which may eventually lead to the drying up of funding sources
in the financial system as all lenders seek to reduce lending activities at the same time.
Valuation risks. This risk is a concern for private credit funds, as the asset class lacks
adequate mechanisms for price discovery, faces minimal regulatory oversight, and suffers
from limited borrower transparency, making it difficult to objectively and thoroughly assess
exposures. For example, unrealized losses might be deferred as long as possible by fund
managers. When they eventually need to be realized, it may all occur at once, which could
trigger a wave of defaults.
Interconnectedness risks. This risk exists due to the labyrinth of leverage with numerous
```
participants (e.g., borrowers, funds, investors), each engaging in leverage transactions. For
```
example, should a given fund be subject to a large capital call in a stressed market, any
default or shortfall could have a domino effect on other participants, thereby causing a
reduction of funding across the entire economy.
Underwriting risks. The sudden and quick rise of private credit has resulted in much greater
```
competition for larger corporate borrowers; however, it has resulted in diminished
```
underwriting standards and less strict covenants by private credit lenders. Should an
adverse economic event occur with high default rates, many lenders may face large losses.
LO 92.d
The most effective methods for managing private credit risks involve boosting the regulations
and supervisory activities on both a cross-sectoral and cross-border basis. This would require
authorities from different jurisdictions to work together to ensure a full understanding of the
risks and impacts of private credit and its possible collateral effects on other asset classes and
systemic institutions.
Policy recommendations in the context of key risk areas are as follows:
For credit risks, funds that have a substantial amount of private credit should focus on
```
more rigorous processes (similar to those employed by banks) to monitor the performance
```
of private credit in structured products and direct lending.
For liquidity risks, only closed-end funds should be permitted, allowing for more infrequent
redemptions, and mandating more notice for redemptions and more time for settlements.
Also, there should be stricter rules to mandate the use of liquidity management techniques
and stress testing where exposure to substantial liquidity mismatches exists.
For leverage risks, there should be stress testing to consider situations where funding
sources become constrained, stress testing of write-downs on leveraged assets, and stress
testing of large and abrupt uses of credit by the firms’ borrowers. Leverage caps may need
to be imposed by regulators when it is determined that leverage levels are too high and
could lead to negative systemic effects.
For valuation risks, there should be greater oversight by regulators of the valuation
methods used by each of the entities. When valuations are significant and/or prone to
error, more frequent external valuations may need to be imposed. Additionally, audits of
valuations should be performed to spot deficiencies in valuation methods and internal
controls so that they can be corrected promptly.
For interconnectedness risks, the lack of data on these risks may require cross-border
cooperation between regulators to ensure proper oversight, especially when the risks are
more concentrated. On a more local level, regulators within different sectors also need to
cooperate to determine what additional information is required, learn more about the
interconnected risks, and reduce any contagion risks.
For conduct risks, conduct supervisors must determine what risks exist and recommend
increased disclosure when there is a conflict of interest. Supervisors should consider the
investors’ best interests first. Controls must be put in place to ensure that retail investors
sufficiently understand the credit and liquidity risks as well as the restrictions on
redemptions.

The limitations on redemptions under the closed-end structure greatly assist in reducing
```
liquidity risk. The other structures are far less common (e.g., about 5% for CLOs and
```
```
about 14% for BDCs). (LO 92.a)

outperformed private credit, while private credit has had similar performance as natural
resources and somewhat better performance than real estate. Private credit has
```

```

```

```
private credit losses are similar to those for leveraged loans (leveraged loans are below
```

investment grade). The backing of private equity firms has certainly reduced defaults.
```
Additionally, the fact that most private credit loans require collateral has also reduced
the amount of credit losses. In general, syndicated loans have a relatively deep
secondary trading market and are investment grade, so their losses are likely the lowest
```
of all four answer choices. (LO 92.c)

The following is a review of the Risk Management and Investment Management principles designed to address the learning
objectives set forth by GARP®. Cross-reference to GARP assigned reading—Demaria et al., Chapter 3.
READING 93
PRIVATE MARKETS INVESTING
Study Session 14
EXAM FOCUS
This reading focuses on how investors participate in private markets. For the exam,
understand the key motivations for allocating capital to private markets, as well as the
various ways investors can access private market assets, including direct investment, private
```
market funds, funds of funds (FoFs), managed accounts, special purpose vehicles (SPVs), and
```
securitization. Also, be familiar with the advantages and disadvantages of the main
```
performance metrics used in private markets: internal rate of return (IRR), multiple of
```
```
invested capital (MOIC), and public market equivalent (PME). In addition, be able to explain
```
the major risks associated with private market investments and the methods used to measure
those risks. Finally, understand how co-investing works and its associated benefits and
drawbacks, as well as the benefits and challenges of accessing private markets through
intermediated vehicles.
MODULE 93.1: MOTIVATIONS AND ACCESS TO PRIVATE
MARKETS
Private Market Investment Motivations
LO 93.a: Explain the motivations for investors to invest in private markets.
Private markets help private companies raise capital through a wide range of securities,
including equity, convertible debt, and traditional debt. Equity investments in private firms are
```
referred to as private equity (PE), debt investments are called private debt (PD), and
```
```
investments in privately held real assets are known as private real asset (PRA)
```
investments. A private equity investment may eventually become publicly traded through a
listing on public markets. In addition, some publicly traded firms with limited trading volume
seek capital through private transactions known as private placements. These transactions
```
are referred to as private investments in public equities (PIPEs).
```
Investors are motivated to pursue private investment vehicles for various reasons. These
motivations can be grouped into the following six categories:
1.Performance and diversification. Private market investments have the potential to generate
higher returns than public market assets. They may also provide diversification benefits by
hedging traditional asset performance during periods of market uncertainty.
2.Control and value creation. Private investors can monitor, guide, and influence company
performance. This level of control is generally not available in public markets unless the
investor holds a very large ownership stake. Private investors can also contribute capital,
industry connections, and operational expertise, all of which can support and enhance the
value creation process within a private company.
3.Access to a broader investment universe. There are far more private firms than public
firms, which creates opportunities for unique diversification. With more than 300 million
private companies worldwide compared to roughly 52,000 listed firms, private markets
offer exposure to a wider range of industries and business models. This results in a more
diverse investment universe than what is available in public markets alone. Private
investors can also invest in companies at all stages of their lifecycle.
4.Potential for absolute returns. Many investors pursue private investments in search of
absolute returns that are less correlated with public market performance.
5.Wider geographic reach. Private investments offer exposure to regions where public
markets are underdeveloped or nonexistent. This expands the range of diversification
opportunities and helps reduce home country bias.
6.Specialized investment structures. Some private investors seek specialized risk exposures.
Structures such as private funds of funds and co-investments can provide unique
opportunities that may not be available through traditional investment channels.
Private Market Investment Structures
LO 93.b: Describe different investment structures that can be used to invest in
private assets and compare their characteristics.
An investment structure determines how capital is pooled, managed, and governed. It also
defines the rights and obligations of managers and investors. The main investment structures
used in private markets are presented in this section and compared in Figure 93.1 across
several key metrics. These investment structures are described as follows:
1. Direct investment. In a direct investment, an investor acquires an ownership interest in a
private company or asset. This structure offers a high degree of control but requires
significant expertise and active involvement. It also carries high concentration risk and
provides limited diversification unless many positions are held. Successful direct investing
typically requires strong networks to source opportunities, as well as an understanding that
private market investing often depends on local presence and relationships. Information
asymmetry between investors and private firms can also be a challenge. Entrepreneurs,
family offices, and buyout investors frequently use this approach. Crowdfunding is another
form of direct investment. Several strategies, such as leveraged buyouts and distressed
debt, are generally not accessible through this format.
2. Private funds. Funds are the most common type of institutional investment structure.
```
Investor capital is pooled, and specialized managers (i.e., general partners) make
```
investment and oversight decisions. This approach provides diversification benefits but
limits the level of control available to individual investors. Institutional and large private
investors commonly use this structure, and pension funds also participate. In some
jurisdictions, retail investors may be permitted to invest in certain types of private funds.
The most common fund structure is a closed-end limited partnership.
3. Private funds of funds (FoFs). In this structure, investors allocate capital to a fund that
itself invests in a portfolio of private market funds. This approach enhances diversification
compared with investing in a single fund, but it introduces an additional layer of
management fees. It is often used by private investors with limited experience as an entry
point into private markets, and it appeals to investors who place a high priority on
diversification. Among the private market options, this structure offers the closest
approximation to a passive investment approach.
4. Managed accounts. A managed account is a separate account tailored to an investor’s
```
specific mandate. Mandates may be discretionary (where the manager makes all
```
```
investment decisions) or advisory (where the investor retains veto authority). Discretionary
```
```
mandates typically involve carried interest (i.e., performance-based fees), while advisory
```
mandates often do not. Managed accounts offer customization and transparency for large
investors, but they rely on negotiation with the manager and usually require a substantial
capital commitment. Institutions seeking tailored solutions are the most likely users of this
structure.
5. Special purpose vehicles (SPVs). An SPV is an entity created to hold a specific asset or
group of assets. SPVs may be structured with either equity or debt, although they are often
used to facilitate investment in PRAs. More sophisticated investors seeking targeted
exposure to a particular deal or asset frequently consider this structure.
6. Securitization. In a securitization structure, assets (typically debt) are pooled together
and divided into tranches with different risk and return characteristics. Although these
securities can trade publicly, this structure introduces additional complexity and reduces
transparency. Investors who want exposure to a specific risk profile or who want to transfer
certain risks are the most likely users of this approach.
Figure 93.1: Characteristics of Private Market Investment Structures
```
Equity investments in private markets (i.e., private equity) can be structured as either
```
common shares or preferred shares. Preferred shares can be negotiated to include specific
rights and protections. As a result, not all preferred shares offer the same features or level of
influence.
```
Debt investments in private markets (i.e., private debt) can be structured as either
```
convertible or nonconvertible, with varying degrees of seniority. Convertible debt provides
downside protection through its debt component and upside potential through the option to
convert into equity. Debt arrangements can be organized with senior, subordinate, or junior
rights, each offering different levels of priority over other creditors.
MODULE QUIZ 93.1
1. Which of the following statements best explains why investors may prefer private investment
vehicles over public markets? Private investment vehicles:
A. require less expertise from investors.
B. harness the power of home country bias.
C. guarantee higher returns than in public markets.
D. offer investors greater control over the value creation process.
2. Which of the following private market investment structures requires the highest level of investor
expertise and involvement, offers high control, but results in concentrated risk and low
diversification unless many positions are held?
A. Private funds.
B. Direct investment.
C. Managed accounts.
D. Private funds of funds.
MODULE 93.2: PERFORMANCE AND RISK MEASUREMENT IN
PRIVATE MARKETS
Private Equity Performance Measurement
LO 93.c: Apply and compare different methods to measure the performance of
private equity investments and describe their advantages and disadvantages.
The three primary metrics for measuring private equity performance are the internal rate of
```
return (IRR), the multiple of invested capital (MOIC), and the public market equivalent (PME).
```
This section summarizes each metric and highlights their key advantages and disadvantages.
```
Internal Rate of Return (IRR)
```
The IRR is the most commonly used private equity performance metric. It reflects the
annualized return on invested capital and incorporates the timing of cash flows. It is well
suited to multi-year investment projects, and to comparing competing investments. However,
early cash flows can artificially inflate IRR, which may encourage fund managers to favor
short-term gains over higher long-term returns. This timing issue can be mitigated by using a
```
time-zero IRR (a.k.a. modified IRR [MIRR]). Because IRR does not incorporate risk, it can
```
sometimes incentivize managers to pursue riskier deals. In addition, managers may delay
capital calls by drawing on credit lines, which shortens holding periods and can artificially
boost reported IRRs.
IRR advantages:
Intuitive and easy to interpret
Incorporates time value of money
Widely accepted and used by industry participants
IRR disadvantages:
```
Measures only deployed capital (not committed capital)
```
Not adjusted for risk
Highly sensitive to timing of cash flows, creating potential for manipulation
Assumes reinvestment at the IRR
Can overstate performance when credit lines are used to accelerate distributions
Does not incorporate explicit benchmarks or broader market context
```
Multiple of Invested Capital (MOIC)
```
The MOIC measures how much value an investment has generated relative to the capital
invested. It is calculated by dividing the sum of total distributions and the current value of
```
any remaining assets by the total capital invested. An MOIC of 1.0 (i.e., 1x) indicates
```
breakeven, meaning the investor has recovered exactly the amount of capital originally
invested. MOIC includes several related submetrics:
```
Total value to paid-in (TVPI). TVPI is the primary MOIC measure. It is calculated as the
```
```
sum of distributions and residual value (i.e., the value of remaining assets) divided by
```
capital called. TVPI can also be expressed as the sum of DPI and RVPI. Because it includes
residual value, TVPI can be manipulated if fund managers inflate asset valuations.
```
Distributed to paid-in (DPI). DPI is calculated as distributions divided by capital called.
```
Because it relies entirely on realized cash flows, it is not subject to valuation manipulation
and is often the preferred measure when sufficient realizations have occurred, meaning the
fund has generated enough cash exits. A limitation of DPI is that realized cash flows may
reflect economic conditions that differ from those affecting the remaining unrealized assets.
```
Residual value to paid-in (RVPI). RVPI is calculated as residual value divided by capital
```
called. It naturally increases as a fund invests in assets that appreciate in value. Like TVPI,
RVPI can be manipulated if residual values are overstated.
MOIC advantages:
Focuses on absolute performance
Independent of dollar size, making comparisons across funds straightforward
Less susceptible to manipulation than the IRR
```
Cash-based submetrics (such as DPI) are robust
```
MOIC disadvantages:
```
Measures only deployed capital (not committed capital)
```
Not adjusted for risk
Ignores time value of money, including holding periods
Cannot be annualized
Does not incorporate explicit benchmarks or broader market context
```
Public Market Equivalent (PME)
```
The PME compares the performance of private equity investments with public market
benchmarks by discounting all fund cash flows using the returns of an index such as the S&P
500. The sum of the discounted distributions is divided by the sum of the discounted capital
calls. A PME greater than 1.0 indicates outperformance relative to the benchmark. PME works
best with fully realized funds, and accurate implementation requires matching each
distribution with its associated capital call to reflect the correct holding period. Cambridge
```
Associates developed a modified PME (mPME) to approximate this calculation. The mPME
```
provides a more robust measure of manager value creation and is less prone to manipulation.
PME advantages:
Incorporates explicit benchmarking to provide market context
Helps neutralize the impact of market cycles on performance evaluation
Measures manager value creation in a way that is less prone to manipulation
PME disadvantages:
```
Measures only deployed capital (not committed capital)
```
Sensitive to the choice of benchmark
Suitable benchmarks may not always exist
Most reliable when applied to fully realized funds
Common Limitation of PE Performance Metrics
A common disadvantage of all three performance metrics is that they measure only deployed
capital, not committed capital. This limitation ignores the cash drag from uncalled capital. It
also creates challenges for investors who commit more capital than they ultimately intend to
deploy, given the trend of funds not calling the full committed amount. For example, an
investor may commit 110% of their desired allocation, expecting that only about 60% will
actually be called. Although this practice helps investors reach their target exposure, it also
creates the risk of overallocation—or even default—if the full commitment is unexpectedly
called.
Private Market Risks
LO 93.d: Explain how different risks can arise for investors in private markets and
assess how these risks can be measured.
Investors in private markets face both traditional financial risks and risks specific to private
assets. These include risks related to illiquidity, information asymmetry, valuation uncertainty,
agency issues, operational challenges, selection and reporting risks, and the timing of cash
flows.
Major Risk Categories
The major risks faced by private market investors can be grouped into the following broad
categories, each arising from different characteristics of private assets:
Systematic risk. Although private assets typically exhibit low correlations with public
market indices, they remain exposed to broad macroeconomic risks such as economic
downturns, shifts in inflation, and changes in interest rate policy. Interest rate risk is
particularly relevant for strategies that rely heavily on leverage. Because private assets are
```
illiquid, some market risks (e.g., foreign exchange risk) may be more difficult to hedge than
```
in public markets. Long holding periods for private assets further complicate hedging.
Systematic risks are commonly monitored through scenario analysis, stress testing, and by
assessing performance relative to public market equivalents.
Illiquidity risk. Private market investments are not traded on public exchanges and are
typically locked up for long periods with limited opportunities to exit. Measuring illiquidity
risk involves assessing the duration of the investment, the time until expected liquidity
```
(which relates to funding liquidity risk), and the liquidity implications of opportunity costs.
```
Credit risk. Credit risk is most relevant for private debt investments and has two
components. The first isrecovery risk, which refers to the possibility that capital may be
```
lost if a borrower defaults; this risk is particularly important for distressed investors,
```
although private debt managers have generally demonstrated a strong ability to limit
recovery losses. The second component iscredit event risk, where negative developments
in publicly traded credit markets may spill over into private debt assets.
Settlement risk. Settlement risk is always present when transacting with counterparties.
However, private market investors typically conduct extensive due diligence, which helps
reduce this risk relative to what is often observed in public market transactions.
Information asymmetry. Private investments are not subject to the same level of
regulation as public firms, which limits the amount and quality of available information. As
a result, due diligence becomes especially important for investors. The frequency and rigor
of reporting and auditing help investors assess and monitor this risk.
Valuation risk. Valuation risk arises in private markets because transactions occur
infrequently and comparable data is often limited. Stale pricing and valuation subjectivity
are both important concerns. One issue related to stale pricing is thedenominator effect,
which occurs when an outdated private asset valuation is compared with the value of a
highly liquid public asset, leading to distorted comparisons. Valuation risk can be assessed
through third-party appraisals, discounted cash flow analysis, the use of comparables when
available, and adherence to the International Private Equity and Venture Capital Valuation
```
(IPEV) Guidelines.
```
```
Strategic asset allocation (SAA) risk. SAA risk arises when a long-term allocation
```
deviates from a market-neutral benchmark. This risk involves more than simply setting
target weights, because constraints related to investability, access, and the unique
characteristics of private assets can limit an investor’s ability to maintain the desired
allocation. Because private asset values adjust slowly compared with public market prices,
some differences in weighting may be artificial, which can lead to unnecessary rebalancing.
Significant deviations in SAA from a market-neutral benchmark may also create challenges
for private fund managers during fundraising.
```
Tactical asset allocation (TAA) risk. TAA risk is associated with market timing.
```
Opportunistic shifts in exposure can be beneficial, but they can also lead to mistakes if
forecasts are inaccurate. In private markets, this risk is more challenging because a capital
commitment made today may not be deployed for several years. Co-investments and
secondary market transactions can help investors make more timely adjustments to their
private market exposures. Investors may also increase allocations to private assets after
periods of strong performance, which reflects procyclical behavior. Time diversification,
achieved by allocating across multiple vintage years over a 5- to 7-year period, can help
mitigate this risk.
Agency risk. Delegating control to fund managers introduces agency risk. This risk can be
assessed by examining fee structures, including carried interest, as well as potential
conflicts of interest and the strength of the fund’s governance framework.
Operational risk. Operational risk is particularly relevant for private debt and PRA
investors. It relates to the risk of default, recovery challenges, and operational failures at
the company or asset level. This risk can be assessed using historical default and loss
statistics, covenant compliance, and thorough operational due diligence.
Selection risk. Selection risk reflects the possibility of choosing the wrong manager or
deal. This risk is not symmetrical, because the performance of top managers can differ
substantially from that of the bottom performers. The wide dispersion between the top 5%
and bottom 5% of managers also creates challenges for Monte Carlo simulations, which
typically rely on the assumption of normally distributed returns.
Reporting risk. Reporting risk in private markets refers to the possibility that performance
metrics and valuations disclosed by fund managers do not fully reflect economic reality,
either because of intentional manipulation or because of structural limitations in
measurement and disclosure. This risk is driven by limited transparency, infrequent
reporting, and less oversight relative to public markets. The subjectivity involved in
```
determining a fund’s net asset value (NAV) also contributes to reporting risk. DPI is less
```
exposed to this risk than TVPI or RVPI because it is based on realized cash flows.
Measuring Private Market Risks
Private market risks can be assessed and monitored using the following qualitative and
quantitative tools:
Performance dispersion. One way to assess macro risk is to monitor the dispersion between
the highest pooled average TVPI and the lowest. This measure can be affected by outliers,
so using a multiyear rolling average may help refine the spread. A large spread suggests
that macroeconomic conditions had a greater influence on returns. Performance dispersion
can also be used to monitor selection risk. Comparing a fund’s TVPI with the pooled
average for its category is one method of evaluating selection risk.
Compare DPI to TVPI. One way to assess reporting risk is to compare DPI, which is based
entirely on realized cash flows, with TVPI, which depends on manager-determined NAV
estimates. A substantial gap between the two may indicate potential reporting risk.
Review portfolio weights. SAA risk can be assessed by comparing an investor’s portfolio
weights with those in a target allocation or a market-neutral portfolio. In this context, a
market-neutral portfolio reflects the geographic and strategy mix of the relevant private
market allocation.
```
Value at risk (VaR) and stress testing. These tools can be used to model worst-case
```
outcomes for strategy-specific risks, including macroeconomic shocks and periods of
reduced liquidity.
Vintage year analysis. Comparing the returns of different vintage years helps evaluate the
impact of market timing on performance and assess how risks vary across economic cycles.
Due diligence reviews. A qualitative review can be used to confirm whether the risks that
materialized align with those expected at the time of investment. Any gaps identified can
help strengthen future due diligence processes.
Default and loss rates. For private debt investments, tracking historical default rates and
loss statistics provides a useful benchmark for assessing recovery risk.

C. Public market equivalent (PME).
D. Multiple of invested capital (MOIC).
2. Which of the following private market performance metrics is most useful for assessing absolute
performance without regard to holding periods?
A. Distributed to paid-in (DPI).
B. Internal rate of return (IRR).
C. Public market equivalent (PME).
D. Multiple of invested capital (MOIC).
3. Which of the following metrics best helps investors monitor the impact of macro risks and
selection risk in private market performance?

MODULE 93.3: PRIVATE MARKET CO-INVESTING AND
INTERMEDIATED VEHICLES
Private Market Co-Investing
LO 93.e: Describe the practice of co-investing in private markets and assess its
advantages and disadvantages.
Co-investing involves a private investor directly investing in a specific deal alongside a
private market fund, typically by invitation from a fund manager. This structure generally
offers lower fees compared with investing through the fund itself. Fund managers may extend
co-investment opportunities when they need additional capital to complete a deal that
exceeds their usual investment limits. Access is usually restricted to large, established
investors with whom the manager has a strong relationship. These opportunities often arise
on short notice, requiring accelerated due diligence and decision-making. Some managers
also offer dedicated co-investment vehicles, which formalize access to co-investment deals.
Co-investing advantages:
Lower fees. Co-investors typically pay reduced, or sometimes no, management fees or
carried interest compared with primary fund investors. They still share in transaction and
due diligence costs.
Greater control. Co-investing allows investors to target specific deals, enabling more
precise shaping of exposures and desired portfolio weights.
Potential for higher net returns. Because fees are lower, co-investments often produce
higher after-fee returns than investments made through the primary fund.
Learning and access. Co-investing provides valuable experience, strengthens relationships
with general partners, and offers exposure to opportunities that may not be available
through traditional fund commitments.
Co-investing disadvantages:
Adverse selection. Co-investors may be offered opportunities that are less attractive or
outside the fund manager’s core expertise. Co-investment deal flow can also be procyclical,
meaning opportunities often appear when valuations are already high.
Speed and complexity. Co-investments frequently require accelerated due diligence and
rapid decision-making, which may force investors to act with less preparation than they
would for primary fund commitments.
Resource and expertise requirements. Effective co-investing requires significant internal
resources, specialized expertise, and the ability to evaluate deals quickly. Investors lacking
these capabilities often underperform.
Concentration and diversification risk. Co-investments are typically larger, more
concentrated positions than standard fund exposures. As a result, portfolios may become
less diversified and more sensitive to deal-specific risks. Many co-investments involve
leveraged buyouts or infrastructure projects, increasing the risk of unintended
concentration.
Legal and governance risks. Co-investors are usually passive participants with limited
influence over deal structure, management decisions, or exit timing. They often accept
fewer legal protections compared with limited partners investing through the primary fund.
Intermediated Private Market Investment
LO 93.f: Explain benefits and challenges of investing in private markets through
an intermediated investment vehicle such as a private equity or private debt fund.
Investing in private markets through an intermediated vehicle, such as a private equity or
private debt fund, involves numerous benefits as well as several challenges.
Benefits of using intermediated vehicles in private markets include the following:
Expertise. Fund managers bring expertise in sourcing, due diligence, structuring,
monitoring, and exiting investments. These capabilities are valuable given the complexity
of private markets and can reduce operational risk for investors who lack specialized skills.
Risk reduction. Diversification is a key benefit of intermediation. Pooling capital enables
access to a wider range of deals, sectors, and geographies. Professional management may
also lead to lower loss ratios, further reducing risk.
Deal flow access. Managers often have access to attractive investment opportunities that
are not available to most individual investors. This benefit becomes especially important as
private markets grow more competitive and specialized.
Cost and resource sharing. Pooling assets allows investors to share the substantial costs
associated with sourcing, analysis, and ongoing oversight. These costs might otherwise
prevent individual investors from participating directly in private markets.
Challenges of using intermediated vehicles in private markets include the following:
High fees. Accessing professional management teams is expensive. Investors typically pay
both management fees and carried interest, which creates a natural performance hurdle.
Principal-agent risk. The goals of the general partner may not always align with those of
the limited partners. This misalignment can create governance and performance concerns,
including the potential for abuse or misconduct.
Lack of transparency. Intermediation introduces layers between investors and the
underlying assets, limiting investors’ ability to monitor or influence investments.
Information asymmetry can arise from limited disclosure, infrequent valuations, proprietary
management practices, and uncertainty regarding the timing of cash flows.
Blind pool risk. Many private market funds, including private equity and private debt funds,
are structured as blind pools in which investors commit capital before knowing exactly
which assets will be acquired. Although strategy guidelines are provided, the fund manager
ultimately decides how capital is deployed, which may result in exposures an investor
would not have chosen directly.
Illiquidity risk. Capital is committed for long periods, with no redemption rights and limited
secondary market options. If liquidity is needed, investors may have to accept significant
discounts to NAV.
Due diligence demands. Investors must conduct thorough due diligence on fund strategies,
manager track records, governance structures, and operational processes to reduce the
risk of underperformance or misconduct.
Potential conflicts of interest. Conflicts may arise in several forms. For example, a fund that
invests in both equity and debt of the same company may face competing incentives
between debtholders and equity holders. Conflicts can also occur in the allocation of co-
investment opportunities.

2. Which of the following is a key benefit of investing in private markets through intermediated
vehicles such as private equity or private debt funds?
A. Access to expertise.
B. More access to liquidity.
C. Elimination of management fees.
D. Increased transparency and control.
3. Which of the following is a key challenge of investing in private markets through intermediated
