The following is a review of the Risk Management and Investment Management principles designed to address the learning
objectives set forth by GARP®. Cross-reference to GARP assigned reading—Gregoriou and Lhabitant.
READING 96
```
MADOFF: A RIOT OF RED FLAGS
```
Study Session 14
EXAM FOCUS
Bernard Madoff operated a securities advisory firm that managed approximately $17 billion in
assets. In December 2008, he admitted to family members that the business was “a giant
Ponzi scheme” and was arrested the following day. For the exam, be familiar with the
```
operations of Bernard L. Madoff Investment Securities, LLC (BMIS), including its history,
```
reported investment strategy, and claimed performance results. Also, be able to identify the
warning signs that should have alerted investors and regulators to the fraud. Understanding
these operational and investment red flags is essential for recognizing how insufficient due
diligence can allow fraud to persist. Many investors focused on the appearance of consistent
returns while overlooking these indicators, leading to billions of dollars in losses for
individuals, pension funds, hedge funds, banks, and charitable organizations.
MODULE 96.1: BERNIE MADOFF’S HEDGE FUND: BACKGROUND
AND OPERATIONS
LO 96.a: Explain the history of Bernie Madoff’s hedge fund and describe the fund’s
reported strategy and its reported performance.
In December 2008, the world witnessed the collapse of one of the largest Ponzi schemes in
history. Bernard Madoff had operated a seemingly successful wealth management business
for more than 17 years. The investment approach used by Bernard L. Madoff Investment
```
Securities, LLC (BMIS) was widely regarded as one of the most effective in the industry.
```
Madoff described his method as a “split-strike conversion strategy,” which allegedly produced
steady annual returns of 8%–12%. Investing with Madoff was considered an exclusive
opportunity, often limited to wealthy, well-connected, and socially prominent individuals.
When BMIS collapsed, the losses extended far beyond individual investors, impacting
charitable organizations, pension funds, hedge funds, funds of hedge funds, banks, and
celebrities.
In hindsight, it seems almost inconceivable that such a massive fraud could have gone
undetected for so many years. From regulators to investors, few asked the right questions—
and due diligence, a fundamental practice in the hedge fund industry, was largely absent.
Madoff’s reputation and influence in the financial community were so great that many
hesitated to question or criticize him, fearing damage to their own careers or relationships.
Subsequent investigations revealed the full extent of the deception, leading to Madoff’s
arrest, conviction, and 150-year prison sentence. Total investor losses were estimated at up
to $50 billion, making it one of the largest financial frauds in history.
BMIS Background and Growth
Founded in 1960, BMIS began as a brokerage business that quoted bid and ask prices and
```
executed over-the-counter (OTC) trades for clients. The firm was an early adopter of
```
technology and quickly focused on electronic trading. Madoff later identified a New York Stock
```
Exchange (NYSE) rule, known as Rule 390, which allowed trading of NYSE-listed stocks
```
away from the exchange floor and its specialists. Because NYSE members were prohibited
from making markets in exchange-listed stocks, this discovery gave Madoff a competitive
advantage in expanding his firm’s trading operations.
As a result of discovering Rule 390, Madoff became a member of the struggling Cincinnati
```
Stock Exchange (CSE) and invested in upgrading its computer systems. His efforts
```
transformed the CSE into the first fully electronic trading exchange in the United States.
Madoff’s firm profited from the bid–ask spread on trades, which at the time was quoted in
```
fractions (such as one-eighth or one-fourth) rather than in pennies. BMIS began making
```
markets in NYSE-listed stocks and executed orders for firms outside the NYSE. To attract
business, BMISpaid brokers $0.01 per share to route their orders through the firm, a practice
that quickly helped it capture a significant portion of trading volume, exceeding 5% by 1989
and approximately 10% by 2008. However, this “payment for order flow” model was
controversial within the industry.
Madoff was also a key figure in the development of the NASDAQ stock market. He served as
a member of NASDAQ’s board of governors and ultimately became chairman of its board of
directors. His leadership in promoting electronic trading and market modernization earned
him significant respect on Wall Street. This reputation as an industry innovator and trusted
market participant later lent credibility to his investment advisory business and helped attract
investors who viewed Madoff as a financial pioneer.
By the late 1980s, shrinking margins and growing competition in the brokerage business
prompted Madoff to establish a separate investment advisory firm located in the same
building as his brokerage operation. The investment advisory business operated alongside the
brokerage arm, blurring the distinction between the two and creating potential conflicts of
interest that complicated regulatory oversight. Although Madoff was often described as a
hedge fund manager, this was not the case. His firm managed several discretionary accounts,
including 23 in 2008 according to its Form ADV, with approximately $17 billion in assets under
```
management (AUM) and $700 million in equity capital. Most of these accounts were feeder
```
funds that opened brokerage accounts with BMIS and granted the firm full trading authority.
The feeder funds, marketed to investors worldwide, served as intermediaries between BMIS
```
and the ultimate investors. As a result, most investors were not direct clients of BMIS; rather,
```
they were clients of the feeder funds themselves.
It is important to recognize that the final investors werenot direct customers of BMIS—they
were clients of the various feeder funds. This distinction is central to understanding the
Madoff story. The individuals and institutions providing capital to Madoff’s firm could conduct
due diligence only on the feeder funds in which they invested, not on BMIS itself. Madoff did
not permit investors to visit his offices, ask questions, or interact with him directly. As a
result, investors had to rely on the feeder funds to perform critical due diligence, since
investors were unable to conduct that assessment themselves.
Reported Investment Strategy and Performance
Madoff referred to his proprietary investment approach as a split-strike conversion
strategy—essentially, a combination of aprotective put and acovered call designed to limit
both upside and downside exposure. The strategy was relatively straightforward and is
commonly known among option traders as acollar orbull spread. It is sometimes described
as a vacation trade because, once established, the position typically required little attention
until the options neared expiration.
According to Madoff, the firm actively traded a portfolio of stocks and options designed to
closely mirror the S&P 100 Index. The strategy consisted of the following steps:
Step 1: Take a long position in a portfolio of stocks that is highly correlated with the S&P 100.
```
Step 2: Write (sell) out-of-the-money call options on the S&P 100 with a notional value approximately equal to
```
the equity portfolio.
Step 3: Buy out-of-the-money put options on the S&P 100 with a notional value approximately equal to the
equity portfolio.
As stock prices rise, gains in the equity portfolio are eventually offset by the increasing
liability of the short call position, creating a ceiling on potential returns. Conversely, the long
put position establishes a floor, because declines in the equity portfolio are offset by gains in
the put options once prices fall below a certain point. Collars are often used to take
advantage of option skew, a condition in which call option premiums are higher than
comparable put option premiums, which allows investors to reduce the overall cost of
hedging.
Madoff claimed that the portfolio was held in cash most of the time and that the split-strike
conversion strategy was implemented over short time horizons, typically less than 30 days.
He promised steady annual returns between 8% and 12% through this approach. The feeder
funds reporting these results did, in fact, show remarkably consistent positive performance
over a 17-year period. One of the most significant red flags, however, was that Madoff’s
investment advisory business never recorded a single down year. More than 95% of all
months showed positive returns throughout its history. Although the returns were comparable
to those of the S&P 100, the volatility in Madoff’s reported results was dramatically lower.
Fund Collapse and Confession
Surprisingly, Madoff was not discovered through an investigation. Instead, he confessed to
family members that his investment advisory business was “a giant Ponzi scheme.” This
confession occurred on December 10, 2008. That evening, his sons reported him to U.S.
authorities, and he was arrested the following day. Madoff was charged with securities fraud,
his family’s assets were frozen, and a receiver was appointed to oversee BMIS. The outrage
and devastation felt by investors who lost their life savings left a lasting mark on the financial
industry.
Note that Madoff did not confess out of remorse or honesty. By late 2008, financial markets
were in turmoil, and investors were panicking. Redemption requests were flooding in at a
pace that BMIS could no longer meet. Madoff tried to raise additional capital but was unable
to do so quickly enough. At that point, he admitted to his sons, wife, and brother that it was
“all a big lie.” Had the market downturn not intensified, the scheme might have continued for
```
some time. The situation was similar to the subprime mortgage crisis; it could persist only
```
while asset prices were rising, but once prices began to fall, the entire structure collapsed.
MODULE QUIZ 96.1
1. Bernard L. Madoff Investment Securities, LLC (BMIS) began operations in 1960:
A. as a brokerage business.
B. as a derivatives clearinghouse.
C. as an investment advisory business.
D. conducting electronic trading on the Cincinnati Stock Exchange (CSE).
2. Which of the following statements about Madoff’s split-strike conversion strategy is incorrect?
A. Part of the strategy involved selling an out-of-the-money call option.
B. A floor value was created by purchasing an out-of-the-money put option.
C. A long position in a portfolio of stocks highly correlated with the S&P 100 was required.
D. Gains on the portfolio were supplemented by gains on the long, out-of-the-money call option
position.
3. When comparing the reported returns of Madoff’s feeder funds with the S&P 100, those returns
```
were:
```
A. significantly higher than the S&P 100, and more volatile.
B. significantly higher than the S&P 100, and much less volatile.
C. approximately the same as the S&P 100, but with much lower volatility.
D. significantly lower than the S&P 100, but as stable as short-term Treasury bills.
MODULE 96.2: RED FLAGS AND FRAUD RISK INDICATORS
LO 96.b: Identify characteristics of Bernie Madoff’s hedge fund that can serve as
warning signs or potential “red flags” and explain how they can indicate potential
fraud risk.
Operational Red Flags
There were several operational warning signs that should have alerted investors and
regulators to the problems at BMIS. These included the following:
Lack of independent service providers. BMIS provided nearly all investment services
internally. In a typical fund, separate entities handle functions such as trade execution,
custody, fund administration, and investment management. BMIS performed all of these
roles in-house, which made it far easier to manipulate performance results and potentially
misappropriate assets.
Conflict of interest. BMIS acted as both broker-dealer and market maker for some of its
own strategies. This dual role created opportunities for self-dealing and removed an
important layer of independent oversight.
Questionable auditing firm. Despite managing $17 billion in assets, BMIS used a three-
person accounting firm, Friehling & Horowitz, operating out of a 550-square-foot office.
```
Jerome Horowitz, one partner, was in his late 70s and largely inactive; David Friehling was
```
the only practicing accountant. The firm had even declared to the American Institute of
Certified Public Accountants that it had no auditing clients. By contrast, Madoff’s feeder
funds were typically audited by major firms such as KPMG, which lent an illusion of
credibility. However, these large firms relied on Friehling & Horowitz’s work without
verifying its legitimacy.
Family members in key roles. Madoff’s family occupied many senior positions within the
firm, including his brother, sons, niece, and nephew. This raised serious concerns about
independence and oversight. Regulatory filings also reported that only one to five
individuals managed the investment advisory business—an implausibly small number for a
firm with $17 billion under management.
Unusual fee structure. BMIS did not charge management or performance fees, collecting
only brokerage commissions on trades. This allowed feeder funds to charge high fees to
their own investors. Given Madoff’s purported success, it made little sense that he would
forgo millions in management fees, which should have raised investor suspicion. The
arrangement likely encouraged feeder funds to overlook potential red flags.
Lack of transparency and restricted communication. Feeder funds were prohibited from
mentioning Madoff or BMIS in marketing materials and offering documents. Madoff did not
disclose AUM, which is rare in an industry known for publicizing size and success. Investors
were typically not permitted to visit BMIS, and on-site due diligence was either denied or
heavily restricted. Madoff also refused to answer questions about his investment strategy.
Late registration with the SEC. BMIS did not register as an investment adviser until 2006,
when a regulatory change closed a loophole that had allowed Madoff to avoid registration
by counting feeder funds as his “clients.” Even after the SEC later reversed this rule, Madoff
did not de-register, which may have given investors a false sense of regulatory oversight.
Use of paper trade confirmations. BMIS relied on paper trade tickets sent by mail rather
than providing electronic account access. This practice, combined with the lack of third-
party verification, made it possible for the firm to fabricate trade confirmations after the
fact to match reported performance results.
Investment Red Flags
Not only were there clear operational warning signs that should have alerted investors, but
there were also investment-related red flags that pointed to inconsistencies in Madoff’s
strategy and performance. These included the following:
Black box strategy. A split-strike conversion strategy can be profitable over time but will
inevitably produce some negative months. Such periods were completely absent from
Madoff’s reported results. The near-perfect consistency of returns should have raised
suspicion. Investors tried to rationalize the too-good-to-be-true performance by suggesting
superior stock selection, the use of market-making profits to smooth results, or leverage to
boost returns. Some even speculated that Madoff engaged in front running, an illegal
practice in the United States. In reality, no legitimate explanation could account for his
```
results; the strategy was effectively a black box.
```
Questionable style exposure. Madoff’s stated split-strike conversion strategy involved a long
index position, a short call, and a long put. However, quantitative style analysis of his
returns indicated exposure patterns that were the opposite of what the split-strike
conversion strategy would generate, implying that Madoff’s reported trades were either
misrepresented or never executed. Statistical analysis of Madoff’s performance data should
have raised serious red flags about what trades, if any, were actually taking place.
Illogical Form 13F filings. BMIS’s required Form 13F filings showed small holdings in minor,
non–S&P 100 companies, which was inconsistent with Madoff’s claim that the strategy
focused on S&P 100 stocks. Madoff claimed that he converted his portfolio to cash at the
end of each quarter to avoid public scrutiny of his positions. However, moving such
enormous sums each quarter would have been impractical and implausible, given the scale
of AUM.
Impossible use of S&P 100 options. Madoff claimed that his strategy used S&P 100 options
rather than S&P 500 options. This would have been prohibitively expensive and virtually
impossible in practice, as S&P 100 options are less liquid and less widely traded. With $17
billion under management, his trades would have moved the market significantly, yet no
such movements were observed. Madoff also claimed to trade OTC to explain the lack of
visibility, but this explanation does not hold up. Counterparties would have suffered losses
year after year, faced enormous credit exposures, and needed to hedge their positions, yet
there is no evidence that any of this occurred. The absence of confirmed counterparties
suggested that these trades were never actually executed.
It is important to note that some observersdid recognize the warning signs. A former
portfolio manager, Harry Markopolos, repeatedly warned the SEC about the inconsistencies in
Madoff’s operation in his 2005 report,The World’s Largest Hedge Fund Is a Fraud. He argued
that Madoff’s returns were impossible without either front running or a Ponzi-scheme
structure, but regulators dismissed his warnings. A few banks also declined to do business
with BMIS because of concerns about its practices. Even using only publicly available
information from BMIS’s Form ADV, a methodology developed by Brown, Goetzmann, Liang,
```
and Schwarz (2008)1, would have identified the firm as a problematic fund. Unfortunately,
```
greed often overrode sound judgment. Ultimately, this financial disaster serves as a powerful
reminder that due diligence is essential. Investors should never allow the promise of
attractive returns to overshadow clear red flags.
MODULE QUIZ 96.2
1. The fee structure at BMIS was a warning sign to investors because Madoff’s firm:
A. charged unusually high performance fees.
B. charged unusually high asset management fees.
C. charged only a brokerage commission on trades.
D. did not charge any fees, relying solely on investment returns.
2. The securities fraud at BMIS came to light when:
A. Harry Markopolos sent a 17-page letter to the SEC detailing the red flags at BMIS.
B. the SEC, after years of investigation, finally gathered enough evidence to indict Madoff.
C. Madoff confessed to his family that his investment advisory business was a giant Ponzi
scheme.
D. Madoff’s sons discovered evidence of the fraud on their own and immediately contacted
authorities.
KEY CONCEPTS
LO 96.a
Bernard Madoff operated what appeared to be a highly successful investment advisory
business for more than 17 years. In December 2008, the illusion collapsed when Madoff
confessed to family members that the business was, in reality, a massive Ponzi scheme.
```
Founded in 1960, Bernard L. Madoff Investment Securities, LLC (BMIS) began as a brokerage
```
firm that quoted bid and ask prices and executed OTC trades. Madoff was an early adopter of
electronic trading and became a prominent figure in modernizing U.S. financial markets. He
played a key role in the development of the NASDAQ stock market, serving on its board of
governors and eventually as chairman of its board of directors.
Madoff described his investment approach as a split-strike conversion strategy, a method that
combined stock and options positions. This strategy allegedly worked as follows:
Take a long position in a portfolio of stocks that is highly correlated with the S&P 100
Index.
```
Write (sell) out-of-the-money call options on the S&P 100 with a notional value comparable
```
to the equity portfolio.
Buy out-of-the-money put options on the S&P 100 with a notional value comparable to the
equity portfolio.
```
The result of the strategy was supposed to limit downside risk (through the puts) while
```
```
capping upside potential (through the calls). Madoff claimed to implement this strategy over
```
short time horizons, typically less than 30 days, and to hold the portfolio in cash much of the
time.
Through feeder funds that invested client capital with BMIS, Madoff reported steady, positive
returns of 8%–12% annually, with negative results in fewer than 5% of months. Although
these returns were similar in magnitude to those of the S&P 100, the volatility of the returns
was far lower. This was an implausible outcome that should have raised suspicion.
When the 2008 market downturn deepened, redemption requests surged, and BMIS could no
longer meet withdrawals. On December 10, 2008, Madoff admitted to his family that his
investment advisory business was “a giant Ponzi scheme.” The following day, he was arrested
and charged with securities fraud. Despite years of red flags and multiple warnings,
regulators had failed to uncover the scheme.
LO 96.b
Operational red flags that should have alerted investors and regulators included the following:
Lack of independent oversight. BMIS performed nearly all key functions internally,
eliminating third-party verification.
Conflict of interest. BMIS acted as both broker-dealer and market maker for some of its
own strategies.
Questionable auditing firm. A small, three-person audit firm oversaw $17 billion in assets, a
clear mismatch in scale and credibility.
Family control. Madoff’s relatives held senior positions, raising concerns about
independence and internal oversight.
Unusual fee structure. BMIS charged only brokerage commissions and no management or
performance fees, which was economically illogical.
Lack of transparency. Investors were not allowed to visit BMIS, and feeder funds were
barred from naming Madoff or the firm in any marketing or offering materials.
Late SEC registration. BMIS avoided registration as an investment adviser until 2006,
minimizing regulatory scrutiny.
Paper trade confirmations. Reliance on mailed paper tickets allowed BMIS to fabricate
trades and manipulate results.
Investment red flags that revealed inconsistencies in Madoff’s reported strategy and
performance included the following:
Black box strategy. Returns were unrealistically consistent with no down months—an
outcome impossible for any legitimate strategy.
Questionable style exposure. Reported returns did not match the risk exposures implied by
the stated split-strike conversion strategy.
Illogical Form 13F filings. Reported holdings in small, non–S&P 100 firms contradicted the
claimed investment focus.
Impossible use of S&P 100 options. The scale of alleged trading was implausible given the
illiquidity of S&P 100 options and absence of known counterparties.

READING 97
MARKET-DRIVEN SCENARIOS: AN APPROACH
FOR PLAUSIBLE SCENARIO CONSTRUCTION
Study Session 14
EXAM FOCUS
```
This reading focuses on scenario testing using a market-driven scenario (MDS) approach. For
```
the exam, understand why an MDS approach is needed, what it entails, and how it functions
as a conditional stress testing framework. Also, know how the Mahalanobis distance metric,
scenarioz-scores, volatilityz-scores, and correlationz-scores are applied within the MDS
methodology. In addition, be familiar with the five steps in the MDS process and how they
were implemented in the 2016 Brexit case study. Finally, be prepared to describe key
challenges and limitations associated with the MDS approach.
MODULE 97.1: MARKET-DRIVEN SCENARIO FRAMEWORK AND
STATISTICAL TOOLS
Market-Driven Scenario Approach
```
LO 97.a: Describe and evaluate the market-driven scenario (MDS) approach used
```
to develop scenarios for stress testing investment portfolios.
```
Before the Global Financial Crisis (GFC) of 2007–2009, stress testing was typically used as an
```
```
add-on to statistical models such as value at risk (VaR) or ex ante tracking error, with the goal
```
```
of estimating the profit and loss (P&L) impact of tail risks. Pre-GFC risk management relied on
```
VaR and tracking-error models with model-consistent stress tests, all of which depended
heavily on historical data. During the GFC, this limitation was exposed when historical
relationships between key market variables broke down entirely, rendering these models
unreliable.
Scenario analysis has emerged as an alternative approach because it allows for the
estimation of forward-looking events. One challenge, however, is the lack of an established
framework for constructing scenarios. In his best-selling bookThe Wisdom of Crowds, James
Surowiecki argues that decision-making improves when information is aggregated from many
individuals. He identifies three criteria:
1. Each individual should possess specialized knowledge that is not available to others.
2. Individual opinions must be independent and not influenced by groupthink.
3. There must be an objective mechanism for aggregating private views into a collective
assessment.
This creates space for a new risk management framework called the market-driven
```
scenario (MDS) approach, which provides a structured framework designed to reduce
```
subjectivity in scenario analysis. The MDS approach gathers input from a diverse group of
investment professionals across asset classes, regions, and areas of expertise. The firm’s risk
management department then develops a centralized process to aggregate these ideas into a
collective view. In doing so, the approach meets the three criteria outlined by Surowiecki.
The MDS approach begins with risk managers, investors, and economists periodically
identifying current issues that could affect financial markets, such as inflation, trade policy, or
monetary policy. Once an issue is selected, a set of idealized outcomes is defined, covering a
broad spectrum of plausible possibilities. These outcomes are then translated into
instantaneous shocks on policy variables, such as equity, bond, and/or commodity indices.
The process requires creativity in envisioning potential scenarios, followed by the use of
statistical tools to constrain and refine those ideas.
A multivariate measure called theMahalanobis distance is used to assess the plausibility and
magnitude of each shock. This process will be discussed in more detail later in this reading.
The shocks to policy variables are then passed through a covariance matrix, which is
reviewed for plausibility. The scenario is refined through several iterations until a collective
version is agreed upon. Once this stage is reached, the finalized shocks are entered into a
valuation engine and applied to the portfolio under analysis, producing a P&L that helps
identify the key underlying risk drivers.
Although this scenario approach contains an element of subjectivity, it remains highly
valuable. It requires risk takers to work through a structured process that quantifies their
subjective views. Once these views are made transparent, they can be challenged and
refined. This iterative feedback process is an essential component of effective portfolio risk
management.
Statistical Tools for MDSs
LO 97.b: Describe the scenario z-score, the volatility z-score, the correlation z-
score, and the Mahalanobis distance, and explain how these are used in
estimating the likelihood of occurrence and magnitude of scenarios.
Conditional Stress Testing Framework
The MDS approach can be viewed as a conditional stress testing framework. The total set of
```
M risk factors is influenced by shocks applied to a smaller subset ofK policy risk drivers (i.e.,
```
```
variables). These policy variables are typically major traded instruments such as the S&P 500
```
```
Index, bond yields, or factor-mimicking portfolios (e.g., momentum or growth). Keeping the
```
number of policy variables small helps minimize arbitrariness in the resulting perturbation
vector, which represents the collection of simultaneous shocks across multiple risk factors.
The shocks to the remainingM −K nonpolicy factors are then implied using multivariate
regression to generate the full perturbation vectorrj, as illustrated in the following formula:
```
where:
```
Estimating the β coefficients is equivalent to estimating the covariance matrix of theM factor
returns. Once the full perturbation vector is obtained from the regression, a portfolio’s P&L
can be calculated based on its factor exposures. Assuming asset returns are linear in the risk
```
factor shocks, the portfolio return under the specified policy and nonpolicy (implied) variable
```
shocks can then be estimated using the following formula:
```
where:
```
Positions with embedded optionality, which exhibit nonlinear payoffs, are typically fully
revalued using the full perturbation vector of factor shocks.
Mahalanobis Distance
As part of the MDS approach, a group of experts may propose a vector of policy variable
```
shocks (r). A natural question is how to objectively evaluate the plausibility of these shocks.
```
```
An implausible shock is one that is highly inconsistent with historical data (i.e., an outlier).
```
The most common statistical method for identifying such multivariate outliers is the
```
Mahalanobis distance (MD), which is a distance metric that accounts for the correlations
```
among data points. It identifies how many standard deviations a point is from the center of a
distribution, considering the shape of the data. The MD is essentially a multivariate
generalization of az-score:
```
where:
```
```
r = shock vector
```
μ = mean vector
∑ –1 = inverse covariance matrix of policy variables
Scenario Z-Score
To prevent the MD from increasing at a rate proportional to the square root of the number of
```
policy variables (n), a new measure called the scenarioz-score is applied. This measure
```
```
represents the severity of a given scenario (in standard deviations). When comparing two
```
scenarios, the one with the lower scenarioz-score is considered more plausible.
For modeling hypothetical future events, analysts often scale them subjectively by comparing
them with past outliers that appeared implausible beforehand but still occurred. This can be
done using a risk ruler, which provides a spectrum of historical events arranged from most
plausible to least plausible. A modified example is shown in Figure 97.1, where past events
```
are listed along with their associated scenarioz-scores (i.e., standard deviations) based on
```
```
the selected covariance matrix (e.g., using historical data or more recent data). Lowerz-
```
scores represent events that were more plausible. An analyst can use a risk ruler to assign a
standard deviation to a hypothetical future event by matching its perceived likelihood to one
of the historical events. This helps determine an appropriate scenarioz-score, with higher
values indicating more severe or less plausible market stresses.
Figure 97.1: Risk Ruler of Scenario Z-Scores1
Scenarioz-scores are designed to measure the relative probability of events under the
assumption that asset returns follow an elliptical distribution. Additional assumptions are
required to convert a scenarioz-score into an absolute probability. Two common approaches
can be applied: there is a parametric approach, which assumes a probability distribution for
```
thez-scores; and a nonparametric approach, which estimates an empirical distribution ofz-
```
scores over a historical time horizon.
Volatility Z-Score
```
Kinlaw and Turkington (2014)2 propose decomposing the scenarioz-score into two
```
```
components: one reflecting the magnitude of the shocks and the other reflecting their
```
consistency with the chosen covariance structure. The magnitude component can be isolated
by computing the scenarioz-score under the assumption that all correlations between shocks
are zero. This measure is known as the volatilityz-score, and its formula is as follows:
Correlation Z-Score
Having both the scenarioz-score and the volatilityz-score allows risk managers to normalize
the scenarioz-score for the magnitude of the shocks. The resulting measure is called the
correlationz-score, and its formula is as follows:
```
The correlationz-score can also be expressed directly in terms of the correlation matrix (Λ)
```
and the individual factorz-scores. This alternative formulation is as follows:
Note that the correlationz-score depends on the signs and relative magnitudes of the
individual factorz-scores. It does not depend on their absolute magnitudes, meaning that
doubling the size of all shocks would not change the correlationz-score. A high positive
```
correlationz-score (e.g., above 1.0) indicates that the covariance matrix may not adequately
```
capture the true co-movement of the policy variable shocks, while a value close to zero
suggests consistency.
Covariance Matrix Selection
Choosing the date and parameters used to construct the risk factor covariance matrix can
significantly affect the resultingz-scores. In practice, risk managers often compare shocks or
stress events using the following three approaches:
Slow data. Historical covariance matrix based on a long lookback period, such as five years
of weekly, equally-weighted data before the event.
Fast data. Historical covariance matrix based on a short lookback period, such as one year
of weekly, equally-weighted data before the event.
Recent data. Current covariance matrix, which may reflect post-event dynamics.
```
Each method changes how surprising a given scenario appears; and each produces a
```
different risk ruler, even when evaluating the same set of events. This is illustrated in Figures
97.2, 97.3, and 97.4. The appropriate covariance matrix choice depends on the scenario being
modeled. For example, if you are modeling a market crash, you should use data or a
covariance matrix from periods that experienced similar crashes, because correlations among
risky assets typically spike during market crises. Relying only on “normal” data would likely
understate the severity of the stress. As mentioned, the following figures illustrate risk rulers
constructed using the three covariance matrix approaches.3
```
Figure 97.2: Risk Ruler Using Historical Covariance Matrix (Slow Data)
```
```
Figure 97.3: Risk Ruler Using Historical Covariance Matrix (Fast Data)
```
```
Figure 97.4: Risk Ruler Using Recent Covariance Matrix (Dec. 2017)
```

MODULE 97.2: BUILDING AND IMPLEMENTING MARKET-DRIVEN
SCENARIOS
Defining, Developing, and Applying MDSs
LO 97.c: Explain steps in the process of defining and developing market-driven
scenarios, and describe the application of the MDS approach in the example
provided.
Guidelines for Reducing Subjectivity in MDSs
When constructing scenarios for the MDS approach, the following guidelines and diagnostic
tools can help reduce unnecessary subjectivity and improve consistency:
Hypothetical scenarios should be defined with enough precision to translate clearly into
shocks on market factors.
Key risk factors should not be highly correlated with one another.
Selected risk factors should be limited to a small, straightforward set that still captures the
key market dynamics.
Scenarioz-scores can be used to evaluate the plausibility, magnitude, and relative sizing of
shocks.
The data sample used to generate the covariance matrix should be consistent with the
nature of the scenario’s shocks.
Steps for Defining and Developing MDSs
The process of defining and developing MDSs consists of the following five essential steps:
```
Step 1: Define a market event. Defining scenarios involves two levels of uncertainty: (1) uncertainty about
```
```
whether the event will occur, and (2) uncertainty about how markets will react if the event does occur.
```
Several basic criteria help reduce this uncertainty:
–Choose a low-probability, but plausible, scenario. Known risks are already priced into
markets. The goal is to identify a risk that is unlikely but still possible and not yet
reflected in current prices.
–Define the scenario precisely. The expected market impact, including both
magnitude and direction, should be clearly specified and not open to interpretation.
–Ensure that the scenario affects markets relevant to the portfolio. For example,
shocks to value versus growth factors would have little relevance for a portfolio
invested solely in U.S. Treasuries.
–Identify a specific catalyst. Because the scenario is unlikely, it is helpful to specify
what event or development could plausibly trigger the scenario.
Step 2: Select policy variables. The key risk factors anchor the scenario and should be chosen to be simple, yet
representative of the underlying market dynamics. It is generally recommended to use a small number of
policy variables to minimize multicollinearity, which occurs when variables are highly correlated—and,
therefore, provide overlapping or redundant information. Avoid adding a new policy variable if it has an
adjusted R2 of 90% or higher relative to the existing policy variables. If a larger set of variables is
required, multicollinearity can be reduced by using “asset blocks,” where different groups of implied risk
factors are regressed on different subsets of policy variables.
```
Step 3: Calibrate shock sizes to policy variables. Shock sizes can be calibrated in two ways: (1) by identifying a
```
```
historical period that resembles the desired scenario, or (2) by setting a target frequency based on a
```
subjective view of the scenario’s likelihood. Under the second approach, the target frequency
corresponds to the likelihood of observing a scenario at least as extreme as the proposed one, as
measured by the scenarioz-score.
Step 4: Generate nonpolicy factor shocks from policy factor shocks. Shocks to nonpolicy risk factors are implied
from the specified policy factor shocks using the covariance matrix, which makes the choice of matrix
critically important. The selected time period should reflect conditions similar to the scenario being
modeled. For example, when simulating the impact of ending quantitative easing on the short end of the
yield curve, it is appropriate to use a period in which short-term rates were more volatile.
Step 5: Estimate portfolio stress test P&L. Stress test the portfolio’s P&L using the implied nonpolicy factor
shocks and the portfolio’s factor exposures. If the portfolio contains embedded optionality, the P&L must
be fully revalued rather than estimated with linear approximations.
Applying the MDS Approach: Brexit Example
Consider an example from the United Kingdom, which held a low-probability referendum in
```
June 2016 on leaving the European Union (EU). This was commonly referred to as “Brexit” (a
```
```
shortened form of “British Exit”). The referendum was driven by concerns related to
```
sovereignty, migration, welfare benefits, regulation, and economic governance. By February
2016, MDSs were created for both possible outcomes: a vote to “remain” or to “leave.” Any
portfolio with exposure to the euro, European sovereign debt, British sovereign debt,
European equities, or British equities needed a view on the potential impact of Brexit.
Although the “remain” camp appeared to hold an early 10% lead, the eventual decision to
“leave” surprised markets.
The goal of the MDS approach is to identify potential outcomes across the full spectrum of
risks. In the context of Brexit, three primary scenarios were considered:
1.Remain. The United Kingdom remains a member of the EU.
2.Soft Brexit. The United Kingdom leaves the EU, but trade relations with the rest of Europe
remain largely intact.
3.Adverse Brexit. The United Kingdom leaves the EU, and trade relations with the rest of
Europe deteriorate significantly.
This reading focuses on theSoft Brexit scenario. Several possible post-exit relationship
models with the EU were considered, including the following:
```
Participation in the EU single market (e.g., Norway)
```
```
Membership in a customs union (e.g., Turkey)
```
```
An EU “single-market-lite” agreement (e.g., Switzerland)
```
The consensus among risk managers and investment teams was that a Soft Brexit would
trigger risk-off sentiment in financial markets and prompt dovish monetary policy from the
Bank of England.
Policy variables were chosen with a focus on UK financial markets, including the FTSE All
```
Share Index, 10-year gilt (UK government bond) yields, and the British pound in foreign
```
exchange markets. Care was taken to distinguish a Soft Brexit from a standard economic
downturn. For example, a Soft Brexit was expected to affect the banking sector and housing
demand in ways that a typical economic shock would not. Figure 97.5 displays the policy
variables considered, along with relevant details sourced from BlackRock’s Aladdin in February
2016.
Figure 97.5: Soft Brexit Policy Variable Selection
```
Notice that a few shocks, such as those to Italian (ITL) sovereign debt and the Mexican
```
```
(MXN) peso, were intentionally kept small to dampen contagion outside the United Kingdom
```
and to prevent these factors from overwhelming the covariance matrix. In addition, policy
shocks were generally restricted to the same asset class to avoid introducing spurious
correlations that do not reflect meaningful economic relationships.
Consider how the Soft Brexit scenario was positioned on the revised risk ruler shown in
Figure 97.6. TheRemain Vote outcome was viewed as more likely thanSoft Brexit, while the
Adverse Brexit scenario, which would have resulted in a much more severe portfolio impact,
was deemed far less likely.
Figure 97.6: Risk Ruler Including Brexit Scenarios
In terms of sizing the shocks, direct historical comparisons offered limited guidance. For
```
example, the British pound’s departure from the Exchange Rate Mechanism (ERM) in 1992
```
was loosely related to Brexit, but that event was focused primarily on currency policy and did
not capture the broader economic implications of the referendum.
After the risk ruler was developed, asset class experts were consulted regarding the policy
variables, but no significant changes were recommended. The most debated variable was
interest rates. It was unclear whether gilts would serve as a safe haven during the volatility,
or whether investors would instead exit the UK fixed-income market. For the Soft Brexit
scenario, it was assumed that gilts would act as a safe haven, while in the Adverse Brexit
scenario, yields were expected to rise.
Figure 97.7 expands on the information presented in Figure 97.5 by adding a column labeled
“scenarioz-score delta,” which shows how much the scenarioz-score would change if a policy
shock were adjusted from its stressed value to its expected conditional value. Notice that the
delta is approximately zero for all policy shocks except rates. This indicates that, with the
exception of rates, the factor shocks are largely consistent with one another.
Figure 97.7: Expanded Soft Brexit Policy Variable Selection
It is very important to choose a time period for the covariance matrix that reflects the type of
stress being modeled. For example, a scenario involving geopolitical risk should use data from
a period characterized by significant political uncertainty. In the case of Brexit, there was no
truly comparable historical event, so the decision was made to use the covariance matrix
based on recent data.
Once the full set of shocks is specified, the P&L impact can be estimated. In this example,
using the policy shocks described, BlackRock’s Aladdin produced the P&L estimates shown in
Figure 97.8. The portfolio was assumed to hold 60% in the FTSE 100 Index and 40% in the
iBoxx GBP bond index. The sizable expected loss in equities was partially offset by gains in
bonds.
Figure 97.8: Estimated P&L Impact on a 60/40 Portfolio
The goal of the MDS approach is not to quantify tail losses or force risk managers to target
specific outcomes. Instead, it is a tool for integrating insights from different perspectives in a
structured way. By quantifying returns for scenarios with varying degrees of plausibility, it
encourages risk managers to reassess their strategic convictions and increases awareness of
the potential impact of alternative market outcomes.
Implementing MDSs
LO 97.d: Describe challenges and considerations that may arise when
implementing the market-driven scenario approach.
Several challenges and practical considerations can arise when implementing the MDS
approach, and understanding these issues is essential for applying the framework effectively.
Thefirst challenge is that the MDS approach is inherently subjective because it relies on
expert judgment to define hypothetical, yet plausible, market shocks. Unlike traditional
statistical models, which are grounded strictly in historical data, there is no universally
```
accepted method for constructing scenarios; this can lead to inconsistencies and ad hoc
```
decisions. With limited research on best practices for scenario design, organizations often
need to build their own internal frameworks, increasing the risk of bias and groupthink during
the scenario development process.
Asecond challenge involves translating economic events into quantitative shocks. Converting
macroeconomic developments into specific policy variable shocks is inherently subjective and
can vary significantly depending on the expertise and perspectives of the individuals involved.
The process may rely too heavily on educated guesses rather than robust structural models,
which may fail to capture out-of-sample dynamics or unexpected interactions.
Athird challenge is balancing simplicity with accuracy. There is an ongoing tension between
including enough variables to capture the essence of a scenario and maintaining a simple, but
effective, set of policy variables to avoid multicollinearity and model instability. Using too
many policy variables can introduce redundant or highly correlated factors, especially as
market conditions change, which reduces the reliability of scenario results.
Afourth challenge concerns the calibration and selection of the covariance matrix. The choice
of shock magnitudes and the time period or regime used to estimate the covariance matrix
can have a substantial effect on scenario results. Using data from “normal” versus “stressed”
markets can produce very different outcomes for the same set of shocks. There is no
universally accepted rule for selecting the appropriate historical window or estimation
method, which introduces subjectivity and increases the risk of misspecification if the chosen
covariance matrix does not reflect the intended scenario.
Afifth challenge involves designing shocks and evaluating their plausibility. Implied shocks to
nonpolicy risk factors are driven largely by the covariance structure and may fail to reflect
current market conditions if the historical covariance matrix is poorly chosen. Assessing the
plausibility of policy shocks, typically using Mahalanobis distance or scenarioz-scores, is
statistical in nature but still requires judgment about what qualifies as reasonable versus too
extreme.
Risk managers also need to consider organizational and process-related factors. Successful
implementation requires effective communication and the integration of diverse views from
risk managers, economists, and investors, while avoiding groupthink and maintaining
transparency and room for constructive challenges. Although the structured process of
“forcing transparency” is valuable, the final output still depends on the quality of the
underlying assumptions and the discipline applied in constructing the scenarios.
MODULE QUIZ 97.2
1. Which of the following statements is not recommended when developing a market-driven
```
scenario (MDS) analysis?
```
A. Specifying hypothetical scenarios precisely so they can be translated into market shocks.
B. Using scenario z-scores to assess the plausibility, magnitude, and relative sizing of shocks.
C. Selecting a covariance matrix sample that reflects market conditions consistent with the
scenario.
D. Including a large number of highly correlated key risk factors to maximize coverage of
different risks.
2. Which of the following statements best describes a key challenge in implementing an MDS
approach?
A. Scenario development often depends on expert judgment.
B. Using only historical data is recommended when constructing scenarios.
C. Using a large number of policy variables guarantees more accurate and reliable scenario
outputs.
D. Translating economic events into quantitative policy shocks relies solely on objective statistical
models.
KEY CONCEPTS
LO 97.a
Traditional forecasting tools rely primarily on historical data, a limitation that became
```
apparent during periods such as the Global Financial Crisis (GFC) when historical relationships
```
```
between market variables broke down. The market-driven scenario (MDS) approach
```
addresses this issue by incorporating forward-looking forecasts in a structured and systematic
way. It gathers estimates from a broad range of knowledgeable investment professionals and
then uses a consistent statistical framework to aggregate these independent views into a
coherent collective scenario. Although subjectivity is unavoidable, the MDS process helps
```
quantify subjective judgments; this makes them transparent and open to critique, ultimately
```
improving the quality and usefulness of scenario analysis.
LO 97.b
The MDS approach can be viewed as a conditional stress testing framework in which a set of
shocks is applied to a small number of policy variables. Limiting the number of policy
variables helps reduce arbitrariness in the resulting perturbation vector of factor shocks. The
Mahalanobis distance is used to identify outliers and assess the plausibility of proposed
shocks, while the scenarioz-score represents the severity of a scenario in standard deviation
terms. A scenario with a lowerz-score is considered more plausible, and a risk ruler based on
scenarioz-scores can help benchmark hypothetical scenarios against historical events.
Scenarioz-scores also tend to rise during periods of market stress.
The volatilityz-score isolates the magnitude of shocks under the assumption of zero
correlations, and the correlationz-score normalizes the scenarioz-score using the magnitude
component. Both measures provide insight into whether shocks are consistent with the
covariance structure. Becausez-scores are sensitive to the underlying covariance matrix, the
choice of data window and parameters can significantly influence results. When modeling a
market crash, it is important to use a covariance matrix derived from similar stressed periods
to avoid understating risk.
LO 97.c
The MDS approach relies on a structured five-step process:
Step 1: Defining a market event
Step 2: Selecting a small and well-targeted set of policy variables
Step 3: Calibrating shock sizes to the policy variables
Step 4: Generating nonpolicy factor shocks using a covariance matrix
Step 5: Estimating the resulting portfolio stress test P&L
Scenarios should be plausible but have a low probability of occurrence, with clearly defined
market impacts and identifiable catalysts that can be monitored. Policy variables should be
limited in number to avoid multicollinearity and ensure a stable perturbation vector. Finally,
the covariance matrix must be chosen carefully so that the estimation window reflects market
conditions similar to the stress being modeled, ensuring the shocks and implied correlations
are appropriate for the scenario.
LO 97.d
Implementing the MDS approach introduces several important challenges. The process is
inherently subjective because it relies on expert judgment without a universally accepted
framework for constructing scenarios. Translating economic events into quantitative shocks
can vary widely across practitioners, and balancing simplicity with accuracy requires limiting
policy variables to avoid multicollinearity while still capturing the essence of the scenario.
Scenario results are also highly sensitive to the calibration choices and the selection of the
covariance matrix, as different data windows can produce materially different implied shocks
and plausibility assessments. Even plausibility testing, typically performed using the
Mahalanobis distance and scenarioz-scores, still requires judgment about what constitutes a
reasonable versus extreme outcome. Finally, successful implementation requires effective
communication and coordination across teams to avoid groupthink and ensure transparency
in the scenario development process.
ANSWER KEY FOR MODULE QUIZZES
Module Quiz 97.1
1. D The MDS approach collects forward-looking views from a diverse group of investment
professionals and aggregates them using a structured framework that incorporates
```
statistical constraints (e.g., covariance matrices). Although subjectivity remains, the MDS
```
process channels it in a disciplined and transparent way, improving the plausibility and
usefulness of scenario analysis compared with traditional pre-GFC methods that
```
depended heavily on historical data relationships. (LO 97.a)
```
2. D The Mahalanobis distance (MD) is the primary tool for identifying multivariate outliers
because it measures how many standard deviations a shock vector lies from the
historical center, accounting for correlations. The scenarioz-score adjusts the MD for the
number of policy variables, the volatilityz-score isolates the magnitude of shocks, and
the correlationz-score measures how consistent the shocks are with the covariance
```
structure. (LO 97.b)
```
Module Quiz 97.2
1. D MDS analysis emphasizes using a small set of policy variables to avoid multicollinearity
and instability in the perturbation vector. Scenarios should be defined precisely, scenario
z-scores help assess plausibility and severity, and the covariance matrix should be
```
chosen to match the conditions of the scenario being modeled. (LO 97.c)
```
2. A A major challenge in implementing the MDS approach is the reliance on expert
judgment, which introduces subjectivity into scenario design. MDSs require a small,
carefully selected set of policy variables to avoid multicollinearity, and the choice of data
period for the covariance matrix is critical because it must align with the type of stress
```
being modeled. (LO 97.d)
```

The following is a review of the Risk Management and Investment Management principles designed to address the learning
objectives set forth by GARP®. Cross-reference to GARP assigned reading—Golub, Chapter 7.
READING 98
LIQUIDITY RISK MANAGEMENT
Study Session 14
EXAM FOCUS
This reading focuses on liquidity risk as it relates to meeting fund redemption needs. For the
exam, understand the four key elements of a liquidity risk framework and the critical
components of such a framework. Also, know why fixed-income securities pose greater
challenges than equities in measuring and managing liquidity risk. Be able to describe and
apply the approaches used to model asset liquidity for fixed-income instruments, including
the days-to-liquidate estimate, techniques for infrequently traded securities, and methods for
estimating transaction costs. In addition, be familiar with the extraordinary measures that
may be required to satisfy fund liquidity needs, such as temporary borrowing, in-kind
redemptions, gates, price-based mechanisms, and suspensions. Finally, understand how
redemption waterfalls operate and how redemption-at-risk models are constructed and
applied.
MODULE 98.1: MANAGING FUND LIQUIDITY RISK
Liquidity Risk Best Practices
LO 98.a: Explain best practices for managing liquidity risk in an investment fund.
The termliquidity can mean many different things, but in this reading, it refers specifically to
fund liquidity risk, which is the risk that a fund may be unable to obtain enough liquidity to
meet investor redemptions. This becomes particularly complex for hedge funds because of
the types of assets they hold and the challenge of meeting redemption requests in a way that
does not disadvantage the remaining shareholders.
Fund liquidity risk can arise as a byproduct of other issues, such as poor fund performance or
reputational problems involving managers. The most effective response is to establish a
robust, top-down governance structure supported by a strong liquidity risk management
framework. This framework should be clearly defined and operate independently within the
fund. It should be built around the following four key elements:
1.Liquidity risk measurement. Risk managers must identify, assess, and quantify the key
sources of liquidity risk.
2.Liquidity risk management. Risk managers must monitor the fund’s liquidity profile and
evaluate redemption risks relative to established limits.
3.Portfolio manager liquidity risk awareness. Risk managers should regularly review and
discuss liquidity risks with portfolio managers and escalate material concerns to senior
```
decision-makers (such as the chief risk officer [CRO] or chief investment officer [CIO]) in a
```
timely manner.
4.Redemption toolkit. Risk managers should understand the available backup liquidity tools
and be prepared to activate extraordinary measures when necessary.
This framework fits within the three lines of defense model. The first line of defense is the
portfolio managers, who must remain consistently aware of liquidity risk and who hold
primary responsibility for managing it. The second line of defense is an independent and
robust risk management function. The third line of defense is a periodic audit that verifies
whether the first two lines are adhering to established policies.
A comprehensive liquidity risk management system should measure the liquidity of the assets
held in the fund, analyze redemption patterns, and establish a process to ensure that
sufficient cash is available to meet expected redemptions in a timely manner. A critical goal is
to avoid creating liquidity for departing investors in a way that leaves the remaining
shareholders with an illiquid portfolio. In other words, the fund must balance meeting
redemption needs with preserving a sound strategy and structure for those who remain
```
invested. Key variables that influence liquidity include market conditions (e.g., normal vs.
```
```
stressed environments), position sizes, transaction costs, and potential market impact.
```
Fixed-Income Liquidity Risk Challenges
LO 98.b: Explain potential challenges fund managers face when managing
liquidity risk in fixed-income portfolios, including challenges related to data
modeling.
```
The low-interest-rate environment in the years following the Global Financial Crisis (GFC) of
```
2007–2009 encouraged significant corporate borrowing, including a surge in nonstandard
issuances. Before the GFC, most asset managers acted as “price takers” in over-the-counter
```
(OTC) markets, requesting quotes from several dealers and accepting the best one. After the
```
GFC, the trend shifted toward being “price makers,” where asset managers set the price they
are willing to transact at and then seek out counterparties at that level. This shift has
contributed to thinner secondary markets, characterized by lower turnover and wider bid-ask
spreads, and has increased the price impact of large transactions. As a result, many
managers now break large trades into smaller orders to reduce market impact. The cost of
liquidity rises as the need for immediacy increases, and this effect is further amplified when
dealing with more complex assets, such as structured or specialized fixed-income products.
Equity markets benefit from strong price discovery. They contain a manageable number of
securities that trade frequently on public exchanges, which creates meaningful price
transparency and supports effective liquidity risk management. Fixed-income markets, in
contrast, are far more fragmented. There are an enormous number of securities, and
```
municipal bonds alone account for roughly one million CUSIPs (unique identification numbers
```
```
assigned to securities). Only a small fraction of available fixed-income securities trade on any
```
given day, with activity concentrated in large and recently issued offerings. This limited and
uneven price discovery process makes it naturally more difficult to assess liquidity risk in
fixed-income markets.
Consider an example of a retired couple who has lived in the same home for 30 years and
has never refinanced, leaving the property mortgage free. Estimating its value requires
relying on the sale prices of comparable homes, but there will never be a perfect one-to-one
comparison because each property has unique features. The valuation becomes easier if the
house is a rental with an established income stream, because that provides an additional
basis for assessment. Fixed-income valuation works in a similar way. The difficulty in
valuation grows rapidly as the structure of the fixed-income product becomes more complex.
Data availability is another challenge in modeling fixed-income liquidity risk. The Financial
```
Industry Regulatory Authority’s (FINRA’s) Trade Reporting and Compliance Engine
```
```
(TRACE) provides daily price and volume information for bonds, but reported trade volumes
```
are capped. Exact volumes are shown only for trades below the cap, while high-yield trades
above $1 million and investment-grade trades above $5 million are reported only as being
above the cap. This creates ambiguity because it matters greatly whether the true volume is
$6 million or $60 million. Many models try to infer the actual volume by using historical
trading patterns, but this can lead to inaccuracies when future conditions differ from past
trends. This issue will be explored further later in this reading.
Several recent initiatives to improve data transparency in fixed-income markets are as
```
follows:
```
As of 2025, FINRA continues to apply caps on reported trade volumes but now provides
uncapped trading volume at the CUSIP level with a six-month delay.
```
In Europe, the Markets in Financial Instruments Directive II (MiFID II) offers detailed
```
intraday snapshots of price and volume data for liquid bonds, along with data for less liquid
bonds that is released with a one-month lag.
MiFID II also requires asset managers to report realized transaction costs for European
funds, which further enhances market transparency.
The European Commission is considering additional amendments to MiFID II, including the
introduction of a standardized consolidated tape that would provide unified pricing data for
both equity and fixed-income securities. A consolidated tape is a unified data feed that
aggregates pricing and volume information from all trading venues into one standardized
source.

spreads.
B. Equity markets rely primarily on OTC transactions, reducing price transparency relative to

MODULE 98.2: MODELING ASSET LIQUIDITY
LO 98.c: Describe and apply different approaches to model asset liquidity,
including an approach to modeling infrequently traded bonds and the t-cost model
to calculate transaction costs for corporate bonds.
Days-to-Liquidate Approach
There are several methods for modeling asset liquidity. One common approach is the days-
to-liquidate approach, which estimates how long it would take to unwind a position based
```
on (1) the size of the intended trade, (2) an assumed market participation rate, and (3) the
```
```
asset’s average daily volume (ADV). The general idea is illustrated in the following formula:
```
The size of the trade can be estimated using historical redemption patterns, portfolio
leverage, derivatives exposures, and other factors evaluated on a case-by-case basis.
The market participation rate reflects the percentage of a security’s ADV that can be sold
without causing significant market impact. It is inherently difficult to accurately estimate. In
practice, actual trading volume is usually lower than the volume the market could
theoretically support. The difference between observable liquidity and this potential capacity
is referred to as latent liquidity.
```
The average daily volume (ADV) represents the average trading volume over a specified
```
```
window, measured on a daily basis (e.g., 20-day or 60-day average daily volume). It may be
```
calculated using data from the firm, exchanges, or third-party vendors, and it can incorporate
modeled inputs such as bonds outstanding or bid-ask spreads. When direct data is limited,
ADV may also be estimated using asset characteristics, sector-level turnover, or insights from
experienced traders.
Modeling Liquidity for Infrequently Traded Bonds
Attempts have been made to adapt the days-to-liquidate approach for infrequently traded
fixed-income securities by using historical data to fill in missing observations. This infrequent
trading problem can be broken into two components:
1. Estimating the probability that a trade will occur
2. Predicting the trade volume if the trade does occur
A random forest regression model, which is a machine learning technique, can be used for
this purpose. This method is well suited for handling nonlinearity, missing data, and large sets
of explanatory variables. The general approach is summarized as follows, assuming that
expected volume on no-trade days is zero:
Historical trading activity can be a reasonable guide to future volume when a bond trades
frequently. However, this relationship breaks down when a bond trades only sporadically. In
such cases, data from comparable bonds can be used to help fill in the gaps.
Another data source is dealer inventory information from secondary markets, which provides
posted bids and asks that help infer potential tradable volume. This approach is imperfect
because actual trading volume is typically lower than the volume the market could
theoretically support. The difference, previously referred to as latent liquidity, can be
estimated using tail measures such as the 90th percentile of the ADV distribution. This
modeling framework helps quantify a security’s potential liquidity within a range defined by
the optimistic tail estimate and the observed ADV.
Another important consideration is the variance in daily observed trading volume. Large
fluctuations in actual volume can lead to similarly large variations in estimated volume. Figure
98.1 shows the daily trading volume for an individual corporate bond over a 180-day period,
illustrating that ADV can be a conservative measure of liquidity when volume variance is high.
Figure 98.1: Daily Trading Volume for a Corporate Bond Over 180 Days1
Transaction Cost Modeling for Corporate Bonds
```
Transaction costs (i.e., t-costs) are also a critical consideration when assessing liquidity
```
risk. Care must be taken to minimize these costs, which are a key component of best-
execution verification. Both the time required to liquidate a position and the potential market
impact are evaluated as part of this process. Quickly liquidating assets on a pro rata basis
may cause undesirable price effects, while maintaining higher ongoing liquidity can constrain
return objectives. A primary input in t-cost modeling is price, which carries significant
uncertainty in fixed-income markets. Recent efforts to standardize fixed-income pricing data
should help improve the accuracy of these models.
At a basic level, t-costs consist offixed costs, such as commissions and bid-ask spreads,
along with realizedmarket impact costs, which reflect how position size affects execution
costs. The bid-ask spread can be normalized by the midprice of benchmark quotes, but
comparability across bonds becomes less reliable when the securities trade infrequently.
A key priority in modeling liquidity risk for fixed-income assets is improving the quality and
availability of price data. Implementation shortfall, which measures the difference
between the executed price and a benchmark price, depends heavily on accurate price
discovery. Because fixed-income markets often lack transparent and timely pricing, estimates
can be noisy. Measurement error must be considered, especially in cases where the potential
error is large relative to the value being measured.
T-cost modeling uses intraday benchmark prices, empirical transaction data, and bond-
specific attributes to forecast both fixed costs and market impact costs. Regression
techniques are also applied to estimate certain components of the model. The standard
functional form that follows reflects this structure—the first component captures fixed costs,
while the second component estimates market impact costs.
The primary purpose of t-cost modeling is to estimate market impact. This is measured using
```
implementation shortfall, which is calculated as the difference between the traded price (the
```
```
actual execution price) and the order-arrival benchmark price or another appropriate pre-
```
trade benchmark.
Some practitioners use the previous end-of-day price as the benchmark for measuring
implementation shortfall. However, this approach can introduce a sizable return component
because any market-driven price movement that occurs between yesterday’s close and the
time of execution becomes part of the measured cost. Using an intraday, pre-trade
benchmark price is considered best practice, as it better reflects prevailing market conditions
and is less susceptible to manipulation.
When estimating transaction costs through regression techniques, it is helpful to weight
observations by the dollar amount traded to account for the large number of small, odd-lot
trades. Security-level data generally provides the most accurate insight, but when a bond has
limited or missing data, it is often necessary to rely on a cohort of similar securities. These
cohorts can be constructed using characteristics such as option-adjusted spread, duration,
yield, or bid-ask spread. Machine learning techniques, including clustering methods, tree-
based models, and neural networks, can further assist in identifying the degree of similarity
across securities and improving the robustness of the estimates.

B. Trade frequency and volume follow a linear pattern that basic regression can capture.

D. Relying on recent trade volumes can bias estimates because latent liquidity requires

benchmark price typically preferred over the previous end-of-day price? The intraday, pre-trade
benchmark price:
A. reduces benchmark-related noise.
B. ensures that market impact is zero.
