MODULE 98.3: MANAGING AND MODELING REDEMPTION RISK
Meeting Unanticipated Redemption Requests
LO 98.d: Identify examples of extraordinary measures that regulators may allow
funds to take to meet unanticipated redemption requests, and explain when each
measure may be allowed.
The most effective way for a fund to manage unexpected redemption requests is through
advance planning, but some jurisdictions also permit extraordinary measures when ordinary
liquidity tools prove insufficient. Although such tools are rarely invoked, they may be needed
on short notice. Legal, operations, risk management, and trading teams should conduct
“break glass” testing to ensure they can activate each measure quickly if required. This
section summarizes the extraordinary measures and provides guidance on when each may be
used.
Temporary borrowing provides a short-term liquidity buffer that gives the fund additional
time to sell less liquid assets in an orderly manner. Common tools include overdrafts,
```
repurchase agreements (repos), bank credit facilities, and interfund lending arrangements.
```
This option is permitted for U.S.-based retail funds but is not allowed for European UCITS
```
(regulated) funds.
```
In-kind redemptions allow specific assets to be transferred directly to the redeeming
investor, eliminating the need for the fund to sell those holdings. This method is generally
used only for certain asset types and is most common with large institutional investors
because it relies on specialized electronic transfer systems. It is permitted by regulation,
although its practical use remains limited to appropriate asset types and investors capable of
receiving securities directly.
Gates allow partial redemptions up to a predetermined threshold, such as 10% of fund
assets. They can temporarily slow or prevent a run on the fund by limiting the pace of
outflows. Gates are permitted for UCITS funds in Europe but are not allowed for U.S.-based
funds.
Price mechanisms include tools such as swing pricing and purchase redemption values
```
(PRVs), which shift transaction costs to subscribing or redeeming investors. Swing pricing
```
```
works by adjusting a fund’s net asset value (NAV) when net flows exceed a threshold,
```
ensuring that the costs of trading are absorbed by transacting investors rather than the
remaining shareholders. By increasing the cost of rapid exits, these mechanisms help reduce
the incentive for a run on the fund. Swing pricing is permitted and widely used for UCITS
```
funds in Europe. PRV is allowed for U.S. collective trust funds (CTFs) under Office of the
```
```
Comptroller of the Currency (OCC) oversight. The SEC does not currently allow swing pricing
```
for mutual funds, although it is under consideration in a rulemaking planned for 2025.
Suspension is the most extreme measure, involving a complete halt to redemptions. While it
can be in the long-term interest of investors during periods of severe market stress, it also
creates significant operational and reputational challenges. Suspensions are not permitted for
U.S.-based mutual funds without explicit approval from the SEC.
Modeling Redemption Risk
LO 98.e: Explain approaches to manage and model redemption risk in an
investment fund, including the redemption waterfall, approaches to model a
fund’s level of redemption-at-risk, and approaches to optimize liquidity to meet
redemption requests.
Understanding and Managing Redemption Risk
A critical aspect of managing liquidity risk is preparing to meet redemption requests under all
market conditions. Unexpected or unusually large outflows may be driven by macroeconomic
developments, poor fund performance, deterioration in a manager’s reputation, severe
```
underperformance by peer funds (which can create contagion risk), or fund-specific
```
characteristics such as investor profiles and concentration levels. Under normal conditions,
redemption demand is typically low before a strategy reaches maturity, but stressed markets
can produce large and highly correlated redemption requests. The ultimate objective is to
manage redemptions in a way that treats all three categories of investors—those redeeming,
those subscribing, and those remaining in the fund—fairly and equitably.
U.S.-based ’40 Act funds typically use a redemption waterfall, which is a sequence of
layers that are accessed to meet redemption requests. When one layer does not have enough
assets to satisfy demand, the fund moves to the next layer. The most common layers are as
```
follows:
```
Layer 1: cash and highly liquid assets. These assets may be used for three purposes:
```
meeting routine or unplanned redemption “noise” while minimizing transaction costs;
```
```
supporting an investment strategy that uses a derivatives overlay for return enhancement;
```
or responding strategically to market conditions.
Layer 2: pro rata or risk-constant asset sales. When redemption requests exceed the assets
available in Layer 1, the fund may need to sell less liquid holdings. Care must be taken to
ensure that the remaining portfolio maintains approximately the same risk profile as it had
before the redemptions.
Layer 3: short-term borrowings. As a final option, funds may access backup liquidity
sources such as reverse repos, overdraft facilities, or lines of credit. ’40 Act funds are
subject to a limit that restricts borrowing to no more than 33.3% of total fund assets.
Stress testing is a natural extension of redemption modeling. Stress scenarios can be applied
to fund assets, redemption volumes, ADV assumptions, and transaction costs. It is also
important to consider investor concentration, relevant historical redemption events, and the
potential effects of any gates that may be in place.
Modeling Redemption-at-Risk
Modeling redemption demand is complex. In an ideal setting, analysts could estimate worst-
case outflows based on historical fund flows, such as the 99th-percentile, five-day redemption
scenario over the life of the fund. This approach forms the basis of the historical
```
redemption-at-risk (HRaR) model, which generates potential stress signals for fund
```
managers. However, several challenges arise when relying solely on historical patterns:
Some funds may not have experienced meaningful outflows because they are either new or
still in a rapid growth phase.
Fund redemptions may be driven by idiosyncratic investor behavior that cannot be modeled
reliably.
Some asset managers lack granular visibility into who is submitting redemption requests
because a third party, such as a custodian, operates between the fund and the end
investor.
Large outflows typically arise from a combination of systemic and idiosyncratic factors.
Systemic influences may include sector-wide disruptions or broader macroeconomic shocks.
Idiosyncratic factors can include investor behavior, peer fund performance, discretionary cash
flow needs, and various event-driven risks such as insider-trading scandals, regulatory
sanctions, or damage to a manager’s reputation.
Machine learning has been explored for HRaR modeling because it can handle the large
number of factors that may influence redemption activity and can capture nonlinear
relationships in the data. Such approaches can be used to estimate an upper bound on likely
redemptions over a given time horizon. However, data availability and data quality remain
significant obstacles to implementation.
Liquidity Optimization to Meet Redemption Requests
Fund liquidity management can also be framed as an optimization problem in which
managers balance liquidity, transaction costs, and portfolio risk while meeting redemption
obligations. Consider a layered approach to optimization using securitiesxi with market value
mi and notional valueni. The portfolio manager must sell a pro rata fractionpi, t of the
portfolio on a given dayt. The first step is to determine the optimal pro rata fraction:
The goal is to maximize the following expression, subject to any constraints that have been
```
specified:
```
Actual or expected redemption obligations can be incorporated as constraints. For example,
the optimization of this expression could be constrained by a required liquidation amountv
over a specified period, such as $200 million within two days. Adding such constraints
enables a more detailed and cost-efficient design of the liquidation schedule.
ADV can also be incorporated as a constraint. Suppose securityi has an ADVvi and belongs
to sectorj, which has an assigned participation raterj. Under this constraint, the following
relationship applies:
```
The capacity (cj, t) for a given market sector (Sj) can also be taken into account. This is
```
important because large, simultaneous sales of similar fixed-income securities can quickly
exhaust the market’s ability to absorb additional supply. Under this constraint, the following
relationship applies:
Some funds may have exposure to strategies with fixed time constraints, such as IPO
lockups. To account for this, a time-lag constraint measured in daysdi can be added to the
optimization process. Under this constraint, the following applies:
Well-managed portfolios also incorporate a risk constraint, such as remaining below a
specified risk thresholdR. An example is an index fund that must monitor and control
tracking error. Under this type of constraint, the following applies:
Note that the benchmark levelbi will equal zero for absolute return funds. This summation
equation should include the full set of securities in both the benchmark and the portfolio.
Additional optimization constraints may be incorporated as needed, including those related to
cash use, tracking error, leverage limits, compliance requirements, and other fund-specific
considerations.
The overall optimization process for meeting redemption requests is ultimately constrained by
the quality and availability of the model’s input data.
MODULE QUIZ 98.3
1. A U.S.-based hedge fund needs to use an extraordinary liquidity measure to meet unexpected
redemption requests. Which of the following options is available to them?
A. Gates.
B. Suspension.
C. Swing pricing.
D. Temporary borrowing.
2. An analyst is applying the historical redemption-at-risk (HRaR) model to estimate potential
redemption demand. Which of the following statements is least likely to represent a key
challenge of this approach?
A. Some funds may lack sufficient redemption history because they are new or have been
growing rapidly.
B. Limited transparency between asset managers and end investors can reduce the quality of
available data.
C. Investor withdrawal behavior may be driven by idiosyncratic factors that are difficult to model
statistically.
D. Historical data provides a stable and reliable indicator of future redemption pressures across
all market environments.
KEY CONCEPTS
LO 98.a
```
A sound liquidity risk framework is built on four pillars: (1) liquidity risk measurement, which
```
```
quantifies trading and funding liquidity needs; (2) liquidity risk management, which uses
```
those measurements to maintain buffers, monitor cash flows, and manage liquidity
```
transformation; (3) portfolio manager awareness, ensuring investment decisions remain
```
```
aligned with the fund’s liquidity profile; and (4) extraordinary tools, such as temporary
```
borrowing or gates, that can be used when normal liquidity is not enough. Across all four
elements, the fund must balance the interests of redeeming, subscribing, and remaining
investors, because forced liquidity creation during stressed periods can distort the portfolio
and disadvantage shareholders who remain.
LO 98.b
Fixed-income liquidity risk is more difficult to manage because bond markets lack the rapid
price discovery found in equities. Price transparency is limited, and the enormous number of
fixed-income securities, combined with the complexity of many structures, makes it
challenging to assess market depth and estimate transaction costs. Many bonds trade
infrequently, so observable pricing information is often sparse.
Data availability adds another layer of difficulty because liquidity models require accurate and
timely trading information, which is often incomplete or inconsistent in fixed-income markets.
Systems such as TRACE provide helpful trade data, but volume caps and inconsistent trading
activity restrict visibility into true trading volumes, which means liquidity models must rely on
incomplete information. Recent regulatory initiatives, including improved TRACE disclosures
and MiFID II transparency rules, have expanded access to fixed-income data, but they have
not fully resolved the underlying challenges of measuring and modeling liquidity in these
markets.
LO 98.c
Fixed-income liquidity can be modeled using several approaches. One common method is the
days-to-liquidate approach, which estimates how long it would take to unwind a position
based on the size of the intended trade, the assumed market participation rate, and the
```
security’s average daily volume (ADV). ADV may be measured directly or estimated, and both
```
trade size and participation rate are often inferred from historical patterns. Because observed
trading activity usually understates true market capacity, analysts must consider latent
liquidity, which is the difference between the actual traded volume and the volume the
market could potentially support.
For bonds that trade infrequently, more advanced models are needed to handle sparse
observations, nonlinear relationships, and wide variation in daily trading volume. These
approaches may draw on information from comparable bonds or dealer inventory postings to
help infer potential tradable volume when direct data is limited.
```
Transaction costs (t-costs) are another important aspect of liquidity modeling. These costs
```
can be estimated by combining fixed costs, such as commissions and bid-ask spreads, with
market impact costs. Market impact is often evaluated using implementation shortfall, which
measures the difference between the executed price and an appropriate benchmark price.
LO 98.d
The best preparation for unexpected redemption pressures is strong liquidity planning, but
some jurisdictions allow extraordinary measures when normal tools are insufficient. Key
measures include the following:
Temporary borrowing. Short-term tools include overdrafts, repos, bank credit facilities, and
interfund lending arrangements. This option is permitted for U.S. retail funds but not for
European UCITS funds.
In-kind redemptions. Specific assets are transferred directly to redeeming investors,
avoiding forced sales. The method is allowed in both the United States and Europe,
although it is typically feasible only for certain assets and large institutional investors.
```
Gates. These limit redemptions to a set percentage of fund assets (e.g., 10%). Gates are
```
allowed for UCITS funds in Europe but not for U.S. mutual funds.
```
Price mechanisms. Tools such as swing pricing and purchase redemption values (PRVs)
```
allocate transaction costs to redeeming or subscribing investors, reducing first-mover
```
incentives. Swing pricing is permitted and common in UCITS funds; PRV is allowed for U.S.
```
```
collective trust funds (CTFs).
```
Suspension. This is a temporary halt to all redemptions, used only in severe market stress.
Suspensions are not permitted for U.S. mutual funds without explicit SEC approval.
LO 98.e
Managers must be prepared for unexpected redemption requests. One common framework is
```
the redemption waterfall, which relies on three layers of liquidity: (1) cash and highly liquid
```
```
assets, (2) pro rata sales of less liquid holdings, and (3) short-term borrowing facilities. Stress
```
testing should be used to evaluate how each layer would function under different market
conditions.
```
A historical redemption-at-risk (HRaR) model can help managers identify potential stress
```
points by estimating extreme but plausible outflows. Because redemptions are often driven by
a mix of systemic and idiosyncratic factors, modeling them is complex. A more detailed
approach views liquidity planning as an optimization problem that incorporates redemption
obligations, market capacity, ADV constraints, time lags, and risk limits. The effectiveness of
these models ultimately depends on the quality and the availability of the underlying data.


1 BlackRock, FINRA TRACE.
The following is a review of the Risk Management and Investment Management principles designed to address the learning
objectives set forth by GARP®. Cross-reference to GARP assigned reading—Ang, Chapter 13.
READING 99
ILLIQUID ASSETS
Study Session 14
EXAM FOCUS
This reading examines illiquid asset market characteristics and the relationship between
illiquidity and market imperfections. Reported return biases are discussed as well as the
illiquidity risk premium within and across asset classes. For the exam, understand that all
markets, even highly liquid markets such as commercial paper, can be illiquid at some points
in time. Also, know the three biases that impact reported returns of illiquid asset classes
```
(survivorship bias, sample selection bias, and infrequent sampling). Finally, understand the
```
factors that influence the decision to include illiquid asset classes in a portfolio.
MODULE 99.1: ILLIQUID MARKETS, MARKET IMPERFECTIONS,
BIASES, AND UNSMOOTHING
Illiquid Asset Markets
LO 99.a: Evaluate the characteristics of illiquid markets.
There are several characteristics that describe illiquid asset markets, including:
1. Most asset classes are illiquid, at least to some degree.
2. Markets for illiquid assets are large.
3. Illiquid assets comprise the bulk of most investors’ portfolios.
4. Liquidity dries up even in liquid asset markets.
Most Asset Classes Are Illiquid
All markets, even large-cap equity markets, are somewhat illiquid. It is clear, however, that
```
some assets (e.g., real estate) are less liquid than others (e.g., public equities). Illiquid assets
```
trade infrequently, in small amounts, and generally exhibit low turnover. For example, there
are mere seconds between transactions in public equity markets with an annualized turnover
```
rate greater than 100%. In contrast, over-the-counter (OTC) equities typically trade within a
```
day, but sometimes a week or more may pass between trades, with annualized turnover of
25% to 35%. Corporate bonds trade daily, and municipal bonds typically trade semiannually.
At the far end of the liquidity spectrum is institutional infrastructure with an average
```
investment commitment of 50 to 60 years (up to 99 years), and art, with 40 to 70 years
```
between transactions. There is negligible turnover in infrastructure. Turnover in residential
real estate is about 5% per year, while turnover in institutional real estate is approximately
7%. The time between real estate transactions can range from months to decades.
Markets for Illiquid Assets Are Large
The size of the U.S. residential mortgage market was $16 trillion in 2012. The institutional
real estate market was measured at $9 trillion. In contrast, the market capitalization of the
NYSE and Nasdaq combined was approximately $17 trillion. The total wealth held in illiquid
assets exceeds the total wealth in traditional, liquid stock, and bond markets.
Investor Holdings of Illiquid Assets
The home is often an individual’s most valuable asset. As a result, illiquid assets represent
approximately 90% of total wealth, not counting human capital, the largest and least liquid
asset for many individual investors. High-net-worth individuals in the United States even
typically allocate 10% of portfolios to fine art and jewelry, known as treasure assets. High-
net-worth individuals in foreign countries hold an average of 20% in treasure assets.
Institutional investors have also increased allocations to illiquid assets over the last 20 years.
University endowments have increased allocations of illiquid assets to approximately 25%, up
from 5% in the early 1990s. Pension funds have increased allocations to approximately 20%,
up from 5% in 1995. Overall, investors tend to maintain significant holdings of illiquid assets.
Liquidity Can Dry Up
In stressed economic periods, such as during the 2007–2009 financial crisis, liquidity can dry
```
up. For example, money markets froze (i.e., repurchase agreement and commercial paper
```
```
markets) during the crisis as investors were unwilling to trade at any price. The residential
```
and commercial mortgage-backed securities markets, structured credit markets, and the
auction rate securities market, a market for floating rate municipal bonds, also became illiquid
during the crisis. The auction rate securities market is still frozen, more than six years later.
Major liquidity crises have occurred at least once every 10 years across the globe, in
conjunction with downturns and financial distress.
Market Imperfections
LO 99.b: Discuss the relationship between market imperfections and illiquidity.
Many economic theories assume that markets are perfect. This means that market
```
participants are rational and pursue utility maximization; that there are no transaction costs,
```
```
regulation, or taxes; that assets are perfectly divisible; that there is perfect competition in
```
```
markets; and that all market participants receive information simultaneously. The reality,
```
though, is that markets are imperfect.
Imperfections that encourage illiquidity include:
Market participation costs. There are costs associated with entering markets, including
the time, money, and energy required to understand a new market. In many illiquid
markets, only certain types of investors have the expertise, capital, and experience to
participate. This is called the clientele effect. There will be less liquidity in markets that
are suited to a limited number of investors and/or where there are barriers to entry in
terms of required experience, capital, or expertise.
Transaction costs. Transaction costs include taxes and commissions. For many illiquid
assets, like private equity, there are additional costs, including costs associated with
performing due diligence. Investors must pay attorneys, accountants, and investment
bankers. These costs can impede investment.
```
When acknowledging the existence of transaction costs (i.e., acknowledging that markets are
```
```
imperfect), some academic studies assume that as long as an investor can pay the
```
```
transaction costs (and sometimes these costs are large), then any investor can transact (i.e.,
```
```
any asset can be liquid if one can pay the transaction cost). However, this is not always true.
```
For example, the following pose challenges:
```
Difficulties in finding a counterparty (i.e., search frictions). For example, it may be
```
difficult to find someone to understand/purchase a complicated structured credit product.
It may also be difficult to find buyers with sufficient capital to purchase an office tower or a
skyscraper in a city like New York. No matter how high the transaction cost, it may take
weeks, months, or years to transact in some situations.
Asymmetric information. Some investors have more information than others. If an
investor fears that the counterparty knows more than he does, he will be less willing to
trade, which increases illiquidity. When asymmetric information is extreme, people assume
all products are lemons. Because no one wants to buy a lemon, markets break down.
Often, liquidity freezes are the result of asymmetric information. Because investors are
looking for non-predatory counterparties who are not seeking to take advantage of
asymmetric information, information itself can be a form of search friction.
Price impacts. Large trades can move markets, which in turn can result in liquidity issues
for the asset or asset class.
Funding constraints. Many illiquid assets are financed largely with debt. For example,
even at the individual level, housing purchases are highly leveraged. As a result, if access
to credit is compromised, investors cannot transact.
Illiquid Asset Return Biases
LO 99.c: Assess the impact of biases on reported returns for illiquid assets.
LO 99.d: Explain the process of unsmoothing returns and the effects of
unsmoothing.
In general, investors should be skeptical of reported returns in illiquid asset markets. The
reason is that reported returns are generally overstated. There are reporting biases that
result in inflated returns. Three main biases that impact returns of illiquid assets are:
Survivorship bias.
Selection bias.
Infrequent trading.
Survivorship Bias
```
There are no requirements for certain types of funds (e.g., private equity, hedge funds,
```
```
buyout funds, and so on) to report returns to database providers. As such, poorly performing
```
funds tend to stop reporting. Additionally, funds may never begin reporting because returns
are not high enough to appeal to investors. This results in reporting biases. In addition,
many poorly performing funds ultimately fail. Performance studies generally include only
those funds that were successful enough to survive over the entire period of analysis, leaving
out the returns of funds that no longer exist. Both of these factors result in reported returns
that are too high. This is called survivorship bias. Non-surviving funds have below-average
returns, and surviving funds have above-average returns, but it is the surviving fund returns
that are reported. Studies show mutual fund returns are 1% to 2% lower than reported, and
returns may be as much as 4% lower for illiquid asset markets. While the solution to
```
survivorship bias seems obvious (to observe the entire universe of funds), it is impossible to
```
do in illiquid asset markets.
Sample Selection Bias
Asset values and returns tend to be reported when they are high. For example, houses and
office buildings typically are sold when values are high. Often, a seller will wait until property
values recover before selling. These higher selling prices are then used to calculate returns.
This results in a sample selection bias.
The problem with selection bias is especially prevalent in private equity markets. Buyout
funds take companies public when stock prices are high. Venture capitalists sell companies
when values are high. Distressed companies are often not liquidated and left as shell
```
companies (these are sometimes called zombie companies). It is difficult to tell, based on old
```
data without any recent transactions, if a company is alive or whether it is a “zombie”
company.
Impacts of sample selection bias include:
Higher reported alphas relative to true alphas because only high prices are recorded. For
example, one study estimates an alpha of more than 90% for venture capital log returns.
However, alpha falls to –7% after correcting for sample selection bias. Another study
estimates returns are decreased 2% to 5% per month if you correct for the bias.
```
Lower reported betas than true betas because there are fewer (only high) prices recorded,
```
```
flattening the security market line (SML). The effect is smaller for real estate returns
```
because volatility is lower than in private equity, and studies often include downturns such
as what happened in real estate in the early 1990s and the early 2000s.
Lower reported variance of returns than the true variance of returns because only high
```
returns are counted (i.e., underestimated risk).
```
In sum, sample selection bias results in overestimated expected returns and underestimated
```
risk as measured by beta and the standard deviation of returns (i.e., volatility).
```
Infrequent Trading
Illiquid assets, by definition, trade infrequently. Infrequent trading results in
underestimated risk. Betas, return volatilities, and correlations are too low when they are
computed using the reported returns of infrequently traded assets. Returns of these
infrequently traded assets are smoothed. For example, if one compares quarterly returns to
```
the daily returns of the same asset, quarterly returns will appear (and actually be) less
```
volatile. Prices will often be higher or lower in a given investment horizon than it appears
when examining quarterly returns. The computed standard deviation of returns often will be
lower when examining quarterly returns compared to daily returns. Also, correlations with
```
other asset classes (e.g., liquid assets such as large-cap stocks) will be artificially low because
```
return volatility is muted by infrequent trades.
It is possible to unsmooth or de-smooth returns using filtering algorithms. Filtering
algorithms generally remove noise from signals. However, unsmoothing adds noise back to
reported returns to uncover the true, noisier returns. Unsmoothing returns affects risk and
return estimates, and could have a dramatic effect on returns. For example, reported real
estate returns during the 1990s downturn were –5.3%. The corresponding unsmoothed
```
returns were –22.6%. The National Council of Real Estate Investment Fiduciaries (NCREIF)
```
returns reached –8.3% in December 2008. Unsmoothed returns during the same quarter
were –36.3%. The standard deviation of the raw returns was 2.25% during the same quarter
compared to 6.26% for unsmoothed returns. For comparison, stock return volatility was
approximately 7.5% per quarter. Correlations between the S&P 500 Index and NCREIF
returns increased from 9.2% to 15.8% when returns were unsmoothed.
MODULE QUIZ 99.1
1. Global liquidity crises generally occur because:
A. governments choose not to engage in monetary policy actions to stimulate economies.
B. financial distress causes markets to freeze.
C. markets for illiquid assets shrink, causing liquidity issues to infect traditional asset classes.
D. transaction costs increase as developing economies get stronger.
2. When an investor has difficulty finding a counterparty for a complicated credit product like a
structured debt instrument, this is known as:
A. market participation costs.
B. agency costs.
C. search frictions.
D. selection bias.
3. Blue Sky Funds, a private equity fund, has suffered low returns for the last five years. As a result,
the find has decided to quit reporting returns. The fund did report returns each year for the last
10 years when performance was strong. This problem of reporting leads to:
A. survivorship bias.
B. sample selection bias.
C. infrequent trading bias.
D. attrition bias.
MODULE 99.2: ILLIQUIDITY RISK PREMIUMS AND PORTFOLIO
ALLOCATION TO ILLIQUID ASSETS
Illiquidity Risk Premiums
LO 99.e: Compare illiquidity risk premiums across and within asset categories.
Illiquidity Risk Premiums Across Asset Classes
As part of the analysis in Antti Ilmanen’s 2011 bookExpected Returns,1 we can relate liquidity
to expected returns as shown in Figure 99.1. Note, however, that we cannot completely
```
pigeonhole asset classes based on illiquidity (e.g., some private equity funds are more liquid
```
```
than some hedge funds or infrastructure investments). Also note that, in this analysis, returns
```
are computed over the period 1990 to 2009 and the illiquidity estimates are just estimates
```
(i.e., they represent Ilmanen’s opinions). Ilmanen’s work does imply a positive relationship
```
between the illiquidity of an asset class and its expected return. Venture capital is considered
the least liquid and has the highest expected return, between 16% and 17%. Buyout funds
and timber are also illiquid but command lower expected returns, approximately 13% and
close to 12%, respectively. Hedge funds are more liquid and are expected to earn a little
more than 12%. Real estate is on par with hedge funds in terms of liquidity but commands a
lower return of nearly 8%. Equities are much more liquid and earned a bit more than 4%
over the period. Cash is the most liquid, and it too earned a little over 4% during the period.
Figure 99.1: Liquidity vs. Expected Returns
It is the conventional view that there is a premium for illiquidity. However, this may not be
true. First, there are illiquidity biases. As discussed previously, reported returns of illiquid
```
assets are too high (i.e., overstated if using raw, unsmoothed data) and risk and correlation
```
estimates are too low.
Second, illiquid asset classes such as private equity, buyout funds, and physical assets like
timber contain significant risks beyond liquidity risk. After adjusting for these risks, illiquid
asset classes are much less attractive. According to one study, after adjusting for risk, most
investors are better off investing in the S&P 500 than in a portfolio of private equity.
Third, there is no “market index” for illiquid assets. Private equity, hedge fund, and real estate
indices are not investable, so no investor is actually earning the index return. For example,
the NCREIF includes thousands of properties. Because individuals do not own thousands of
properties, they are much more subject to idiosyncratic risks and are less diversified within
the asset class.
Fourth, you must rely on manager skill in illiquid asset classes. There is no way, as there is
```
with tradeable, cheap bond and equity index funds, to separate factor risk (i.e., systematic
```
```
risk) from the talents of fund managers. As noted, there is no way to earn index returns. If
```
an investor cannot earn index returns in illiquid asset class markets, he has no way of
separating passive returns from alpha generated by active managers.
These factors imply that it may not be possible to generate substantial illiquidity risk
premiums across illiquid asset classes. However, there is evidence of large illiquidity risk
premiums within asset classes.
Illiquidity Risk Premiums Within Asset Classes
Less liquid assets generally have higher returns than more liquid assets, within asset classes.
Currently, there is no formal theory about why illiquidity risk premiums exist within asset
classes but not between. It might be that investors simply overpay for illiquid asset classes,
chasing the illusion of higher returns. It may also be that firms do not manage portfolios as a
```
cohesive whole, but instead put asset classes in different silos. Mispricing (i.e., the lack of a
```
```
premium across classes) may be due to slow-moving capital across classes, limits to
```
```
arbitrage, and institutional constraints (e.g., the fixed-income desk doesn’t talk to the equity
```
```
traders, and so on).
```
Illiquidity Effects in U.S. Treasury Markets
```
On-the-run (i.e., newly issued) Treasury bills (T-bills) are more liquid and have lower yields
```
```
than off-the-run (seasoned) T-bills. The difference is called the on-the-run/off-the-run bond
```
spread. During the 2007–2009 financial crisis, same maturity T-bonds and T-notes traded
with different yields. While prices should have been the same, T-bond prices were more than
5% lower than T-note prices. Given that the U.S. Treasury market is one of the largest and
most liquid in the world, it is surprising to observe large illiquidity effects.
Illiquidity Effects in Corporate Bond Markets
Larger bid-ask spreads and infrequent trading led to higher yields in corporate bond markets.
Studies indicate that illiquidity risk explains 7% of the variation in investment grade bond
yields and 22% of the variation in junk bond yields. Also, as bid-ask spreads increase, yield
```
spreads increase by more than double the amount (e.g., a one-basis point increase in the
```
```
bid-ask spread results in a more than two-basis point increase in the yield spread).
```
Illiquidity Effects in Equity Markets
There are several variables related to illiquidity that are shown to impact equity returns.
Studies indicate that less liquid stocks earn higher returns than more liquid stocks. Illiquidity
factors that impact equity returns are:
Bid-ask spreads.
Volume.
Turnover.
Volume measured by whether the trade was initiated by buyers or sellers.
Ratio of absolute returns to dollar volume, called the “Amihud measure.”
Price impact of large trades.
```
Informed trading measures (i.e., adverse selection).
```
Quote size.
Quote depth.
Frequency of trades.
```
Number of “zero” returns (in liquid markets returns are usually not zero).
```
```
Return autocorrelations (which are a measure of stale prices).
```
All of these factors are characteristics of illiquidity that are unique to each stock. There are
also illiquidity risk betas that are covariances of stock returns with illiquidity factors.
Researchers estimate illiquidity risk premiums at 1% to 8% depending on the illiquidity
measure used. Research also indicates that risk premiums have declined, although studies
find a 1% risk premium for listed equities compared to a 20% risk premium for OTC stocks.
Secondary Markets for Private Equity and Hedge Funds
Private equity funds trade companies with each other, providing needed liquidity. In 2005,
secondary buyouts represented about 15% of all private-equity buyout deals. This does allow
```
funds to get out of specific deals, may give limited partners (LPs) some cash in the process,
```
and may allow LPs to better understand the values of portfolio companies. However,
secondary buyouts do not allow limited partners to get out of the private equity fund itself.
LPs can exit private equity funds in secondary markets. However, these markets are
immature, small, and more opaque. Firms participating in these markets on the buy side were
called vultures in the 1990s. Buyers took advantage of distressed sellers, getting discounts of
30% to 50%. Discounts fell below 20% in the early 2000s, but shot up again during the
2007–2009 financial crisis.
Harvard University saw its endowment fall by more than $8 billion, or 22%, between July 1,
2008, and October 31, 2008. Harvard relies on the endowment for some of its operating
funds. Endowment fund managers attempted to sell stakes in private equity to free up cash
for operations and faced discounts of 50%.
Because hedge fund investors can typically redeem their investments at predetermined dates,
discounts on secondary market transactions are much smaller than in private equity
investments. During the 2007–2009 financial crisis, hedge fund discounts were 6% to 8% on
```
average. Some funds traded at a premium, even during the crisis, due to strong demand (i.e.,
```
```
the funds were closed to new investors). Large asset owners like sovereign funds and
```
pension funds can supply liquidity in hedge fund and private equity markets, buying stakes at
reduced prices and harvesting illiquidity risk premiums.
In sum, there are four ways that investors can harvest illiquidity premiums:
1. Allocating a portion of the portfolio to illiquid asset classes like real estate. This ispassive
allocation to illiquid asset classes.
2. Choosing more illiquid assets within an asset class. This means engaging inliquidity
security selection.
3. Acting as amarket maker for individual securities. For example, Dimensional Fund Advisors
```
(DFA) is a liquidity provider that buys stock at a discount from those wanting to sell quickly
```
and sells small-cap stocks at a premium to investors demanding shares. The firm avoids
adverse selection problems by choosing counterparties who fully disclose information about
stocks. The firm is trustworthy in its dealings and does not manipulate prices or engage in
front running. Sovereign wealth funds, large pension funds, and other large asset owners
can also act as market makers, providing liquidity while buying at discounts and selling at
premiums.
4. Engaging indynamic factor strategies at the aggregate portfolio level. This means taking
long positions in illiquid assets and short positions in liquid assets to harvest the illiquidity
risk premium. Investors rebalance to take advantage of the liquidity differences as less
liquid assets become more liquid. Rebalancing the portfolio is the simplest way to provide
liquidity. As long as buyers buy when others want to sell and sell when others want to buy,
rebalancing is countercyclical. Of the four ways investors can harvest the illiquidity
premium, this is the easiest to implement and can have the greatest effect on portfolio
returns.
Portfolio Allocation to Illiquid Assets
LO 99.f: Evaluate the impact of allocating illiquid assets to a portfolio, including
the impact on rebalancing and trading and on optimizing the proportion of illiquid
assets.
In determining the portfolio allocation to illiquid asset classes, or any asset class for that
matter, investors must consider their personal circumstances. The illiquid asset allocation
decision is influenced by different investment horizons, the lack of tradeable indices, the need
to hire talented active portfolio managers, and the need to monitor those managers. Portfolio
choice models that include illiquid assets must consider two important aspects of illiquidity
that impact investors:
1. Long time horizons between trades (i.e., infrequent trading).
2. Large transaction costs.
Asset Allocation to Illiquid Asset Classes With Transaction Costs
The primary issue with asset allocation models that include transaction costs is that they
assume an asset will always trade if the counterparty pays the transaction cost. However, this
is not true in private equity, infrastructure, real estate, and timber markets. It may not be
possible to find a buyer quickly, or a buyer may not be available at all. Counterparties, if
identified, must perform due diligence, which takes time. In some cases, the counterparty,
upon completion of due diligence, chooses not to buy the asset. In periods of stress, even
liquid asset classes face liquidity freezes, and it becomes impossible to find buyers at any
price.
Asset Allocation to Illiquid Asset Classes With Infrequent Trading
As anyone trying to sell in a period of illiquidity knows, one cannot “eat” illiquid assets.
Consider the example of Harvard University, briefly described earlier. The only way the
university could generate cash for operations in a period of significant losses and illiquidity
```
across what some would consider some of the most liquid assets (i.e., commercial paper and
```
```
repurchase agreements), Harvard would have had to sell at huge discounts. Only liquid assets
```
can be consumed. As a result, illiquidity has a major effect on investors’ portfolio choices.
Illiquidity causes the following with respect to portfolio choice:
Reduces optimal holdings. The less frequently a liquidity event is expected to occur, the
lower the allocation to the illiquid asset class.
```
Rebalancing illiquid assets (i.e., when there is infrequent trading in the asset class) causes
```
allocations tovary significantly. The investor must wait until a liquidity event arrives. As
```
such, the allocation prior to a liquidity event (or during nonrebalancing periods) can vary
```
from too high to too low relative to the optimal allocation.
Investors cannot hedge against declining values when an asset cannot be traded. As a
result,illiquid asset investors must consume less than liquid asset investors to offset the
risk.
There are no illiquidity “arbitrages.” To construct an arbitrage, an asset must be
continuously traded. Illiquid assets are not continuously traded.
Due to infrequent trading, illiquid asset investors mustdemand an illiquidity risk premium.
The more frequently the asset is traded, the lower the premium. For example, one study
indicates that private equity investments generate returns 6% higher than public markets
to compensate investors for illiquidity.
The inclusion of illiquid assets in a portfolio is not as simple or desirable as it might seem.
The following points should be considered:
1. Studies show that illiquid assets do not deliver higher risk-adjusted returns.
2. Investors are subject to agency problems because they must rely on the talents and skills
```
of the manager. It is difficult to monitor external managers (e.g., private equity managers).
```
3. In many firms, illiquid assets are managed separately from the rest of the portfolio.
4. Illiquid asset investors face high idiosyncratic risks. There is no “market” portfolio of illiquid
assets. Recall the example of the NCREIF versus the individual investor. It is not possible
for most investors to hold thousands of properties, and small numbers of properties can
```
lead to undiversified, property-specific risks (but also returns, making illiquid assets
```
```
compelling to investors). Illiquid assets are compelling because:
```
– Illiquid asset markets are less efficient than stock and bond markets.
– There are large information asymmetries in illiquid asset markets.
– High transaction costs keep many investors out of the market.
– Management skill is crucial, and alpha opportunities are widely dispersed.
All of these factors suggest there are great opportunities for the skilled investor to profit
from investments in illiquid assets. Investors must have the skills and resources to find,
evaluate, and monitor illiquid asset opportunities. Endowments like Harvard, Yale, and
Stanford have the skills and resources. Unskilled investors, even those endowments at less
sophisticated, skilled, and connected schools, can lose big in illiquid asset markets.
MODULE QUIZ 99.2
1. Which of the following variables is not an illiquidity factor that affects equity returns?
A. Measures of adverse selection.
B. The number of recorded positive returns.
C. Turnover.
D. Volume.
2. Rick Fairview, a general partner and portfolio manager with Fairview Funds, is considering ways in
which his company can profit from illiquidity risk premiums. He has studied several alternative
methods for capturing illiquidity risk premiums. Which of the following strategies is most likely to
produce the greatest impact on portfolio performance?
A. Acting as a market maker for individual securities.
B. Choosing the most illiquid assets within an asset class, even if the asset class is generally
considered to be liquid.
C. Allocating a portion of a portfolio to illiquid asset classes.
D. Using dynamic factor strategies at the aggregate portfolio level.
KEY CONCEPTS
LO 99.a
There are four main characteristics that describe illiquid asset markets, including:
1. Most asset classes are illiquid, at least to some degree.
2. Markets for illiquid assets are large.
3. Illiquid assets comprise the bulk of most investors’ portfolios.
4. Liquidity dries up even in liquid asset markets.
LO 99.b
Market imperfections encourage illiquidity in asset markets. Specifically, market participation
```
costs (i.e., clientele effects) and transaction costs give rise to illiquidity. Some academic
```
```
models assume that all assets can be traded if one will pay the required (sometimes very
```
```
high) transaction cost. However, this is not necessarily true in illiquid asset markets. There
```
```
are search frictions (i.e., difficulties finding a counterparty and information asymmetries),
```
price impacts, and funding constraints that may prevent trades from occurring, no matter
how high the transaction cost.
LO 99.c
In general, investors should be skeptical of reported returns in illiquid asset markets as they
are generally overstated. There are reporting biases that result in artificially inflated returns.
The three main biases that impact reported illiquid asset returns are:
1. Survivorship bias: Poor performing funds often quit reporting results. Also, many poor
performing funds ultimately fail. Finally, some poor performing funds never begin reporting
returns because performance is weak. All of these factors lead to survivorship bias.
Survivorship bias leads to an overstatement of stated returns relative to true returns.
2. Selection bias: Asset values and returns tend to be reported when they are high. For
example, houses and office buildings typically are sold when values are high. These higher
selling prices are used to calculate returns. This results in sample selection bias, which
again leads to overstated returns.
3. Infrequent trading: Illiquid assets, by definition, trade infrequently. Infrequent trading
results in underestimated risk. Betas, return volatilities, and correlations are too low when
they are computed using the reported returns of infrequently traded assets.
LO 99.d
Unsmoothing adds noise back to reported returns to uncover the true, noisier returns. This
process affects risk and return estimates and could have a dramatic effect on returns.
LO 99.e
There is little evidence that there are large illiquidity risk premiums across asset classes.
However, there are large illiquidity risk premiums within asset classes.
There are four primary ways that investors can harvest illiquidity premiums:
1. Allocating a portion of the portfolio to illiquid asset classes like real estate. This is passive
allocation to illiquid asset classes.
2. Choosing more illiquid assets within an asset class. This means engaging in liquidity
security selection.
3. Acting as a market maker for individual securities.
4. Engaging in dynamic factor strategies at the aggregate portfolio level. This means taking
long positions in illiquid assets and short positions in liquid assets to harvest the illiquidity
risk premium. Of the four ways investors can harvest illiquidity premiums, this is the easiest
to implement and can have the greatest effect on portfolio returns.
LO 99.f
There are several points to consider when deciding to allocate portfolio resources to illiquid
```
assets:
```
1. Studies show that illiquid assets do not deliver higher risk-adjusted returns.
2. Investors are subject to agency problems because they must rely on the talents and skills
of portfolio managers. It is difficult to monitor external managers.
3. In many firms, illiquid assets are managed separately from the rest of the portfolio.
4. Illiquid asset investors face high idiosyncratic risks. There is no “market” portfolio of illiquid
assets. Illiquid assets are compelling because illiquid asset markets are less efficient than
stock and bond markets, there are large information asymmetries in illiquid asset markets,
high transaction costs keep many investors out of the market, management skill is crucial,
and alpha opportunities are widely dispersed.
ANSWER KEY FOR MODULE QUIZZES
Module Quiz 99.1
1. B In stressed economic periods, such as during the 2007–2009 financial crisis, liquidity can
dry up. Major liquidity crises have occurred at least once every ten years across the
```
globe, in conjunction with downturns and financial distress. (LO 99.a)
```
2. C Difficulties finding a counterparty are called search frictions. For example, it may be
difficult to find someone to understand/purchase a complicated structured credit
product. It may also be difficult to find buyers with sufficient capital to purchase
multimillion-dollar office towers in major metropolitan areas. No matter how high the
transaction costs, it may take weeks, months, or years to transact in some situations.
Asymmetric information can also be a type of search friction as investors search for non-
```
predatory counterparties with which to transact. (LO 99.b)
```
3. A There are no requirements for certain types of funds, like private equity funds, to report
returns. As such, poorly performing funds tend to stop reporting. Additionally, many
poorly performing funds ultimately fail. Performance studies generally include only those
funds that were successful enough to survive over the entire period of analysis, leaving
out the returns of funds that no longer exist. Both of these factors result in reported
```
returns that are too high. This is called survivorship bias. (LO 99.c)
```
Module Quiz 99.2
1. B There are several variables related to illiquidity that are shown to impact equity returns.
They are bid-ask spreads, volume, turnover, volume measured by whether the trade was
initiated by buyers or sellers, the ratio of absolute returns to dollar volume, the price
```
impact of large trades, informed trading measures (i.e., adverse selection), quote size
```
and depth, the frequency of trades, the number of zero returns, and return
autocorrelations. It is the number of recorded zero returns, rather than positive returns,
```
that is relevant. (LO 99.e)
```
2. D There are four primary ways that investors can harvest illiquidity premiums:
```
Allocating a portion of the portfolio to illiquid asset classes like real estate (i.e.,
```
```
passive allocation to illiquid asset classes).
```
```
Choosing more illiquid assets within an asset class (i.e., liquidity security selection).
```
Acting as a market maker for individual securities.
Engaging in dynamic factor strategies at the aggregate portfolio level. This means
taking long positions in illiquid assets and short positions in liquid assets to harvest
the illiquidity risk premium. Of the four ways investors can harvest illiquidity risk
premiums, this is the easiest to implement and can have the greatest effect on
portfolio returns.
```
(LO 99.e)
```

The following is a review of the Current Issues in Financial Markets principles designed to address the learning objectives set
```
forth by GARP®. Cross-reference to GARP assigned reading—International Monetary Fund (IMF).
```

READING 100
ADVANCES IN ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE:
IMPLICATIONS FOR CAPITAL MARKETS
ACTIVITIES
Study Session 15
EXAM FOCUS
```
This reading focuses on artificial intelligence (AI) and its growing impact on participants and
```
regulatory bodies within the financial markets. While the benefits of AI can be substantial,
they are accompanied by heightened risks and the need for a more robust regulatory
framework. For the exam, understand both the current and potential future applications of
AI, as its growth continues to shape market dynamics and influence financial stability. Also,
```
be able to distinguish the key benefits (e.g., improved efficiency, liquidity, forecasting
```
```
accuracy) from the major risks (e.g., increased volatility, opacity, cyber vulnerabilities,
```
```
concentration risk). For supervisors, this means recognizing how AI is being used and how it
```
provides value. For regulators, this involves identifying high-risk areas that require attention
and determining where policy recommendations should be implemented.
MODULE 100.1: CURRENT AND FUTURE USES OF AI IN CAPITAL
MARKETS
```
LO 100.a: Describe current uses of artificial intelligence (AI) and machine learning
```
```
(ML) in capital markets, and potential future uses of sophisticated AI models
```
including GenAI.
Current Uses of AI and ML in Capital Markets
This reading examines the use of AI and ML in capital markets. The material is based on
feedback gathered through several surveys—most notably, an IMF staff market outreach
dedicated to AI. The objective of this initiative is to capture how financial institutions are
currently applying AI and ML technologies and how they expect their usage to grow in the
future.
The outreach included input from a wide range of market participants, such as banks and
dealers, asset managers, rating agencies, AI vendors, academic institutions, and market
infrastructure firms. In total, 27 firms formally responded to the survey, representing both
```
buy-side participants (e.g., mutual funds, hedge funds, asset managers, private equity firms,
```
```
pension funds, and institutional investors) and sell-side participants (e.g., brokerage firms,
```
```
research analysts, market makers, and investment banks).
```
For reference throughout this reading, AI models are grouped into two categories:
1. AI/ML models. These are established predictive analytics tools, such as natural language
```
processing (NLP), neural networks, decision trees, and clustering algorithms.
```
2. Sophisticated AI models. These are the most advanced innovations, including large
```
language models (LLMs), deep learning, and reinforcement learning techniques.
```
The use of AI and ML is expected to bring significant changes to capital market structures,
driven by faster execution speeds and stronger network effects. On the positive side, AI and
ML can enhance financial stability, efficiency, productivity, and forecasting accuracy, while also
refining investment frameworks and improving the quantification of market crash risk.
However, these technologies may also introduce new financial stability risks and amplify
existing concerns related to liquidity, leverage, and interconnectedness.
Four broad areas of risk include the following:
1. Increased operational risks
2. Greater market volatility and trading speed under stress
3. Heightened cyber and market manipulation risks
4. Challenges in monitoring and transparency (opacity)
```
Across the financial sector, generative AI (GenAI) is already being applied through use
```
cases that build on existing investment strategies and financial models. GenAI has enhanced
efficiency in several areas, including investment idea generation, code development for
analysts, and customer-facing activities. From a predictive standpoint, LLMs are increasingly
being integrated into analytical frameworks to support more accurate forecasting. Looking
ahead, potential benefits include lowering barriers to entry for less liquid asset classes and
accelerating market reactions to new information.
Respondents to the IMF survey emphasized the continued importance of human involvement
in AI-driven processes. They agreed that humans must remain “in the loop,” understand AI-
generated strategies, and actively participate in managing associated regulatory and ethical
risks. Respondents also noted that GenAI could deliver particular benefits to emerging and
developing economies by strengthening local financial markets and improving access to
credit. However, they cautioned that the high fixed costs of AI adoption may pose a
significant challenge for these markets.
Key categories, processes, and potential benefits of adopting AI within capital markets
include the following:
```
Institution/client profiling (identification of needs and constraints). Enhancing the
```
assessment of client profiles through more accurate identification of objectives,
preferences, and constraints.
```
Asset allocation (security selection, sector allocation, asset class allocation). Improving
```
analytical precision and forecasting techniques, optimizing strategic allocation, and deriving
actionable signals from alternative and unstructured data sources.
```
Trading (order placement and execution). Enhancing liquidity management and operational
```
efficiency, minimizing market impact, and supporting more effective price discovery.
```
Risk management (reporting and monitoring). Streamlining compliance monitoring,
```
generating tailored risk insights, developing advanced risk scenarios, and enabling dynamic
risk sensing.
Although AI and ML may seem relatively new, financial institutions have been using AI-driven
```
applications, such as robo-advisors and exchange-traded funds (ETFs) powered by AI, for
```
```
quite some time. While assets under management (AUM) in robo-advisory services have
```
grown significantly in recent years, AI-powered ETFs, in which AI systems build and adjust
portfolios, still represent a relatively small share of the overall market.
Emerging Trends and Future Applications
Although advancements in computing hardware and algorithmic efficiency have reduced the
unit costs of training AI models, the increasing complexity of these models has driven up
overall costs. The high fixed costs associated with both infrastructure and specialized talent
required for AI implementation may lead to greater market concentration, with a few private-
sector developers dominating the space. Moreover, firms with extensive access to trading and
client data are more likely to maintain their competitive advantage and reinforce this
concentration. Similar patterns are evident at the country level, with North America clearly
leading in the development of foundational AI models.
Respondents to the IMF survey observed that although AI has been used for some time, the
pace of its adoption has accelerated markedly in recent years. The most promising areas for
value creation through AI lie in liquid asset classes traded in public markets, such as equities
and listed derivatives, with government bonds and foreign exchange also offering significant
opportunities. Because these markets are highly dynamic, AI systems can more readily learn
from and adapt to changing conditions. Looking ahead, the private credit and emerging
markets segments may also present new opportunities for value generation through AI.
Notably, the survey highlighted a strong correlation between market liquidity and AI adoption
among investment managers.
AI use cases identified in the survey span a wide range of applications, including the
development of forward-looking indicators, integration of alternative datasets, exploration of
new asset classes, and extraction of trading signals from data. Additional uses include
portfolio allocation and optimization, back-office operations, customer service functions,
pricing and forecasting, risk assessment, and enhancements to trade automation.
Respondents also cited applications such as code generation, prototyping, information
extraction, and summarization.
In the asset management space, one specific AI use case involves analyzing text and
alternative data to uncover causal relationships in previously unexplored markets. Another
application leverages more traditional AI and ML techniques to enhance the accuracy and
robustness of existing models. However, the use of AI in trading and investment decision
execution remains in its early stages. To date, sophisticated AI has been applied mainly to
generate signals that serve as inputs to existing systems, with human traders still making the
final decisions rather than relying on fully autonomous AI trading agents that operate without
human intervention.
```
According to IMF survey participants, the key benefits of AI and ML include (1) enhanced
```
```
productivity and efficiency, (2) improved processing and analysis of unstructured data, (3)
```
```
narrower bid-ask spreads, and (4) cost savings in the design and execution of trading
```
algorithms. From the perspective of financial supervisors, AI provides significant advantages
```
through the use of supervisory technology (SupTech) tools, which help monitor
```
institutions and markets, detect anomalies in large datasets, and support regulatory
```
compliance. Similarly, banks are increasingly adopting regulatory technology (RegTech)
```
solutions powered by AI to improve efficiency and strengthen compliance management.
Looking ahead, AI is expected to play an increasingly significant role in investment and
```
trading activities, with greater autonomy in AI-driven decision-making (particularly in markets
```
```
such as equities). Nonetheless, a “human-in-the-loop” approach is likely to remain in place,
```
ensuring that models continue to operate within established rules and under human
oversight.
One indicator of future trends in AI innovation is the growing number of AI-related patent
filings. There has been a significant rise in patents referencing AI and ML technologies in the
context of algorithmic and high-frequency trading. More recent filings have focused on
developing systems capable of computing trading signals with high throughput and low
latency, as well as enhancing the operational efficiency of trading and brokerage platforms.
Asset management patent filings have also increased in volume over the same period. Within
this category, many filings focus on applying ML techniques to automate asset class
rebalancing, determine capital requirements tailored to individual needs, enhance liquidity
management and cash flow efficiency, and improve forecasting and valuation methods.
Additional areas of innovation focus on developing systems that can process information from
alternative data sources and interpret unstructured data. Efforts are also directed toward
```
expanding access to (and improving the management of) alternative asset classes, including
```
digital assets, emissions trading, and signed transaction validation.
Broader Implications and Opportunities
From a human capital perspective, there is growing evidence of an expanding pool of AI-
skilled professionals within the financial services industry. Demand for this expertise continues
to rise, creating a highly competitive environment for attracting and retaining talent. Job
postings requiring AI-related skills have also increased, particularly for roles directly
influencing financial market transactions and investment decisions.
As noted earlier, AI has the potential to create greater financial opportunities for emerging
and developing markets by lowering barriers to entry, enhancing liquidity, increasing capital
flows, and stimulating investment. These benefits may manifest through improvements in
financial services access, loan origination, portfolio construction, credit scoring, and robo-
advisory solutions. Enhanced liquidity and reduced investment barriers may also result from
GenAI’s ability to interpret and structure unorganized or fragmented data. Additionally, the
use of synthetic data generated by AI could prove valuable for training investment models in
markets where data availability is limited.
```
There are two key considerations when relying on synthetic (i.e., generated) data: (1) the
```
```
potential for biases to be amplified by the data, and (2) the risk of overrepresenting or
```
underrepresenting certain values within real-world data distributions. It is also important to
recognize potential differences among emerging markets, particularly between larger and
smaller economies. Although survey respondents acknowledged the risk of automating lower-
skilled jobs, they also expressed optimism about AI’s potential to promote greater financial
inclusion, expand access to credit, and lower barriers to entry through applications such as
robo-advisors.
MODULE QUIZ 100.1
1. Value creation through the use of AI is most likely to occur in which of the following types of
investments?
A. Antiques.
B. Futures contracts.
C. Private equity funds.
D. Commercial real estate.
2. Which of the following benefits did IMF survey respondents specifically identify regarding the use
of AI and ML?
A. Wider bid-ask spreads.
B. Relatively low fixed costs.
C. Improved processing of structured data.
D. Cost savings in trading algorithm design.
MODULE 100.2: IMPLICATIONS FOR MARKET DYNAMICS AND
FINANCIAL STABILITY
Impact of AI on Market Dynamics
LO 100.b: Explain the implications of further adoption of AI on market dynamics.
AI Adoption and Shifts in Market Structure
The incorporation of AI into capital market activities is expected to influence both market
dynamics and overall structure. AI adoption may also facilitate the growth of nonbank
```
financial institutions (NBFIs). Given that NBFIs hold more than half of global financial market
```
assets, their agility and relatively less restrictive operating environments position them to
benefit significantly from AI integration. In contrast, larger banks may face greater challenges
due to legacy infrastructure and stricter regulatory constraints.
A major area of growth in advanced economies is algorithmic trading. In the United States
alone, algorithmic trading accounts for roughly 70% of equities trading, over 50% of futures
trading, and more than 40% of options trading, while representing less than 10% of fixed-
income trading. Markets such as the United States, Europe, and Asia, where algorithmic
trading is most prevalent, tend to have activity concentrated among a relatively small number
of dominant participants. The high fixed costs associated with developing and deploying AI
systems mean that larger firms benefit most from AI implementation, while smaller firms
often need to outsource their AI capabilities.
Algorithmic Trading and Market Implications
Algorithmic trading provides significant benefits through AI’s ability to extract value from, and
rapidly process, massive quantities of unstructured and high-frequency data. Potential
outcomes from the use of algorithmic trading include the following:
Market efficiency and liquidity. AI can have continued positive effects on market efficiency
and liquidity. However, it also carries the risk of increased volatility during periods of stress.
Such volatility may arise after negative economic news, when informed traders withdraw
from the market, reducing efficiency. Liquidity can also deteriorate during stressed
conditions if high-frequency traders step back, which has been observed in markets such
as U.S. Treasuries and other bond markets.
Price behavior. Over the past two decades, evidence suggests that algorithmic trading has
helped reduce price swings unrelated to new information during normal liquidity conditions.
Conversely, it has also been associated with occasional large price swings in illiquid or
stressed environments.
Market stability and risk limits. Risk controls associated with algorithmic trading can
sometimes reduce market stability under stress. Protective mechanisms such as risk
reduction and automatic shutdowns during high-volatility periods may create feedback
loops, trigger trading limits, and drain liquidity. Research indicates that during such periods,
high-frequency traders reduce their order cancellations as they step back from the market,
while institutional investors increase their use of hidden orders, which are orders that are
partially or fully concealed from the order book in an effort to limit price impact. This
behavior reflects a pattern known as “flighty liquidity under stress.”
Impact of GenAI. The integration of GenAI into algorithmic trading could bring substantial
benefits by lowering barriers to entry through improved testing, coding, and trade
automation. GenAI may also help overcome challenges in automating trading across
diverse asset classes by processing complex text-based data and enabling more
standardized risk analyses.
Figure 100.1 summarizes the potential positive and negative scenarios associated with
adopting AI in algorithmic trading.
Figure 100.1: Potential Impacts of AI Adoption in Algorithmic Trading
Emerging Market Dynamics and Financial Stability Risks
New market dynamics and potential financial stability risks may also emerge as AI adoption
expands. These include the following:
Faster market reactions to news. Reviews of market responses to the release of Federal
```
Open Market Committee (FOMC) minutes indicate that initial reactions now more
```
accurately reflect the eventual market impact compared with the period before the
introduction of LLMs.
Higher and procyclical trading volumes from AI-driven strategies. The ability of AI to rapidly
process and act on new information can lead to more frequent and larger portfolio
adjustments, resulting in higher trading volumes. Comparisons of ETFs using AI-driven
strategies with those following other active or passive strategies show significantly higher
turnover among AI-based funds. While elevated trading volume can support price discovery
in stable markets, it may amplify volatility and instability in stressed conditions.
Potential for AI-driven manipulation or collusion. Theoretical models simulating interactions
among AI trading algorithms suggest the possibility of market inefficiencies, such as
persistent price gaps between fundamental values and market prices—particularly when
one algorithm holds a strategic advantage over others. These conditions could create
opportunities for manipulation or even unintended collusion among AI systems.
Impact of AI on Financial Stability
LO 100.c: Explain the implications of further adoption of AI on financial stability.
Financial Stability Risks and Vulnerabilities
Several areas of concern regarding the potential impact of AI on financial stability were
highlighted by participants in the IMF outreach survey. These include the following:
Concentration risk. Concentration risk was identified as a principal concern, particularly
if investment and trading strategies are built primarily on open-source AI models trained
using data from a limited group of vendors. Vendor concentration was also noted as a
vulnerability, as a small number of dominant providers could create systemic risk if any
were to fail in the future.
Market fragility. With market fragility, risks such as excess volatility, flash crashes,
illiquidity, cyberattacks, and large-scale data poisoning could arise from ineffective
guardrails and rapid, automated decision-making. Respondents also expressed concern
about the availability of professionals with the necessary expertise to operate effectively in
AI-driven environments.
Model hallucinations and lack of explainability. These issues could undermine market
confidence in AI-generated outputs, making it more difficult for participants to rely on
automated systems.
Market manipulation. The potential misuse of AI for misinformation campaigns or deepfake
generation could disrupt market integrity and investor trust.
High fixed costs. The substantial infrastructure and resource requirements associated with
AI implementation may give large firms a competitive advantage, further entrenching
concentration risks and market imbalances.
Note that with market fragility risks, cyberattacks in particular were identified as a
significant vulnerability, with the frequency and sophistication of such attacks increasing in
recent years. Threats include audio and video impersonations, system disruptions, deepfakes,
and the spread of misinformation through social media and other channels. These threats
could erode confidence in financial institutions, potentially triggering market selloffs and/or
deposit runs. Since advanced economies have been the earliest and heaviest adopters of AI,
most reported cyber incidents have occurred within these markets.
Lesser concerns identified by survey participants included issues related to data access and
unauthorized use, the potential for customer fraud, and transmitting economic and market
shocks from advanced economies to emerging and developing markets, which could heighten
capital flow volatility.
Regulatory Responses and Systemic Implications
Survey participants also expressed an expectation that regulatory authorities would provide
greater guidance and clarity on AI-related risk management, including requirements for
transparent disclosures and the implementation of extreme-scenario stress testing.
Respondents emphasized the importance of establishing accountability frameworks and
offering guidance on both the use of AI in customer-facing applications and the development
of regulatory structures across different industries. They highlighted the need to balance
innovation, customer protection, and responsible AI governance. Overall, participants viewed
best practices and flexible guidelines as more effective for supporting the evolving nature of
AI in financial markets than strict, rules-based regulations.
Financial stability challenges arising from the adoption of AI in capital markets include the
```
following:
```
Higher volatility and increased market speed during periods of stress. While efficiency
gains, faster price adjustments, narrower margins, and greater liquidity can result from the
growth of algorithmic trading strategies, the potential for higher leverage could amplify
volatility and deleveraging during market stress. Herding behavior and similar outputs
across AI models under stressed conditions may collectively trigger a flight to safe assets
and large-scale selloffs in other asset classes. Moreover, AI models may shut down or
generate complex and inconsistent outputs during significant adverse events, such as the
COVID-19 pandemic.
Greater monitoring and opacity challenges. As AI adoption expands, capital market,
trading, and investment activities are increasingly shifting from the banking sector to
NBFIs. While banks are subject to stringent transparency and model validation
requirements, NBFIs operate under relatively lighter oversight, which could lead to reduced
transparency. Additionally, increased correlations and interconnectedness across markets
may emerge as AI-generated portfolios link geographic regions, trading venues, and asset
classes in ways that have not existed previously.
Elevated cyber risks and potential market manipulation. Financial markets and asset prices
could become targets for manipulation through disinformation, deepfakes, or fraudulent
activity. AI models themselves may also be exposed to compromised data integrity or
confidentiality, leading to flawed investment and trading decisions.
Operational risks from dependence on third-party AI service providers. An overreliance on a
small number of AI model and IT service providers could heighten systemic vulnerability.
The failure or disruption of any major provider could have severe and widespread
consequences for capital markets.

2. During periods of high volatility or market stress, algorithmic trading activity often exhibits which

B. Higher order cancellations and lower hidden order rates.

D. Higher order cancellations and higher hidden order rates.
3. Which of the following statements best reflects the expectations that IMF survey respondents had

B. Strict, rules-based regulations are needed rather than flexible guidelines.
C. Authorities should emphasize the need for extreme-scenario stress testing.
D. Risk management for AI models should be left to individual firms’ discretion.
