MODULE 100.3: REGULATORY AND SUPERVISORY
CONSIDERATIONS
LO 100.d: Describe best practices for regulators pertaining to AI, including the use
of AI by supervisors and recommendations for policy on the use of AI by regulated
entities.
The regulatory and supervisory frameworks that govern capital markets also extend to the
use of AI systems. However, it is important to recognize that institutions remain fully
responsible for the AI systems they deploy, regardless of whether those systems are
developed internally or obtained from external providers.
Best Practices and Supervisory Use of AI
Standard-setting bodies expect financial sector authorities to remain attentive to the growing
deployment of AI in capital markets—and to be prepared for the continued acceleration of AI
adoption across the industry. Supervisory authorities should ensure that their skills and tools
are capable of handling large volumes of detailed data and monitoring increasingly complex
investment strategies. Existing regulatory frameworks must also be adaptable to ongoing AI
developments. Key areas of focus for standard-setting bodies in responding to AI applications
include governance of AI use, transparency and explainability, outsourcing practices, data
quality and bias, reporting requirements, and maintaining meaningful human oversight.
```
Entities such as the Financial Stability Board (FSB), the Basel Committee on Banking
```
```
Supervision (BCBS), the Bank for International Settlements (BIS), the U.S. National Institute
```
```
of Standards and Technology (NIST), the International Organization of Securities
```
```
Commissions (IOSCO), and other national regulators have taken steps to strengthen investor
```
protection, maintain market integrity, and safeguard financial stability. These efforts build on
existing regulatory frameworks and, in some cases, introduce new ones, such as the NIST’s
2024 AI framework. IOSCO, in particular, has focused on addressing market volatility
associated with algorithmic trading, as well as AI-related risks in market intermediation and
asset management. In parallel, many governments have begun developing broader AI
strategies emphasizing cybercrime prevention, data protection, and sound AI governance.
Recommendations to support the safe adoption of AI include evaluating the adequacy of
existing regulatory frameworks, developing new risk management frameworks, and
advancing principles for AI governance through public-private collaboration. When reviewing
current frameworks, it is essential to assess whether they sufficiently address key
considerations, such as robustness, explainability, cybersecurity, and data bias and privacy.
For supervisors, the advantages of AI include efficiency gains through automated data quality
checks, integration of multiple data sources, and the detection of trading pattern anomalies.
Additional applications, such as real-time market monitoring and the identification of
misleading information, can further enhance supervisory effectiveness. GenAI offers even
greater potential value by enabling legacy code optimization, content creation, improved
information retrieval, and advanced capabilities in code generation, debugging, and
explanation.
Policy Recommendations and Regulatory Priorities
Given the financial stability risks faced by both the banking and NBFI sectors, regulatory and
supervisory frameworks for AI should be strengthened to balance the realization of benefits
with the need for enhanced risk management. Key policy recommendations and risk-mapping
priorities include improving cyber resilience and addressing dependencies on models, data,
and third-party service providers. Policymakers should also focus on mitigating market
```
volatility, particularly in over-the-counter (OTC) markets, and enhancing the quality and
```
timeliness of regulatory reporting.
The following areas have been identified as key priorities to address:
Market speed increases and volatility under stress. To respond effectively to market crashes
or dislocations caused by AI-driven trading, it may be necessary to modify existing volatility
response mechanisms or develop new ones. Authorities should assess the quality of circuit
breakers, conduct algorithm testing in both controlled and stressed environments, and
evaluate the adequacy of margin requirements and other buffers during large price swings.
Based on proposals from IOSCO, the Basel Committee, and the BIS, further work is needed
in reviewing initial margin levels during normal conditions, margin call volatility, variation
margin collections and distributions, and the responsiveness of central counterparties’
margin models during periods of market stress.
Heightened operational risk due to reliance on a small number of third-party AI service
providers. Financial sector authorities should map the interconnections between critical IT
infrastructure providers and major AI service providers, as disruptions or failures in these
entities could have widespread market consequences. Capital markets must be resilient—
not only by preventing cyberattacks where possible, but also by being able to protect
against, respond to, and recover from attacks when they occur. AI systems are particularly
vulnerable to attacks targeting model training data or the models themselves, highlighting
the need to evaluate cybersecurity safeguards at every stage of the AI lifecycle.
Increased opacity and monitoring challenges. Financial institutions should be required to
map interdependencies among AI models, data sources, and underlying technological
infrastructure. Risks arise when data fails to capture a full financial cycle, or when it
depends on a small number of external providers. NBFIs should be required to identify
themselves and disclose AI-related information. While large traders have always been a
regulatory focus, their importance increases in an AI-driven landscape. Strengthening
liquidity buffers and enhancing risk management could improve NBFI resilience, mitigating
the risk of liquidity runs and asset mispricing.
OTC market resilience and monitoring needs. As AI use expands, maintaining the integrity,
efficiency, and resilience of OTC markets becomes essential. Authorities could require the
collection and dissemination of detailed information on OTC transactions by market
participants. Potential policy options include adjusting liquidity frameworks, expanding
incentives for central clearing, enhancing liquidity provisions by market makers, and
introducing margin requirements for non-centrally cleared derivatives. Backstop measures
to provide liquidity and funding should also be developed to support markets during
periods of extreme stress.
MODULE QUIZ 100.3
1. As standard-setting bodies develop responses to the growing use of AI in financial markets, which
of the following areas is most accurately identified as a key focus?
A. Human oversight is becoming unnecessary as AI models improve.
B. Transparency and explainability are essential areas that must be addressed.
C. Outsourced AI models are inherently riskier than those developed internally.
D. Reporting requirements should gradually be reduced to streamline compliance.
2. To effectively assess increases in market speed and volatility during periods of stress, which of
the following types of margin should be evaluated during non-stress periods?
A. Margin calls.
B. Initial margin.
C. Variation margin.
D. Maintenance margin.
KEY CONCEPTS
LO 100.a
```
Artificial intelligence (AI) and machine learning (ML) have the potential to enhance financial
```
stability, efficiency, productivity, and forecasting accuracy, while also refining investment
frameworks and improving market crash risk quantification. However, the adoption of these
```
technologies introduces four broad categories of risk: (1) increased operational risks, (2)
```
```
greater market volatility and trading speed under stress, (3) heightened cyber and market
```
```
manipulation risks, and (4) challenges in monitoring and transparency.
```
Key categories, processes, and potential benefits of adopting AI within capital markets
include the following functional areas:
Institution and client profiling can be strengthened through more accurate assessments of
client needs and constraints.
Asset allocation benefits from improved analytical precision, enhanced forecasting
techniques, strategic optimization, and the ability to derive signals from alternative and
unstructured data.
In trading, AI supports better liquidity and execution management, minimizes market
impact, and aids in more effective price discovery.
In risk management, AI facilitates compliance monitoring, generates customized insights,
supports advanced scenario analysis, and enables dynamic risk sensing.
The most promising areas for AI-driven value creation lie in liquid public markets, such as
equities and listed derivatives, with government bonds and foreign exchange also offering
significant opportunities. According to the IMF survey, participants identified several key
benefits of AI and ML, including improved productivity and efficiency, enhanced processing of
unstructured data, narrower bid-ask spreads, and cost savings in trading algorithm design.
Innovation in AI continues to accelerate, reflected in the notable increase in patent filings
referencing AI and ML in the context of algorithmic and high-frequency trading. Patent
activity in asset management has also grown, focusing on automation, liquidity management,
and forecasting improvements. From a human capital perspective, the financial services
industry is experiencing rapid growth in the AI-skilled workforce, with rising demand and
intense competition for this expertise. Finally, AI may generate greater financial opportunities
for emerging and developing markets by lowering barriers to entry, enhancing liquidity,
increasing capital flows, and expanding access to credit and investment opportunities.
LO 100.b
The further adoption of AI is expected to reshape market dynamics and the structure of
```
capital markets. AI models may facilitate the growth of nonbank financial institutions (NBFIs),
```
whose agility and relatively fewer operational constraints give them a competitive advantage
over larger, more regulated banks. In markets such as the United States, Europe, and Asia,
where algorithmic trading is widely used, activity tends to be concentrated among a small
number of participants. The high fixed costs associated with developing and implementing AI
systems mean that larger firms benefit the most from these technologies, while smaller firms
often rely on outsourcing their AI capabilities.
Algorithmic trading, a key area of AI application, has produced several notable outcomes.
These include continued improvements in market efficiency and liquidity, reduced volatility
```
during normal trading conditions (but heightened volatility under stressed environments), and
```
shifts in trading behavior during periods of market stress, such as fewer order cancellations
by high-frequency traders and greater use of hidden orders by institutional investors. In
```
addition, AI and generative AI (GenAI) are reducing barriers to entry by streamlining testing,
```
coding, and automation, which may help expand automated trading across a wider range of
asset classes.
However, new market dynamics and potential financial stability risks have also emerged
alongside greater AI adoption. These include faster market reactions to news, higher and
more procyclical trading volumes driven by rapid AI-based portfolio adjustments, and the
potential for algorithmic interactions that could lead to market manipulation or even
unintentional collusion.
LO 100.c
IMF survey respondents identified several significant areas of concern regarding the impact of
AI on financial stability. These include concentration risk, market fragility, model
hallucinations, lack of explainability, market manipulation, and high fixed costs—all of which
could amplify systemic vulnerabilities within capital markets. Lesser concerns cited by
participants involved data access and unauthorized use, customer fraud, and the potential for
economic and market shocks originating in advanced economies to transmit to emerging and
developing markets, increasing capital flow volatility.
Respondents also expressed strong expectations for regulatory authorities to provide clear
guidance on AI-related risk management and accountability frameworks. They emphasized
the importance of maintaining a balance between fostering innovation, protecting customers,
and implementing appropriate oversight, given the rapid pace of technological change.
Financial stability challenges arising from AI adoption include heightened volatility and faster
market reactions during periods of stress, increased opacity and monitoring difficulties as
activity shifts toward NBFIs, elevated cyber and market manipulation risks, and greater
operational vulnerabilities stemming from heavy reliance on a limited number of third-party
AI service providers.
LO 100.d
Institutions remain responsible for the AI systems they use, whether developed internally or
sourced externally. However, standard-setting bodies also play a critical role in shaping sound
governance and oversight practices. Key areas of focus for these entities include AI use
governance, transparency and explainability, outsourcing arrangements, data quality and
bias, reporting requirements, and maintaining meaningful human oversight.
```
Global entities such as the Financial Stability Board (FSB), the Basel Committee on Banking
```
```
Supervision (BCBS), the Bank for International Settlements (BIS), the U.S. National Institute
```
```
of Standards and Technology (NIST), the International Organization of Securities
```
```
Commissions (IOSCO), and other national regulators have acted to strengthen investor
```
protection, preserve market integrity, and enhance financial stability. Their efforts build upon
existing frameworks while introducing new AI-specific guidance.
For supervisors, AI provides opportunities to improve efficiency through automated data
quality checks, integration of multiple data sources, anomaly detection, and real-time
monitoring of trading activity and potential misinformation. Policy development and risk-
mapping priorities emphasize strengthening cyber resilience and addressing dependencies on
models, data, and third-party service providers.
Key policy areas for regulators to address include managing market speed increases and
volatility during stress periods, mitigating operational risks stemming from a concentrated
pool of AI service providers, enhancing transparency and monitoring capabilities, and
```
ensuring resilience and oversight in over-the-counter (OTC) markets.
```
ANSWER KEY FOR MODULE QUIZZES
Module Quiz 100.1
1. B AI systems are most effective in markets characterized by high liquidity and dynamic
trading activity, where they can continuously learn from and adapt to changing
conditions. As a result, value creation through AI is more pronounced in liquid asset
```
classes such as listed derivatives (including futures contracts), equities, and government
```
bonds. In contrast, illiquid investments, such as antiques, private equity funds, and
commercial real estate, offer fewer opportunities for AI-driven insights and automation.
```
(LO 100.a)
```
2. D IMF survey respondents identified several key benefits of AI and ML, including improved
```
productivity and efficiency, enhanced processing ofunstructured data,narrower (more
```
```
compressed) bid-ask spreads, and cost savings in the design and execution of trading
```
algorithms. In contrast, AI and ML implementation typically involveshigh fixed costs.
```
(LO 100.a)
```
Module Quiz 100.2
1. C Algorithmic trading represents the highest share of activity in the U.S. equity market,
accounting for approximately 70% of total equities trading. By comparison, it accounts
for just over 50% of futures trading, more than 40% of options trading, and less than
```
10% of fixed-income trading. (LO 100.b)
```
2. C In periods of heightened volatility or market stress, order cancellations (typically used by
```
high-frequency traders) tend to decline as these participants reduce their activity. At the
```
same time, hidden order rates, which are commonly used by institutional investors to
minimize market impact during low-liquidity conditions, tend to increase. This
combination reflects the phenomenon of “flighty liquidity-under-stress,” where overall
market liquidity deteriorates despite the presence of sophisticated trading technology.
```
(LO 100.b)
```
3. C Survey respondents indicated that regulatory authorities should play an active role in
providing guidance and clarity on AI-related risk management. This includes
encouraging transparent disclosures, accountability frameworks, and extreme-scenario
stress testing. Respondents also viewed best practices and flexible guidelines as more
effective than strict, rules-based regulations, while stressing that both innovation and
```
customer protection are equally important priorities. (LO 100.c)
```
4. BConcentration risk refers to situations in which market activity, data, or technology
becomes dependent on a limited number of participants. In the context of AI, the
number of service providers capable of developing and maintaining large-scale models is
relatively small. The failure of any one of these firms could have widespread
repercussions for financial stability. By contrast,opacity risk relates to reduced
```
transparency in markets such as those dominated by NBFIs;market fragility risk involves
```
```
volatility, illiquidity, and cyberattacks during stress periods; andmarket manipulation risk
```
includes actions such as spreading misinformation and using deepfakes to influence
```
prices. (LO 100.c)
```
Module Quiz 100.3
1. B Transparency and explainability are among the primary areas of focus for standard-
setting bodies in developing guidance for AI use in financial markets. Other key focus
areas include governance, outsourcing, data quality, bias, and human oversight. There is
no indication that outsourced models are riskier than internally developed ones, and
maintaining human involvement remains critical. While reporting requirements may
increase administrative workload, they are essential for ensuring accountability and
```
should not be minimized. (LO 100.d)
```
2. B Initial margin levels during non-stress periods are important to evaluate, as they
establish the baseline for managing risk when markets experience stress. During volatile
conditions, margin call volatility and the collection and distribution of variation margin
become critical areas for review. Maintenance margin, by contrast, represents the
minimum account balance an investor must maintain and is not the primary focus of this
```
analysis. (LO 100.d)
```
The following is a review of the Current Issues in Financial Markets principles designed to address the learning objectives set
```
forth by GARP®. Cross-reference to GARP assigned reading—Financial Stability Board (FSB).
```
READING 101
THE FINANCIAL STABILITY IMPLICATIONS OF
ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE
Study Session 15
EXAM FOCUS
```
This reading explores the current and potential uses of artificial intelligence (AI) in the
```
```
financial sector, including tools such as generative AI (GenAI) and large language models
```
```
(LLMs), along with their associated benefits and risks. For the exam, understand how
```
```
developments since the foundational 2017 Financial Stability Board (FSB) report have
```
reshaped the landscape, paying particular attention to both supply-side and demand-side
drivers. Also, be able to identify the AI use cases already present across the industry, as well
as emerging applications in regulatory and supervisory contexts. Because the motivations for
AI innovation can conflict with financial stability objectives, it is important to recognize how
the vulnerabilities highlighted in this reading may translate into risks across the financial
system.
MODULE 101.1: RECENT AI DEVELOPMENTS: SUPPLY VS.
DEMAND DRIVERS
LO 101.a: Explain key developments in AI since 2017, differentiating between
supply-side drivers and demand-side drivers.
Overview of Recent AI Developments
```
Although artificial intelligence (AI) tools have long been used in the financial services
```
industry, adoption has accelerated in recent years, leading to a wide range of new
```
applications. These applications span operations (such as model risk management and capital
```
```
optimization), customer-focused activities (such as automated client interactions and credit
```
```
assessments), portfolio management, trading, supervisory technology (SupTech), and
```
```
regulatory technology (RegTech). Generative AI (GenAI) in particular offers notable
```
benefits in areas such as document processing, fraud detection, customer support,
information retrieval, software development, and market analysis. However, alongside these
advantages, AI also introduces meaningful risks and potential vulnerabilities across the
financial sector.
```
In 2017, the Financial Stability Board (FSB) issued a report on AI in the financial services
```
industry. Since then, major developments in big data, computational power, deep learning,
and GenAI have significantly advanced the field. Interest in AI has also grown considerably,
with the launch of ChatGPT in November 2022 serving as an important catalyst. The 2017
report examined both supply-side and demand-side factors expected to drive AI adoption in
financial services. Although both continue to matter today, evidence indicates that supply-side
forces have been the more influential driver.
Supply-Side Factors
The supply-side factors driving AI adoption include technological developments, data-related
developments, and business model developments.
Technological Developments
Advances in both software and hardware, along with the shift toward GenAI, have fueled
significant growth in AI adoption. Key developments include the following:
1. Improved ability to process unstructured data using embeddings (i.e., numerical
```
representations of data such as text, images, or audio), enabled by advances in deep
```
learning models
2. The emergence of the Transformer architecture, which revolutionized natural language
processing and laid the foundation for large language models and GenAI
3. The ability to process larger datasets and deploy more complex models at lower cost due
```
to increased computational power and broader use of graphics processing units (GPUs)
```
One ongoing challenge is the shortage of human capital able to keep pace with these rapid
technological advances, as workers with the necessary skills can be difficult and expensive to
hire. Figure 101.1 outlines key technological developments in AI.
Figure 101.1: AI Technological Developments
Data-Related Developments
A key driver of AI adoption has been the increasing level of digital engagement between
financial institutions and their customers, along with the rapid growth of unstructured data.
Advances in AI have made it possible to combine traditional quantitative datasets with large
volumes of unstructured data, such as images and videos. However, modern AI and ML
models often require more data for training than may be available, potentially leading to a
shortage of high-quality real-world data. Synthetic data, which is artificially generated to
resemble real-world datasets, may be needed to help close this gap, although this introduces
important considerations regarding both data quality and the reliability of the underlying
models.
Business Model Developments
Increased use of cloud computing and pre-trained AI models has been a major business
model development in recent years. Cloud computing offers flexible delivery of services,
prompting many financial institutions to rely more heavily on cloud-based solutions. In
addition, the high costs associated with training AI models and maintaining the necessary
infrastructure have led firms to depend more on cloud providers that offer pre-trained AI
capabilities.
There is now far greater availability and use of pre-trained models than in the past. These
models allow financial institutions to save time and reduce costs compared with building
models in-house, although accountability and explainability requirements can create
challenges. Pre-trained LLMs are typically available in two forms: closed source and open
source. The industry trend has shifted toward open-source models, driven by their improving
performance relative to closed-source alternatives. Open-source models are often free, easily
accessible, and customizable to meet a firm’s specific needs.
Demand-Side Factors
The demand-side factors driving AI adoption include profitability, competition, and regulation.
Profitability
AI adoption in financial institutions continues to be driven by profit opportunities arising from
three areas:
1.Revenue growth. This includes more tailored products and services, improved customer
```
onboarding, and reduced churn (i.e., the rate at which customers discontinue using
```
```
products or services).
```
2.Cost reduction. This is achieved by automating manual tasks, improving worker
productivity, and reallocating resources toward revenue-generating activities that are
difficult to automate.
3.Improved risk management. This leads to enhanced forecasting of future cash flows,
losses, and delinquencies.
Competition
Pressure to keep up with competitors that are fully leveraging AI, and GenAI in particular,
continues to drive adoption. As competitive pressures increase, the outcomes may be
positive, such as lower prices and greater innovation—but they may also be negative if
safeguards and testing are insufficient.
Regulation
Companies need to enhance compliance and meet regulatory requirements more efficiently.
AI is increasingly viewed as a tool that can strengthen compliance capabilities, particularly as
regulations related to data protection, guidance on AI development and adoption, and
international standards continue to expand.
MODULE QUIZ 101.1
1. Automating manual tasks to reduce costs falls under which of the following demand-side drivers
of AI adoption?
A. Profitability.
B. Competition.
C. Technological developments.
D. Business model developments.
2. Which of the following statements best describes a feature of the Transformer architecture used
```
in large language models (LLMs)? The Transformer:
```
A. processes input data sequentially rather than in parallel.
B. uses positional encoding to predict future words based on earlier words.
C. uses feed-forward components to capture the order of words in a sentence.
D. employs an attention mechanism to focus on the most relevant parts of the input text.
MODULE 101.2: AI USE CASES IN FINANCIAL SERVICES
LO 101.b: Identify and analyze existing and emerging AI use cases in financial
services, including industry use cases as well as regulatory and supervisory use
cases.
Overview of AI Use Cases
While financial services firms have certainly increased their adoption of AI, a key challenge is
the lack of comprehensive data to assess the extent of that adoption. Competitive pressures
may also contribute to a gap between perceived and actual AI use. Figure 101.2 highlights
key financial services tasks along with example use cases and the types of learning or
reasoning involved.
Figure 101.2: Tasks and AI Use Cases
Industry Use Cases
Industry use cases have primarily focused on four areas: customers, operations, trading and
portfolio management, and regulatory compliance.
Customer-Focused Applications
AI has become an important tool for assessing customer creditworthiness in banking
transactions. During the loan preapproval process, banks increasingly rely on ML models to
supplement traditional credit scoring methods with additional data and behavioral insights.
ML is also used to target marketing campaigns based on customer behavior, helping firms
tailor products and improve retention. Other applications include assigning financial advisors
to individual investors and enhancing the pricing and risk management of insurance policies
by predicting policy risk and insured behavior. In addition, GenAI has enabled more
sophisticated chatbot interactions by shifting from rules-based systems to LLM-based
approaches.
Operations-Focused Applications
```
Operations-focused (i.e., back-office) use cases include model risk management, capital
```
optimization, code generation, and market impact analysis. AI is also used to support
volatility and liquidity risk management by helping firms build models that better predict how
heightened volatility may affect their positions. More complex and potentially riskier
applications involve optimizing regulatory capital requirements, such as estimating default risk
probabilities. GenAI has further expanded the range of operations use cases, including
content generation, information search and retrieval, code generation, legacy code
modernization, and voice transcription. An additional benefit of GenAI is its ability to broaden
access to advanced tools for firms that previously lacked sufficient staff with specialized
coding skills.
Trading and Portfolio Management
GenAI has been valuable for firms implementing reinforcement learning for trade execution
and for assessing market sentiment embedded in text data. LLMs can identify both obvious
and subtle relationships between words that may be difficult to capture with traditional
approaches. In addition to enhancing trading algorithms, GenAI can also support portfolio
management by providing automation tools that streamline analysis and decision-making.
Regulatory Compliance
A major area of growth since 2017 has been the use of AI to meet regulatory requirements,
```
often referred to as regulatory technology (RegTech). Use cases span fraud detection,
```
```
anti-money laundering (AML), and combating the financing of terrorism (CFT). Examples
```
include identifying the misuse of legal entities and arrangements, investigating sanctions
evasion, detecting tax evasion and other scams, and uncovering trade-based money
laundering and fraud. AI-generated financial crime reports can also provide significant
efficiency gains for firms.
Regulatory and Supervisory Use Cases
```
Supervisory technology (SupTech), which refers to technology use by supervisory
```
authorities, has expanded substantially in recent years. Central banks are increasingly using
AI to enhance information collection, statistical compilation, and payment system oversight.
Efficient use of AI in supervisory work also includes leveraging alternative data for
assessments, conducting real-time economic analysis, and experimenting with LLMs for tasks
such as analysis and research. As noted earlier, the analysis of text data using GenAI and NLP
has also proven valuable in this area.
Inspection is another area in which GenAI and NLP have proven useful, allowing supervisors
to review large volumes of regulatory examination documents to draft inspection reports and
related summaries. LLMs can also generate customized error messages to support
supervisory workflows. Potential future applications include using GenAI to model stress tests
and bank runs by simulating social media interactions. More broadly, SupTech will likely need
to incorporate additional AI capabilities so that regulatory authorities can understand and
keep pace with the rapid growth of AI adoption within financial institutions.
MODULE QUIZ 101.2
1. Facial recognition as an AI use case is most closely associated with which of the following learning
or reasoning types?
A. Supervised classification of images, voice, and text.
B. Supervised learning using historical data to predict future outcomes.
C. AI technique used to determine optimal solutions to decision problems.
D. Development of individual profiles using supervised or reinforcement learning.
2. Content generation, information search and retrieval, code generation, legacy code streamlining,
and voice transcription are examples of which of the following categories of industry use cases?
A. Regulatory compliance.
B. Customer-focused applications.
C. Operations-focused applications.
D. Trading and portfolio management.
MODULE 101.3: AI AND FINANCIAL STABILITY: SYSTEMIC RISKS
AND VULNERABILITIES
LO 101.c: Assess the potential implications of AI for financial stability, with a focus on the
following vulnerabilities:
Third-party dependencies and service provider concentration
Market correlations
Cyber risks
Model risk, data, and governance
Other vulnerabilities, such as fraud, disinformation, and misalignment
Overview of AI-Related Financial Stability Challenges
To maintain financial stability, risk management and appropriate controls must keep pace with
the speed at which financial institutions are adopting AI. Potential impacts on financial
stability arise from several key developments related to AI:
Expanded AI integration in financial services. The use of AI in finance has grown
dramatically in recent years, with many institutions applying AI methods across core
operations and business lines.
Greater reliance on opaque or unstructured training data. AI models increasingly depend
on training data that may be unavailable, opaque, or highly unstructured, which can
introduce uncertainty and reduce model transparency.
Dependence on specialized hardware and infrastructure. AI development is closely tied to
cloud service providers and a concentrated market for advanced computing chips, creating
potential bottlenecks and vulnerabilities in critical infrastructure.
Technological breakthroughs in GenAI and LLMs. Competitive pressures and relatively easy
access to GenAI and LLM tools may accelerate deployment, enabling more advanced
applications but also introducing new risks and vulnerabilities.
```
From a regulatory and supervisory standpoint, two key challenges are (1) the limited
```
```
availability of data on AI use and (2) the uncertainty created by rapid innovation. Effective
```
surveillance is difficult because representative and consistent data on how AI is being
deployed are often lacking. For financial institutions that fall outside traditional regulatory
frameworks, AI use is even less visible to authorities. Regulators also need to keep pace with
the knowledge and technical skills of the firms and individuals they oversee, which will
require significant investment in training and capability building.
This section discusses four specific AI-related vulnerabilities and their potential impact on
financial markets. Figure 101.3 summarizes these vulnerabilities, and the subsequent material
provides more detailed explanations.
Figure 101.3: Vulnerabilities and Impacts on Financial Markets
Third-Party Dependencies and Service Provider Concentration
Developing and deploying AI tools internally requires significant resource investments, which
has led many financial institutions to rely on third-party service providers to meet their AI
needs. From a hardware perspective, institutions that build their own AI models, customize
pre-trained models, or deploy AI applications depend heavily on accelerated computing chips,
which are obtained either through cloud service providers or directly from chip suppliers.
```
As noted earlier, cloud service providers (CSPs) play a central role in AI development,
```
including the training and dissemination of LLMs. Pre-trained third-party models have also
become increasingly popular among institutions seeking to avoid the cost and complexity of
developing models from scratch. Vendor-provided models have long been used in applications
such as cyber anomaly detection and fraud detection.
Not surprisingly, only a limited number of entities provide cloud services and accelerated
computing chips. In addition, there is substantial vertical integration across the AI supply
chain. The data aggregation market has also seen consolidation, with smaller competitors
being acquired by larger data providers.
Additional concentration in the LLM and GenAI service provider market is likely driven by the
following factors:
Complexity and costs. Significant human capital and financial resources are required to
develop and maintain advanced AI systems.
Vertical integration. Key activities across the AI supply chain are concentrated among a
small number of firms.
Supply chain bottlenecks. Accelerated computing chips, such as graphics processing units
```
(GPUs), remain scarce.
```
Investment behavior. Incumbent firms are making large investments in new AI labs,
reinforcing their market position.
Multimodality. Growing demand for models capable of performing a wide range of GenAI
tasks may favor providers with broad capabilities.
However, there are several forces that may support a more competitive landscape:
Hardware competition. Increased competition among chip manufacturers could expand
supply and reduce GPU prices.
Open-source models. Continued availability and performance improvements in open-source
models may broaden market participation.
Architectural innovations. Advances that improve scalability and efficiency could lower
barriers to entry.
Domain- and task-specific models. Growth in specialized models may diversify provider
options and reduce concentration.
As AI continues to grow in importance within financial markets, service provider
concentration and third-party dependencies will continue to expose financial institutions to
operational risks, including supply chain disruptions and cyber events affecting key vendors.
At the same time, increased coordination and technological synergies may enhance the
effectiveness and efficiency of risk management efforts.
In addition to deploying AI in core business areas, firms are increasingly using AI to support
risk management functions such as liquidity, market, and credit risk. A key challenge,
however, is the lack of representative data on AI use, which makes it difficult to assess the
extent to which AI contributes to these risk management activities.
Financial institutions’ adoption of GenAI has been cautious so far, but the accessibility of
technology and strong industry interest may lead to more rapid deployment and deeper
integration into critical operations in the future. Potential applications include investment
advisory services, price discovery, coding assistance for functions such as claims processing,
loan servicing, core banking, and payment execution, as well as risk management and
regulatory compliance activities. GenAI may also be used to deliver tailored product
recommendations to customers across investment, credit, insurance, and payment offerings.
Third-party risk management standards and frameworks are in place to help mitigate risks
associated with AI service providers. Financial institutions are expected to apply these
standards by conducting periodic risk assessments, maintaining detailed vendor inventories,
and performing ongoing monitoring and due diligence.
Market Correlations
Greater correlations in financial markets may naturally emerge as institutions increasingly use
similar models and data sources. This behavior is driven by factors such as limited model
choices, herding tendencies, network externalities, and a lack of transparency in model
design. The risks associated with higher correlations include a greater likelihood of flash
crashes, amplified volatility, and more severe liquidity crunches during market downturns.
Financial institutions may also face unexpected interconnections when models trained on
similar datasets generate correlated predictions. In addition, homogeneity in risk assessments
can arise when institutions build models around the same risk management standards.
Pre-trained core models are often used by financial institutions that want to apply LLMs to
domain-specific tasks. Because these models share similar architectures and training data,
their outputs may be naturally correlated, which can contribute to market stress and liquidity
pressures. Correlations may also increase if GenAI-generated outputs are incorporated into
future training datasets. The extent and manner in which LLMs and GenAI are used will
influence the degree of market correlation risk. If these models are used to inform risk
management practices, shape investment strategies, or support the development of new AI
applications, correlation risk is higher—but if they are used primarily to address internal,
back-office inefficiencies such as information retrieval, employee training, or document
drafting, the associated correlation risk is far lower.
Market correlations could also increase as greater automation and faster decision-making in
financial markets lead to more synchronized behavior, such as coordinated reactions to
market conditions or shifts in corporate deposit activity, which may amplify market stress.
However, AI may also help reduce correlations if it is used to design more customized
investment, portfolio management, and trading strategies. In addition, AI could diversify the
capital market base by lowering barriers to entry for new participants.
Additional mitigation strategies to reduce the impact of AI-driven correlation vulnerabilities
include volatility control mechanisms, such as circuit breakers, and enhanced transparency
requirements. Complementary measures, such as consistent monitoring and improved
surveillance and data mechanisms, can also help address these risks.
Cyber Risks
An increase in cyberattack opportunities and an expansion of threat actors’ capabilities could
lead to AI-driven growth in operational vulnerabilities. GenAI may enable new and more
effective cyberattacks at a higher frequency, while also lowering barriers to entry for less
sophisticated threat actors. LLMs themselves could be exploited to carry out certain types of
attacks. As modern AI systems allow interaction through many different channels and rely on
```
increasingly large datasets, the number of potential attack vectors has grown significantly;
```
these vectors are the pathways an attacker can use to compromise a system. Examples
include prompt injection attacks that extract confidential information and model/data
poisoning attacks, in which model weights and/or training data are intentionally
manipulated.
The financial sector is particularly vulnerable when cyberattacks target central banks, critical
service providers, financial market infrastructures, essential financial services that cannot be
easily substituted, or other highly important firms within the industry. Volatility, liquidity
vulnerabilities, and operational losses can all be amplified by service disruptions and declines
in confidence resulting from such cyber events.
Ironically, although firms and other legitimate actors may benefit from breakthroughs in
GenAI, bad actors may benefit even more in the short term. Potential advantages for
legitimate users include automation of routine tasks, faster incident response, improved cyber
event detection, more effective diagnosis of malicious code, and enhanced employee training
on key cybersecurity techniques and standards.
Cyber resources at both the local jurisdictional level and the international level can help
authorities and financial institutions monitor and mitigate vulnerabilities to cyber events. Best
practices for managing cybersecurity risks include developing a new AI risk management
framework, integrating AI-specific considerations into an existing framework, and
incorporating AI-focused questions into the vendor or service provider due diligence process.
Model Risk, Data, and Governance
During periods of crisis, it may become more difficult to monitor, validate, and correct models.
This increase in model risk is a natural consequence of greater AI use and reduced
explainability at both the model and methodology levels. As models become more complex
and opaque, they become harder to validate. Accountability challenges may also arise
throughout the entire lifecycle of an AI system.
Because LLM outputs are typically generated as unstructured text, assessing their accuracy
and quality can be challenging. As a result, conducting effective outcome analyses and
determining error rates are often difficult. Hallucinations represent a type of model
inaccuracy associated with GenAI, in which a model produces a highly confident but incorrect
response. Data quality assessments are further complicated by the lack of transparency or
availability of model training data, the use of large and unstructured data sources, and limited
familiarity among financial institutions with evaluating these types of datasets.
Firms should have strong and comprehensive governance structures in place to properly
monitor underlying data sources and AI use. A natural concern is whether the rapid pace of
AI adoption is matched by the implementation of appropriate controls that reflect the speed,
scale, and complexity of that adoption.
AI-related data quality challenges and model risk must be addressed by regulated financial
institutions through their existing risk management frameworks. Model risk management
standards should include requirements for data quality assessments, model validation,
performance and outcome analysis, and ongoing monitoring. These standards should also
specify risk mitigation strategies for situations in which data is unavailable or limited.
Other Vulnerabilities
```
Additional AI-related vulnerabilities include (1) fraud, such as the use of GenAI to facilitate
```
```
financial fraud; (2) disinformation, which can be generated and disseminated by malicious
```
```
actors during periods of stress; and (3) misalignment, where systems do not operate as
```
intended or fail to meet regulatory and legal standards.
Financial Fraud
For many financial institutions, the costs of managing and correcting fraud continue to rise
due to the increasing volume of fraud cases. GenAI’s strong capabilities in video and voice
generation create significant risks, including deepfakes, fake profiles and IDs, bypassed
security checks, and fraud targeting both customers and institutions. As noted earlier,
advancements in GenAI may benefit malicious actors more than legitimate users in the short
run. Ironically, a more cautious and risk-averse approach taken by legitimate actors can
create asymmetries, because malicious actors are often willing to adopt AI rapidly and take
greater risks. Currently, generating fraudulent AI content may be easier than detecting it. To
help reduce fraud risk, improving both AI literacy and financial literacy among customers is
essential.
Disinformation
More sophisticated disinformation campaigns, amplified by the role of social media, may be
enabled by GenAI and could have significant financial stability implications during periods of
stress. Disinformation may become more convincing and scalable, increasing vulnerabilities in
financial markets that already tend to react strongly to information shocks. As technology
advances and malicious actors become more capable, the risks associated with disinformation
and its impact on financial markets are likely to grow.
Misalignment
AI systems designed to maximize profits may behave in ways that are not aligned with
regulatory or legal standards—particularly, if they do not operate as intended. Such
misalignment can contribute to the spread of disinformation or behaviors that negatively
affect financial markets and financial stability. Examples include AI systems that learn
collusive strategies to raise prices or systems that spread disinformation that triggers bank
runs while profiting from the resulting decline in a bank’s stock price.
Longer-Term Systemic Implications
From a long-term perspective, several additional considerations arise as AI use continues to
expand rapidly. The demand for data and computing resources has grown substantially,
though many financial firms remain focused on improving internal efficiencies rather than
developing customized AI applications that generate value beyond efficiency gains. Another
ongoing issue is the uneven playing field created by differences in regulatory and supervisory
oversight across firms and jurisdictions.
In the broader economy, AI could contribute to shifts in interest rates, inflation, and labor
market dynamics. Output and wages may be significantly affected by the use of GenAI,
potentially creating greater risks for high-skilled workers than those observed during previous
waves of technological innovation.
Although AI-related energy consumption currently represents only a small share of global
```
energy use (approximately 1%), it is expected to increase in the future. Training, developing,
```
and operating AI models require significant amounts of reliable and competitively priced
energy. As financial institutions and markets become more dependent on AI, they may
become more exposed to broader energy supply and cost pressures. To avoid exacerbating
climate risks, energy sources must be clean—although improvements in model training
efficiency, commitments to clean energy, and investments in renewable energy innovations
can help mitigate these concerns.
Summary and Implications
Since the 2017 FSB report was issued, the financial services industry has seen broader
adoption of AI tools, a more diversified set of use cases, and increased reliance on both
RegTech and SupTech. Financial institutions are drawn to the potential for improved
operational efficiency, stronger regulatory compliance, more personalized financial products,
and enhanced analytics and data capabilities.
However, these potential benefits come with heightened risks and vulnerabilities. Limited data
on AI use, along with the speed of technological change and implementation, creates
challenges for financial authorities. Ongoing research, policy development, and monitoring
are essential—they should be undertaken by financial authorities in partnership with
institutions, and within established risk management frameworks and standards.
```
For authorities, regulators, and boards such as the FSB, key priorities may include (1)
```
addressing information and data gaps in monitoring AI use within the financial system and
```
assessing its implications for financial stability; (2) evaluating whether the vulnerabilities
```
discussed in this reading are adequately covered by existing supervisory and regulatory
```
frameworks; and (3) strengthening supervisory and regulatory capabilities to support policy
```
oversight of AI applications in the financial sector.
MODULE QUIZ 101.3
1. Which of the following factors is most likely to support a more competitive landscape in the LLM
and GenAI service provider market?
A. Multimodality.
B. Vertical integration.
C. Supply chain bottlenecks.
D. Architectural innovations.
2. Which of the following statements is most accurate regarding cyber risks in the financial sector?
A. GenAI can be used to carry out cyberattacks, but LLMs cannot be used in the same way.
B. Model or data poisoning occurs when confidential information is extracted from AI models.
C. Reduced barriers to entry may make it easier for malicious actors to take advantage of AI
capabilities.
D. Malicious actors are likely to benefit more than legitimate actors from AI advancements in the
long term.
KEY CONCEPTS
LO 101.a
```
Since the 2017 Financial Stability Board (FSB) report, artificial intelligence (AI) has advanced
```
rapidly due to major developments in big data, computational power, deep learning, and
```
generative AI (GenAI). These advances have expanded the capabilities of AI systems and
```
accelerated their adoption across the financial sector.
AI adoption has been strongly supported by the following supply-side factors:
```
Technological developments. These have (1) improved the processing of unstructured data
```
```
through the use of embeddings in deep learning models, (2) enabled significant progress in
```
```
natural language processing (NLP) through the introduction of the Transformer
```
```
architecture, and (3) allowed larger and more complex models to be trained at lower cost
```
due to increased computational capacity and broader integration of graphics processing
```
units (GPUs).
```
Data-related developments. These have made it possible to combine traditional
quantitative datasets with large volumes of unstructured data, such as images and videos.
At the same time, modern AI models require large amounts of high-quality training data,
raising the possibility of future data shortages and increasing the potential need for
synthetic data.
Business model developments. These have also played a role, as financial institutions have
increasingly relied on cloud computing and pre-trained AI models, allowing them to reduce
infrastructure needs and lower the costs associated with in-house model development.
AI adoption is further influenced by the following several demand-side factors:
```
Profitability. This remains a key motivator, as institutions use AI to (1) increase revenue,
```
```
(2) reduce costs through automation and productivity improvements, and (3) enhance risk
```
management.
Competitive pressures. These contribute as firms strive to keep pace with peers that are
incorporating advanced AI and GenAI into their operations, which can lead to greater
innovation and lower prices but may also introduce risks if proper safeguards and testing
are lacking.
Regulatory developments. These further encourage adoption, as firms use AI to strengthen
compliance in areas such as data protection, AI governance, and international standards.
LO 101.b
Industry use cases for AI have largely centered on four areas: customers, operations, trading
and portfolio management, and regulatory compliance.
Customer-focused applications include assessing creditworthiness, modeling customer
behavior for targeted marketing, assigning financial advisors to individual investors,
improving insurance policy pricing and risk evaluation, and enabling more advanced
chatbot interactions.
```
Operations-focused (back-office) uses include model risk management, volatility and
```
liquidity risk modeling, capital optimization, code generation, market impact analysis,
content creation, information retrieval, legacy code streamlining, and voice transcription.
In trading and portfolio management, AI supports market sentiment analysis, identifying
relationships between words and textual signals, and improving trading algorithms and
portfolio decision-making.
Regulatory compliance applications extend to fraud detection, anti-money laundering
```
(AML), and combating the financing of terrorism (CFT).
```
From a supervisory and regulatory perspective, central banks and oversight authorities are
also leveraging AI. Applications include improving information collection, statistical
compilation, and payment system oversight, as well as using alternative data for supervisory
assessments and conducting real-time economic analysis. Supervisors are also experimenting
```
with large language models (LLMs) for analytical and research tasks, generating inspection
```
reports from large document sets, and creating customized error message feedback to
enhance supervisory workflows.
LO 101.c
Potential impacts on financial stability arise from several key developments related to AI,
including the expanding integration of AI across financial services, greater reliance on opaque
or unstructured training data, increased dependence on specialized hardware and
infrastructure, and rapid technological advances in GenAI and LLMs. These developments
interact with the following four major AI-related vulnerabilities that could significantly affect
financial markets:
Third-party dependencies and service provider concentration. Many financial institutions
rely on third-party providers for AI capabilities, including cloud services, pre-trained
models, and accelerated computing chips. Only a limited number of firms supply cloud
infrastructure and high-performance chips, and the AI supply chain is highly vertically
integrated. This concentration increases operational risk, as disruptions affecting key
providers can have widespread implications.
Market correlations. Greater use of similar models and data sources across institutions can
increase correlations in financial markets. This behavior is driven by limited model choices,
herding tendencies, network externalities, and limited model transparency. Higher
correlations can raise the likelihood of flash crashes, amplify volatility, and intensify liquidity
shortages during periods of stress.
Cyber risks. AI and GenAI expand the number and sophistication of potential cyberattacks,
```
lowering barriers to entry for malicious actors and broadening the attack surface (i.e., the
```
```
range of points within an institution’s system that attackers can attempt to exploit). Cyber
```
events targeting systemically important entities can heighten volatility, magnify liquidity
vulnerabilities, and erode confidence in financial markets. Although AI offers benefits to
legitimate users, malicious actors may be able to exploit AI tools more rapidly.
Model risk, data, and governance. During periods of stress, it becomes more difficult to
monitor, validate, and adjust models. Increased complexity and reduced explainability
heighten model risk, while large unstructured datasets and opaque training data create
additional data quality challenges. Effective governance frameworks are essential to ensure
proper oversight of model development, data sources, and AI use.
Additional vulnerabilities includefraud, such as the use of GenAI to generate deepfakes or
```
bypass security controls;disinformation, which can spread rapidly during crises and
```
```
destabilize markets; andmisalignment, where AI systems behave in ways that are
```
inconsistent with regulatory or legal expectations and may cause harmful market outcomes.
From a longer-term perspective, broader structural considerations include growing demand
for data and computing resources, uneven regulatory and supervisory environments, and
potential macroeconomic effects on interest rates, inflation, and labor markets. AI-related
energy consumption is also expected to rise, highlighting the need for clean and reliable
energy sources to avoid exacerbating climate-related risks.

(such as automating manual tasks to improve efficiency), and improved risk
```
management. Competition is also a demand-side factor, but it relates to firms adopting
AI to keep pace with industry peers. Technological and business model developments
```
are supply-side drivers of AI adoption. (LO 101.a)

relevant sections of the input text. Transformers process data in parallel, not
sequentially. Positional encoding captures information about word order, while feed-
```
forward components help predict subsequent words based on previous ones. (LO 101.a)

READING 102
THE GLOBAL DRIVERS OF PRIVATE CREDIT
Study Session 15
EXAM FOCUS
Private credit has grown rapidly in recent years and now accounts for more than $2.5 trillion
in assets across a wide range of industries and regions. For the exam, be able to identify the
```
four key forces behind this expansion: (1) low policy rates, (2) inefficiencies within the
```
```
banking sector, (3) increased regulatory pressures on banks, and (4) rising corporate
```
```
leverage. Also, understand how private credit funds (PCFs), including business development
```
```
companies (BDCs), compare with banks in terms of competitive dynamics. Lower equity costs
```
and increased reliance on cheaper debt have reduced PCFs’ overall cost of capital, eroding
the traditional funding advantage enjoyed by banks.
MODULE 102.1: PRIVATE CREDIT MARKET STRUCTURE AND
GROWTH DRIVERS
LO 102.a: Describe the structure and characteristics of private credit funds, as
well as the patterns in the global growth of the asset class.
Structure and Characteristics
```
Private credit funds (PCFs) are structured to provide loans directly to borrowers, such as
```
small- and medium-sized nonfinancial firms. From the fund’s perspective, these loans are held
as assets for their full term. PCFs are typically closed-end vehicles, which means investor
capital is committed for roughly five to eight years. This structure helps reduce liquidity and
maturity transformation risks. As investment products, PCFs are unlisted and, therefore,
relatively illiquid.
```
In the United States, business development companies (BDCs) account for roughly one-
```
fifth of the private credit market. BDCs are often publicly listed and structured to appeal to
retail investors. This is accomplished by providing more opportunities for earlier redemptions
through open-end features, rather than requiring the long capital commitments typical of
closed-end structures.
The underlying investors in PCFs are predominantly institutional investors who are willing to
commit their capital for extended periods without needing regular liquidity. There is also a
small but growing segment of retail investors. In contrast, banks rely heavily on short-term
funding sources, which exposes them to significant asset-liability mismatches. If the retail
investor base in PCFs continues to expand, PCFs may begin to encounter similar asset-liability
mismatch risks as those faced by banks.
PCFs frequently pursue a focused strategy ofdirect lending, which often involves floating-rate
structures and extensive covenants. Other common strategies includemezzanine lending,
```
which involves subordinated debt and may include equity rights;asset-based lending, which
```
```
requires substantial collateral; anddistressed debt investing, which involves purchasing
```
credit-risky debt at significant discounts.
Global Growth Patterns
```
Over the past two decades, private credit assets under management (AUM) have grown from
```
roughly $0.2 trillion to $2.5 trillion. The United States remains the dominant market,
accounting for nearly 90% of loan originations, while Europe, the United Kingdom, and
Canada make up most of the remaining activity. The sector has also broadened its reach,
expanding from its earlier focus on manufacturing, technology, and industrial firms to a wider
```
range of industries, including cleantech (e.g., environmental technologies) and life sciences.
```
Despite the overall growth and diversification of the private credit market, individual PCFs
tend to maintain highly concentrated loan portfolios, often focused on a limited set of
industries. This concentration is typically measured using the Herfindahl-Hirschman Index
```
(HHI), which shows that PCFs are far less diversified than banks or other nonbank lenders in
```
the syndicated loan market. The rationale for such concentration lies in the specialized
screening and monitoring capabilities that fund managers develop within their chosen
sectors. These capabilities allow them to better assess risk and tailor loan terms to the needs
of riskier or more highly leveraged borrowers, who often have limited collateral and a higher
likelihood of incurring net losses. Such borrowers are frequently rejected by traditional banks
but may still be able to secure financing through PCFs.
However, the lack of diversification also exposes PCFs to sector-specific downturns, which
could pose significant risks to investors. As this asset class continues to expand and fund
managers seek to attract more retail participation, the tradeoff between concentration and
diversification is likely to become more important. Institutional investors, who currently make
up most of the investor base, are generally better positioned to manage these risks through
broader portfolio diversification, whereas retail investors may be less aware of the
concentration risks inherent in PCFs. Ultimately, greater diversification may enhance safety
but can also reduce the higher returns that concentrated strategies sometimes offer.
LO 102.b: Discuss the potential drivers of private credit growth and interpret
regression results about their impact.
Potential Growth Drivers
```
The global expansion of private credit has been driven by several key factors, including (1)
```
```
accommodative monetary policy, (2) varying levels of efficiency within the banking sector, (3)
```
```
stricter regulatory requirements applied to traditional lenders, and (4) rising corporate
```
leverage.
1.Monetary policy. Prolonged periods of low policy rates have played a significant role in
supporting private credit activity. When interest rates remain low, banks often experience
```
compressed profit margins because the value of their assets (e.g., floating-rate loans)
```
```
declines more quickly than the value of their liabilities (e.g., fixed-rate customer deposits).
```
This can reduce banks’ willingness to extend credit and create opportunities for PCFs to fill
the lending gap. PCFs are not subject to the same margin compression because both their
assets and liabilities are generally structured on a floating-rate basis. As a result, PCFs may
be able to earn higher returns, which can attract more institutional investors to the sector.
2.Banking sector efficiency. Lending preferences within the banking sector are another
important factor. These preferences are often captured by the International Monetary
```
Fund’s (IMF’s) financial institutions (FI) index, which measures how effectively
```
banks meet loan demand. Banks are frequently reluctant to lend to many small- and
medium-sized firms because these borrowers cannot provide sufficient collateral, which
leads to lower FI index values. PCFs offer an alternative source of credit by reducing or
removing collateral requirements and relying instead on more targeted covenants.
3.Regulatory stringency. As banking regulations continue to evolve and impose higher capital
requirements along with stricter oversight, banks have become less willing to lend to firms
they view as riskier. As a result, these riskier firms increasingly turn to PCFs, which
generally operate with greater flexibility and are more willing to extend credit to higher-risk
borrowers.
4.Corporate leverage. Corporate leverage in the nonfinancial sector has increased alongside
the growth of private credit. PCFs are more willing than banks to extend credit to highly
leveraged firms. The substantial rise in corporate leverage across many countries has been
closely correlated with the expansion of lending by PCFs.
Regression Analysis
A regression analysis conducted by the BIS examined the global drivers of private credit
growth across 45 countries from 2010 to 2019. The analysis included four explanatory
```
variables: (1) the policy rate; (2) IMF’s FI index, which measures banking sector efficiency;
```
```
(3) the degree of bank regulatory stringency; and (4) the level of corporate leverage. The
```
regression results estimated how each variable affected the amount of newly originated
private credit.
Regarding thepolicy rate andFI index, the regression results indicate that these two
variables have the largest overall influence on private credit growth. PCFs tend to engage in
```
more direct lending when the policy rate is lower, reflected in a small negative coefficient (–
```
```
0.03). A one-standard-deviation decline in the policy rate is associated with roughly a 12%
```
increase in private credit loan origination. PCFs also increase direct lending when the FI index
```
is lower, which exhibits a much larger negative coefficient (–1.679 to –1.906). The FI index
```
has the largest economic effect among the four variables, with a one-standard-deviation
decline linked to about a 33% increase in private credit activity.
Thebank regulation stringency andcorporate leverage variables show smaller economic
effects in the regression results. PCFs tend to increase their direct lending when regulatory
```
stringency is higher, consistent with a modest positive coefficient (0.463 to 0.667). A one-
```
standard-deviation increase in regulatory stringency raises private credit by roughly 2%–4%.
Corporate leverage has the weakest relationship with private credit growth, reflected in a very
```
small positive coefficient (0.001). Its economic impact is minimal, with a one-standard-
```
deviation increase in leverage associated with less than a 1% rise in private credit.

D. closed-end fund structure.

A. Direct lending.
B. Mezzanine lending.
C. Asset-based lending.
D. Distressed debt investing.
3. According to the BIS regression analysis on private credit growth, which of the following variables

A. Policy rates.
B. Corporate leverage.
C. Bank regulation stringency.

MODULE 102.2: COMPETITIVE DYNAMICS: PRIVATE CREDIT VS.
BANK LENDING
LO 102.c: Compare private credit loans with bank loans and assess how changes
in cost of equity, cost of debt, and leverage have influenced the competitive
dynamics between them.
Private Credit Loans vs. Bank Loans
Private credit loans typically offer more flexible terms, tailored covenants, and faster
execution, targeting borrowers who are often declined by traditional banks. These borrowers
usually have limited tangible collateral, high leverage, and/or lower accounting transparency,
which makes them less appealing to banks but better suited to PCFs that specialize in
customized lending solutions.
Impacts of Changes in Cost of Equity, Cost of Debt, and
Leverage
```
The cost of capital (i.e., the weighted average cost of capital [WACC]) reflects both the cost
```
```
of debt (CoD) and the cost of equity (CoE), weighted according to their shares in a firm’s
```
capital structure. The analysis of cost of capital in this section focuses on U.S. banks and
BDCs.
Since the 2007–2009 financial crisis, the CoE for BDCs has remained consistently lower than
that of banks. Because banks face a higher CoE, they have increasingly relied on lower-cost
debt financing, which has led to significantly higher leverage relative to BDCs.
Over the past two decades, the CoD for BDCs has been consistently higher than that of banks
```
(though only marginally). This gap tends to widen during periods of monetary tightening,
```
when interest rates rise. These patterns are consistent with BDC funding costs increasing in
step with policy rates, while bank funding costs, particularly customer deposits, adjust more
gradually.
Historically, the cost of capital for BDCs was higher than for banks, but over the past decade,
the two have converged. Banks once enjoyed a substantial funding advantage because their
CoD was lower. However, this advantage narrowed by roughly 250 basis points between 2010
and 2019. During this period, banks experienced a significant increase in CoE due to
heightened market risk and stricter regulatory requirements.
At the same time, BDCs increased their leverage by making greater use of debt financing,
effectively shifting their funding mix away from more expensive equity toward relatively
cheaper debt. Although BDCs’ cost of debt remains higher than that of banks, debt is still
substantially cheaper for BDCs than equity. As a result, substituting equity with debt lowered
their overall cost of capital. This decline in funding costs enabled BDCs to offer more
competitive loan rates, eroding banks’ traditional funding advantage and supporting the rising
market share of private credit. Notably, the increase in leverage caused only a modest rise in
BDCs’ cost of equity, and this increase was more than offset by the benefit of relying more
heavily on lower-cost debt.
Overall, both the lower CoE of BDCs relative to banks and the increase in BDC leverage
contributed roughly equally to the erosion of banks’ funding advantage. The CoD played
almost no role in narrowing the gap between banks and BDCs, since the difference in their
```
CoD remained small during periods of falling policy rates (even though the cheaper debt still
```
```
helped lower BDCs’ own cost of capital). This dynamic may shift when policy rates rise, as
```
BDCs could gradually lose the benefit of cheap debt financing and face a higher overall cost
of capital.
MODULE QUIZ 102.2
1. What is the primary reason for the narrowing cost of capital between banks and business
```
development companies (BDCs) since the Global Financial Crisis of 2007–2009?
```
A. Increase in bank leverage.
B. Decrease in bank cost of debt.
C. Increase in bank cost of equity.
D. Decrease in BDC cost of equity.
2. Which of the following factors best explains why borrowing firms prefer private credit over bank
loans?
A. Tailored covenants.
B. Lower interest rates.
C. Longer repayment periods.
D. No collateral requirements.
KEY CONCEPTS
LO 102.a
```
Private credit funds (PCFs) provide loans directly to small- and medium-sized nonfinancial
```
firms. They are typically structured as closed-end, unlisted vehicles, which makes them
relatively illiquid but helps limit liquidity and maturity transformation risks. In the United
```
States, business development companies (BDCs) account for about one-fifth of the private
```
credit market. Unlike most PCFs, BDCs are often publicly listed and are designed to attract
retail investors by offering more frequent redemption opportunities through open-end
features.
Private credit has grown rapidly over the past two decades, expanding from roughly $0.2
trillion to more than $2.5 trillion in assets. The United States remains the dominant market,
and the sector has broadened from its initial focus on manufacturing, technology, and
industrial firms to a wider range of industries.
Although the market has diversified overall, individual PCFs often maintain highly
concentrated loan portfolios focused on a small set of industries. This specialization supports
stronger screening and monitoring but increases vulnerability to sector-specific downturns. As
retail participation grows, the tradeoff between concentration and diversification will become
increasingly important for both fund managers and investors.
LO 102.b
The global expansion of private credit is supported by four main drivers: accommodative
monetary policy, low banking sector efficiency, increased regulatory stringency on traditional
banks, and rising corporate leverage. Each factor influences the ability and/or willingness of
banks to lend, thereby creating opportunities for PCFs to fill financing gaps.
A regression analysis conducted by the BIS showed that banking sector efficiency, measured
```
by the IMF’s financial institutions (FI) index, has the largest economic impact on private credit
```
growth. A one-standard-deviation decline in the FI index is associated with roughly a 33%
increase in private credit lending. The policy rate is the second-most important driver, with
lower policy rates being linked to about a 12% increase in private credit activity. Regulatory
stringency and corporate leverage also contribute to private credit growth, but their effects
are much smaller. A one-standard-deviation increase in regulatory stringency raises private
credit by only about 2% to 4%, while a similar increase in corporate leverage results in less
than a 1% rise.
LO 102.c
Private credit loans differ from traditional bank loans by offering more flexible terms, tailored
covenants, and faster execution. These features allow PCFs to serve borrowers who often
lack sufficient collateral, have higher leverage, or exhibit lower transparency, which makes
them less appealing to banks.
The competitive gap between banks and PCFs has narrowed over time because of changes in
funding costs. Banks now face a higher cost of equity due to increased market risk and
stricter regulatory requirements. In contrast, BDCs and other PCFs have made greater use of
lower-cost debt financing, which has increased their leverage and reduced their overall cost
of capital. These shifts have eroded the historical funding advantage of banks and
strengthened the competitive position of private credit lenders.
ANSWER KEY FOR MODULE QUIZZES
Module Quiz 102.1
1. D Closed-end funds lock in investor capital for the life of the fund, helping align the
maturity of assets and liabilities and reducing the risk of forced sales or liquidity
mismatches. This structure is a key reason that PCFs can safely hold illiquid loans. In
contrast, open-end structures and greater retail investor access increase liquidity risk
because they require more frequent redemption opportunities. Most private credit funds
are unlisted and target institutional investors, who are more willing to commit capital for
```
longer periods without requiring regular liquidity. (LO 102.a)
```
2. A Private credit funds most commonly engage in direct lending, typically involving floating-
rate loans with extensive covenants. While they also participate in mezzanine lending,
asset-based lending, and distressed debt investing, these strategies are less central to
```
the typical PCF business model. (LO 102.a)
```
3. D The regression results showed that the FI index has the largest economic impact on
private credit growth. A one-standard-deviation decline in the FI index is associated with
roughly a 33% increase in private credit lending, which is the strongest effect among
the four variables. The policy rate is the second-most significant driver, with a
comparable decline linked to about a 12% increase in private credit activity. Bank
regulation stringency and corporate leverage also contribute to private credit growth,
```
but their effects are much smaller. (LO 102.b)
```

substantially between 2010 and 2019, driven by higher market risk and stricter

equity, and their greater use of lower-cost debt financing helped reduce their overall
cost of capital. The cost of debt played little role, because the difference in debt funding
costs between banks and BDCs remained small during periods of declining policy rates.

```
