READING 103
GLOBAL FINANCIAL STABILITY REPORT
Study Session 15
EXAM FOCUS
This reading examines how geopolitical risks affect financial markets and economies. Military
conflicts and other geopolitical shocks can create significant instability, causing disruptions
both domestically and across borders through contagion. These vulnerabilities become more
pronounced when economies are highly interconnected through trade-related revenue
exposures and cross-border capital flows. For the exam, understand how geopolitical conflicts
increase market, credit, and liquidity risks, leading to asset price declines, higher tail risk, and
tighter lending conditions in affected economies. Also, be familiar with how these risks are
priced, including the impact of geopolitical events on sovereign risk premiums, sovereign
```
credit default swap (CDS) spreads, geopolitical risk betas, and equity and option prices.
```
Although average stock market reactions to geopolitical tensions are often modest, major
conflicts or persistent shocks can trigger financial contagion, prolonged declines in economic
activity and asset values, and higher risk premiums.
MODULE 103.1: GEOPOLITICAL RISK INFLUENCES AND MARKET
RESPONSES
Geopolitical Risk Channels
LO 103.a: Identify and explain the key channels through which geopolitical risk
influences asset prices and financial stability and discuss policy measures to
address potential consequences.
Geopolitical risk refers to the adverse effects on financial markets, trade relations, and
economic growth that arise from major conflicts such as wars, terrorist actions, and trade
disputes involving tariffs or sanctions. Even the threat of rising geopolitical tensions can
depress asset prices and prompt a reallocation of capital flows, ultimately weakening financial
stability. Geopolitical risk affects asset prices through two primary channels: the economic
channel and the market sentiment channel.
The economic channel reflects disruptions to trade, capital flows, and production capacity.
For example, Russia’s 2022 invasion of Ukraine led to sharp declines in stock prices and
sovereign bond values, while commodity prices such as oil and grains increased due to supply
concerns. These disruptions could also elevate sovereign debt burdens as governments boost
military spending or experience reduced fiscal revenues.
The market sentiment channel captures the effects of heightened uncertainty, increased
risk aversion, and reduced investor confidence. Even in the absence of direct conflict, the
perception of instability can depress valuations and raise market volatility.
Appropriate policy responses are essential for mitigating these risks. Entities exposed to
geopolitical risk should be able to assess, measure, and manage these exposures effectively.
Countries, particularly emerging market economies, can strengthen their resilience by
improving regulatory frameworks, preserving fiscal and monetary flexibility, and deepening
financial markets. Financial institutions should also maintain adequate capital and liquidity
buffers to absorb potential losses.
Impact of Geopolitical Risk Across Countries, Sectors, and
Asset Classes
LO 103.b: Analyze how global geopolitical risk events affect countries, sectors,
and asset classes differently, including their varying impacts on commodity
importers and exporters.
Geopolitical risk events affect countries unevenly, largely based on their economic structure
and commodity exposure. Nations directly involved in conflicts or closely linked to affected
regions tend to experience the most severe consequences, including physical damage in
directly affected countries, sanctions, and disruptions to trade and investment. Commodity-
exporting countries, particularly energy producers, often benefit from higher prices that
accompany global tensions. For instance, oil exporters gained from surging energy prices
following Russia’s 2022 invasion of Ukraine, while European importers faced currency
depreciation and rising sovereign spreads, increasing their vulnerability.
Sectoral impacts of geopolitical risk are also uneven. Defense and energy companies often
benefit from geopolitical shocks, as military spending increases and commodity prices rise.
For example, U.S. defense firms have historically maintained stable cash flows during conflicts
due to steady government contracting. In contrast, the banking, retail, and utilities sectors
typically face declines, reflecting weaker consumer demand, tighter credit conditions, and
valuation pressures.
Asset classes also respond differently to geopolitical shocks. Commodities such as oil and gold
typically rise in value, reflecting supply concerns and increased demand for safe-haven
```
assets. Equity prices generally decline, particularly in emerging markets; in contrast,
```
sovereign bonds in safe-haven countries often appreciate, resulting in lower yields. Event-
study evidence suggests stocks fall about 3% immediately after major geopolitical events,
with severe events causing declines of up to 9%. Exchange rates, especially in commodity-
importing countries, also tend to weaken, further amplifying vulnerabilities.
The severity of the impact also depends on the nature of the geopolitical event. Extreme
events such as wars and military interventions tend to produce more severe and long-lasting
economic consequences because of loss of life, physical destruction, and prolonged
disruptions, compared with sanctions or trade restrictions. Longer-lasting conflicts naturally
lead to more persistent effects. Although most major events since World War II have resulted
in modest and short-lived stock price declines, some episodes such as the 1973 Arab oil
embargo and Iraq’s 1990 invasion of Kuwait caused sharper and more sustained market
reactions, with declines that persisted for several months.
Stock Market Responses to Geopolitical Risk Events
LO 103.c: Assess the stock market responses to different domestic and global
geopolitical risk events, including Russia’s invasion of Ukraine and the U.S.–China
trade tensions, and explain the cross-border effects of these events.
General Stock Market Responses
Stock markets generally react only modestly to geopolitical events, but the severity and
duration of shocks can significantly amplify the market response. The impact depends on
both macroeconomic uncertainty and investors’ risk attitudes. On average, aggregate stock
prices fall by about 0.3% after a country-specific geopolitical risk shock. However, large
regional shocks that are at least two standard deviations above normal geopolitical risk levels,
such as major conflicts, can produce losses up to 7x larger—and with greater persistence.
Global geopolitical shocks that affect multiple countries at the same time can have an even
stronger impact, with effects that may be as much as 20x larger than typical returns.
```
The Chicago Board Options Exchange (CBOE) Volatility Index (VIX) is an important
```
measure of expected market volatility. The VIX typically spikes following geopolitical shocks,
signaling higher uncertainty and increased risk aversion. During such periods, tail risk, which
refers to the likelihood of extreme market outcomes, also tends to rise. Global geopolitical
events that affect multiple countries can have effects that persist for up to six months after
the initial shock.
International military conflicts tend to have a stronger impact on equity markets than other
geopolitical events, particularly in emerging markets. On average, domestic geopolitical risk
events lead to a decline of about 1% in equity markets within a month, but the impact
increases to roughly 5% in emerging market economies experiencing a military conflict.
Cross-border effects are transmitted through trade and financial linkages. Countries that
are geographically or economically close to conflict zones often face sharper declines,
sometimes as much as 2.5x larger. Firms with revenue exposure to affected countries, or with
subsidiaries or significant shareholders located there, also experience declines in stock prices,
with the effects generally more pronounced in emerging markets.
Case Study: Russia’s 2022 Invasion of Ukraine
Russia’s invasion of Ukraine was a major global geopolitical risk event that caused significant
stock market declines not only in Russia and Ukraine but also in countries with close
economic and revenue ties to them. For example, firms in the defense and energy sectors
with revenue exposure to Russia and Ukraine experienced sharp declines following the
invasion. At the same time, equity returns in the same sectors rose in other countries
because investors anticipated higher defense spending and rising energy prices.
European markets fell more sharply than those in other advanced economies after the
invasion because of their close economic links to the region and their reliance on energy
imports. Firms with subsidiaries in Russia or Ukraine also experienced negative returns, with
valuations declining by about 2.5% within a week of the invasion. This illustrates how cross-
border exposure can amplify the impact of geopolitical shocks.
Since 2022, both the aggregate exposure of firms and countries to Russia and the number of
firms with subsidiaries there have declined significantly. However, exposure has increased for
some firms, particularly in certain Asia-Pacific and European countries.
Case Study: United States–China Trade Tensions
Trade tensions between the United States and China, which led to a series of tariff and
retaliatory tariff measures from 2018 to 2024, represented another major set of geopolitical
risk events. These tensions affected firms in both countries and contributed to sizeable stock
price declines. The effects were particularly pronounced in China, where equity values fell by
an average of about 4% following tariff announcements, including a drop of nearly 8% after
the May 2019 U.S. tariff announcement on Chinese products. In contrast, U.S. firms
experienced more moderate declines, averaging about 1.3% between 2018 and 2024, and
about 1.6%–1.8% following China’s retaliatory tariff announcement in August 2019.
PROFESSOR’S NOTE
Although this IMF report was published in April 2025, the analysis of United States–
China tariff announcements only covers events from 2018 to 2024. There was no
tariff event data from 2025 included in the study.

2. Which of the following policy approaches by financial institutions would most effectively mitigate

A. Relying solely on capital buffers.
B. Maintaining fixed asset allocations.
C. Deepening financial markets and developing robust regulatory frameworks.

```

```
A. is negative only if these firms have weak defense and energy sectors.
B. is limited to changes in commodity prices and does not affect firm-level stock returns.
C. is low because firms are insulated from geopolitical risk due to diversified revenue streams.

countries.
MODULE 103.2: MEASURING AND PRICING GEOPOLITICAL RISK
AND IMPACT ON FINANCIAL INSTITUTIONS
Effects of Geopolitical Shocks on Sovereign Risk Premiums
LO 103.d: Evaluate the effects of geopolitical shocks on sovereign risk premiums.
Geopolitical risks, particularly those involving emerging markets or military conflicts, can
increase financial instability and lead to higher sovereign risk premiums. Conflicts that
affect a country’s key trading partners can also raise its sovereign risk premiums by
weakening expected economic activity and heightening external vulnerabilities.
Sovereign risk premiums are commonly assessed using sovereign credit default swap
```
(CDS) spreads. These spreads tend to rise sharply after military conflicts because investors
```
become more concerned about fiscal stability and debt sustainability. For instance, sovereign
```
CDS spreads in emerging markets (EMs) have widened by roughly 180 basis points (bps)
```
within one month of major conflicts, compared with about 40 bps in advanced economies
```
(AEs), as shown in Figure 103.1. Consistent with wider sovereign risk premiums, long-term
```
```
sovereign yields tend to rise in conflict-affected (and more vulnerable) emerging markets. In
```
contrast, yields in safe-haven countries, such as the United States, United Kingdom,
Germany, Switzerland, and Japan, generally fall as investors seek safety during global shocks.
The magnitude of the safe-haven response increases with the size and severity of the risk
event.
Figure 103.1: Monthly Response of Sovereign CDS Spreads to Geopolitical Risk Events1
Sovereign risk tends to be higher in countries with elevated debt-to-GDP ratios, limited fiscal
and reserve buffers, and weaker institutional quality. Historically, countries with international
reserve adequacy ratios below the median have experienced sovereign CDS spread increases
of about 100 bps following major geopolitical shocks. In countries where institutional quality
is below the median, the increase has been even larger, reaching roughly 120 bps.
Overall, sovereign risk premiums reflect both a country’s direct exposure to geopolitical
shocks and its underlying fiscal strength. Rising fiscal vulnerabilities can undermine financial
stability and heighten the sensitivity of sovereign risk premiums to geopolitical events.
Geopolitical Risk in Equity and Option Markets
LO 103.e: Explain how investors price geopolitical risk in equity and option
markets, including changes in downside risk and tail-risk premiums.
Investors price geopolitical risk into both equity and option markets through risk premiums.
Under modern asset-pricing theory, risk premiums are positive for assets that lose value when
geopolitical risk increases because investors demand compensation for bearing those losses.
In contrast, risk premiums are negative for assets expected to gain value during periods of
elevated geopolitical risk because investors are willing to accept lower, or even negative,
premiums in exchange for their protective value.
Pricing Geopolitical Risk in Equity Markets
```
Geopolitical risk (GPR) betas are commonly used to measure the sensitivity of equity
```
returns to geopolitical shocks. Equity returns vary across sectors, with energy and defense
stocks typically exhibiting positive GPR betas and consumer-oriented sectors showing
negative GPR betas. These differences reflect underlying economic sensitivities: energy and
defense firms often benefit when geopolitical tensions rise, while consumer demand tends to
weaken during periods of heightened uncertainty.
GPR beta premiums can shift substantially in response to major geopolitical events,
particularly wars. For example, the GPR beta premium between the energy sector and other
sectors moved from negative to positive following Russia’s 2022 invasion of Ukraine. This shift
indicates a change in investor preferences toward stocks that provide protection, or act as
hedges, during periods of elevated geopolitical risk.
Pricing Geopolitical Risk in Option Markets
Geopolitical risks are also reflected in option markets, particularly in equity put options that
provide downside protection against stock price declines. Premiums for downside risk
```
protection (put options) and for tail risk protection (deep out-of-the-money put options)
```
typically rise sharply during major geopolitical shocks.
For example, put option premiums increased sharply after Russia’s invasion of Ukraine—not
only for Russian firms but also for European firms with revenue or subsidiary exposure to
Russia and Ukraine. Similarly, United States–China trade tensions during the 2018–2019 tariff
announcements led to higher option premiums in both countries, but this increase did not
spill over meaningfully to other global markets, as shown in Figures 103.2 and 103.3.
Premiums for tail risk protection rose the most, reflecting heightened investor concerns about
extreme losses.
```
Figure 103.2: Change (%) in Downside Risk (Protection Premium) Around Tariff Announcement
```
Weeks2
```
Figure 103.3: Change (%) in Downside Tail Risk (Protection Premium) Around Tariff
```

Implications of Geopolitical Risk for the Resilience of Financial
Institutions
LO 103.f: Describe the implications of geopolitical risk exposure for the resilience
of banks and nonbank financial institutions, including effects on equity valuations,
lending, and investment fund flows.
Effects on Banks and Nonbank Financial Institutions
Both bank and nonbank financial institutions face elevated market, credit, and liquidity risks
during geopolitical conflicts due to rising market volatility and heightened macroeconomic
uncertainty. Increased volatility can trigger selloffs that erode asset and collateral values,
leading to tighter lending conditions. Capital outflows may further amplify volatility,
particularly in less liquid markets. These vulnerabilities can be compounded by the effects of
sanctions, capital controls, and the growing frequency of cyberattacks.
As of 2024, financial institutions’ exposure to markets with elevated geopolitical risk had
increased markedly, accounting for about 8% of total cross-border bank claims, 10% of total
cross-border bank liabilities, and 13% of equity fund assets. At the same time, banks have
reduced their direct exposure to Russia and Ukraine over the past decade following Russia’s
2014 annexation of Crimea. Investment funds also cut their direct exposure by roughly 60%
after Russia’s 2022 invasion of Ukraine and lowered their indirect exposure through asset
holdings linked to these two countries.
Effects on Equity Valuations and Lending
Bank lending and financial stability are also negatively affected by heightened geopolitical
shocks and weakening borrower creditworthiness. These conditions can lead to reduced
cross-border lending, declines in asset valuations and bank equity, and greater rollover risk on
foreign debt.
Effects on Investment Fund Flows
Investment funds with significant exposure to countries involved in military conflicts are
particularly vulnerable to redemption pressures and valuation declines. According to the IMF,
bond funds with 10% exposure to conflict-affected countries experienced a 2.3% drop in
asset flows and a 1% decline in returns, while equity funds saw smaller effects. Following
Russia’s 2022 invasion of Ukraine, funds with a 10% direct exposure to these markets lost
about 6% in cumulative returns within one week and 8% over the next six months. By
contrast, United States–China tariff announcements between 2018 and 2024 did not lead to
meaningful declines in fund returns.
The broader financial system can be destabilized if institutions lack sufficient capital and
liquidity buffers, increasing the risk of contagion across markets. To strengthen resilience
against large but plausible losses from geopolitical events, banks and investment funds
should maintain adequate buffers, avoid concentrated exposures, and enhance their risk
management practices. Policymakers should also ensure that regulatory frameworks account
for geopolitical risks and help safeguard financial stability during periods of heightened global
stress.
MODULE QUIZ 103.2
1. Which of the following types of economies is most vulnerable to rising sovereign CDS spreads
following a geopolitical shock?
A. Economies with a large defense sector.
B. Commodity exporters with weak reserves.
C. Emerging market economies with low fiscal buffers.
D. Advanced economies with moderate trade ties to impacted countries.
2. Following a major geopolitical risk event, investment funds are most likely to experience:
A. increased inflows from retail investors.
B. redemption pressures and forced asset sales.
C. improved liquidity due to central bank support.
D. minimal net asset value changes due to diversification.
KEY CONCEPTS
LO 103.a
Geopolitical risk arises from wars, armed conflicts, terrorism, and trade tensions that can
disrupt international relations and economic activity. These risks influence asset prices and
financial stability through two main channels:
1. The economic channel operates through disruptions to trade, supply chains, capital flows,
and production, which can depress asset prices, raise sovereign risk premiums, and weaken
overall economic growth.
2. The market sentiment channel reflects heightened uncertainty, increased risk aversion, and
reduced investor confidence, which can lower valuations and increase market volatility.
Effective policy measures are essential for mitigating these risks. Countries can strengthen
resilience by improving regulatory frameworks, maintaining fiscal and monetary flexibility, and
deepening financial markets. Financial institutions should also hold adequate capital and
liquidity buffers to absorb potential losses associated with major geopolitical risk events.
LO 103.b
Geopolitical risk events affect countries, sectors, and asset classes differently. Countries
directly involved in conflicts or closely linked to affected regions tend to face the most severe
consequences, including disruptions to trade and investment. Commodity exporters often
benefit from higher energy prices following major shocks, while commodity importers
typically experience currency depreciation, rising sovereign CDS spreads, and greater financial
pressure.
Sectoral impacts are similarly uneven. The defense and energy sectors usually gain as military
spending increases and commodity prices rise. In contrast, the banking, retail, and utilities
sectors tend to decline due to weaker demand, tighter credit conditions, and valuation
pressures.
Asset classes also react distinctly. Commodities such as oil and gold generally increase in
value, while equity markets fall, especially in emerging economies. Safe-haven sovereign
bonds often gain, resulting in lower yields.
The severity of the impact depends on the nature of the event. Wars and prolonged military
conflicts produce deeper and more persistent economic and financial effects than sanctions or
short-lived disruptions.
LO 103.c
Stock market responses to geopolitical events vary widely and depend on the severity and
duration of the shock. Large cross-border conflicts, such as Russia’s invasion of Ukraine and
the United States–China trade tensions, tend to generate much larger and more persistent
equity market declines than typical geopolitical events. Cross-border spillovers arise through
trade, revenue, and financial linkages, causing countries that are geographically close or
economically connected to experience sharper declines. These linkages also transmit effects
to exposed firms, especially those with subsidiaries, significant revenue shares, or major
shareholders in affected regions.
LO 103.d
Sovereign risk premiums tend to rise following major geopolitical shocks, particularly in
emerging markets and commodity-importing countries. Increases in sovereign CDS spreads
after such events signal heightened concerns about fiscal sustainability and external
vulnerabilities. Countries with high debt-to-GDP ratios, limited fiscal or reserve buffers, and
weaker institutional quality face larger increases in sovereign CDS spreads and are, therefore,
more exposed to geopolitical risks.
LO 103.e
Investors price geopolitical risk into both equity and option markets through risk premiums.
Assets that lose value when geopolitical risks rise require positive risk premiums, while assets
that tend to gain value during periods of elevated geopolitical stress often exhibit negative
premiums.
```
Geopolitical risk exposure is commonly measured using geopolitical risk (GPR) betas, which
```
differ across sectors. Energy and defense stocks typically have positive GPR betas because
they benefit from higher prices or spending during conflicts, while consumer-focused sectors
generally have negative GPR betas due to weaker demand.
Option markets also reflect geopolitical risk, particularly through put option pricing. Premiums
for downside protection—and more notably, for tail risk protection—tend to rise sharply
during major geopolitical shocks, signaling heightened concerns about extreme losses.
LO 103.f
Geopolitical shocks raise market, credit, and liquidity risks across banks, nonbanks, and
investment funds by increasing volatility and macro uncertainty, which can trigger selloffs,
falling asset prices, and tighter lending conditions. Stress is often amplified by capital
outflows, especially in less liquid markets, and can be worsened by sanctions, capital
controls, and cyber risks.
Higher geopolitical risk can also weaken borrower credit quality, reduce cross-border lending,
and increase rollover and funding risks, particularly for balance sheets reliant on foreign
currency debt or unstable external funding. For investment funds, exposure to conflict
affected markets can lead to valuation losses and redemption pressure, increasing the risk of
spillovers and cross market contagion.

alone are not sufficient, because institutions also need robust scenario analysis and

firms typically do not face heightened downside risk during these events, and revenue
```

READING 104
MONETARY AND FISCAL POLICY:
SAFEGUARDING STABILITY AND TRUST
Study Session 15
EXAM FOCUS
This reading focuses on the channels, boundaries, and limits of fiscal and monetary policy.
For the exam, understand the different channels through which fiscal and monetary policy
operate. Also, be aware of policy challenges and how the region of stability functions. Be able
to describe the evolution of policy breaches from the 1960s to the COVID-19 pandemic while
```
contrasting the experience between advanced economies (AEs) and emerging market
```
```
economies (EMEs). In addition, know the three primary challenges associated with the
```
interconnectedness between fiscal and monetary policy. Finally, be able to identify healthy
policy boundaries for fiscal and monetary policy.
MODULE 104.1: MONETARY AND FISCAL POLICY
Channels of Policy Influence
LO 104.a: Compare and contrast the channels through which fiscal policy and
monetary policy influence a country’s economic activity and financial markets,
and define the “region of stability” in terms of their joint policy stances.
The two core economic functions of the state are fiscal and monetary policy. These functions
play a critical role in ensuring trust in policymakers and economic stability. Fiscal policy
focuses on the federal government’s authority to raise money through taxes or borrowing and
then spend money in pursuit of objectives. Monetary policy controls the money supply and
policy interest rates.
There is a natural overlap between these two types of policies. If the central bank uses
monetary policy to increase interest rates, then federal borrowing costs rise, the economy
slows, and tax revenues decrease. If monetary policy items produce positive revenues, then
those revenues flow through to the federal government and support fiscal spending. Too
much fiscal policy stimulus can produce inflation, which requires a monetary policy response.
Fiscal and monetary policies operate through different channels to influence economic
activity. Both can lead to economic expansion or contraction, depending on which actions are
taken in the respective channels:
Fiscal policy channels. Fiscal policy has a direct impact on the economy through federal
spending, tax policy, the production of some services, and transfer payments to individuals
and businesses.
Monetary policy channels. Monetary policy manages the money supply through open
market transactions and policy interest rates, which spill over into bond yields, borrowing
costs, asset prices, and exchange rates.
The overlapping nature of these two policy channels creates an environment that could foster
financial stability, but it could also undermine this goal under the wrong circumstances. The
safe zone, where stability is maintained, is often referred to as the region of stability.
Within this region, the economy is growing and healthy.
There are several challenges that should be understood:
The boundaries of the region of stability vary by country.
It is difficult to precisely quantify the region in a single metric.
The shape, size, and locus of the region are constantly changing.
```
Some changes occur gradually (e.g., demographic shifts or financial innovations).
```
```
Other changes occur rapidly (e.g., a commodity price spike due to a geopolitical event or a
```
```
sudden-onset pandemic).
```
Regulation and foreign exchange rate interventions can alter this region.
The boundaries of the region of stability can easily get blurred. For this reason, there should
always be a margin of safety. In other words, fiscal policy should not be pushed to the outer
limit of stability—it should stop short of what is expected to be the limits to allow for
```
unexpected outcomes. For example, prolonged periods of monetary policy expansion (e.g.,
```
```
ultra-low interest rates) can encourage the government to build excessive debt. Too much
```
```
debt can lead to inflation, which hinders the economy; therefore, moderation is important.
```
Testing boundaries can trigger painful outcomes in the economy.
Consequences of Violating the Region of Stability
LO 104.b: Describe the consequences of breaching the boundaries of the region of
stability, and how these consequences have evolved over time in advanced
economies and in emerging market economies.
Before the COVID-19 pandemic, monetary and fiscal policy were already pushing the
boundaries of the region of stability. Monetary policy had promoted low interest rates and a
```
ballooning central bank balance sheet in the decades since the Global Financial Crisis (GFC)
```
of 2007–2009. Fiscal policy has recently been characterized by excessive public debt and
persistently high annual deficits. This confluence of policy actions leaves a small margin for
```
error and makes the economy more prone to economic shocks, high price levels (i.e.,
```
```
inflation), and financial fragility. There has perhaps been overconfidence regarding how far
```
the boundaries could be stretched.
```
Among advanced economies (AEs), the pre-pandemic period can be subdivided into two
```
```
phases: (1) the 1960s through the mid-1980s and (2) the mid-1980s through the pandemic.
```
```
Phase 1 (1960s to mid-1980s). Rising inflation was the indicator that forced policy actions
```
```
to test the boundaries of the region of stability. There was a belief that policymakers (i.e.,
```
```
politicians and central bankers) could fine-tune the economy through fiscal and monetary
```
```
channels; the ideal goal was the stability of unemployment and inflation. The result of
```
policy actions in this period was very high inflation, which was fought with very high policy
rates. This period led to a surge in public debt and a mindset shift to prioritize price
```
stability (i.e., inflation).
```
```
Phase 2 (mid-1980s to the pandemic). Financial systems shifted from being government
```
led to market led, which is known as financial liberalization. Globalization also accelerated
```
during this time period. Cheaper labor in emerging market economies (EMEs) helped
```
propel a wave of decreased inflation—and as a result, testing the boundaries of the region
of stability shifted from excessive inflation to risk-taking behavior and financial imbalances
```
(e.g., outsized expansion in credit and asset prices). Inflation-induced recessions were
```
replaced by financial recessions, which had a longer-lasting impact on economic growth as
the economy suffered under a burden of higher public debt.
During Phase 2, monetary policy responded to economic contractions by lowering policy
rates. When the recovery arrived, monetary policy did not tighten because the economy was
```
expanding without the problem of higher inflation (partially as a result of globalization during
```
```
this period). A low interest rate regime enabled public debt to increase massively. This was
```
done to further stimulate economic growth because low interest rates were not increasing
inflation to targeted levels on their own.
After Phase 2, the COVID-19 pandemic arrived. Significant fiscal stimulus was offered to
```
combat the anticipated economic effects of government responses (e.g., lockdowns) to the
```
pandemic. The economy rebounded rapidly, leading to elevated inflation levels. This surge in
inflation was combined with historically high public and private debt levels and elevated asset
prices—all of which increased financial vulnerability.
Factors contributing to the trajectory of monetary and fiscal stimulus include the following:
1.Loss of policy traction as boundaries of stability were approached. Policy rate changes have
```
less impact on aggregate demand when rates are low. Larger adjustments (e.g., 50 or 75
```
```
basis point moves) are then needed to have an impact. Fiscal stimulus also becomes less
```
powerful when public debt levels are high because stimulus sustainability and default risk
begin to enter the conversation. The region of stability is narrowed when low policy rates
have encouraged high risk-taking and debt buildup.
2.Self-reinforcing interactions between the two types of policy. Lower policy rates reduce
fiscal borrowing constraints and encourage higher public debt loads. Post-pandemic, central
bank holdings of government debt have also soared for AEs. Higher public debt levels make
```
monetary normalization (e.g., interest rate levels and central bank balance sheets) more
```
challenging. Lower rates are sought to help minimize public debt servicing costs.
The outcome in EMEs has some common experiences shared with AEs. One similarity is that
financial liberalization increased the incidence of financial cycles and banking crises, including
the Southern Cone crisis in Latin America in the early 1980s, the Tequila crisis in Mexico in
1994, and the Asian crisis in the late 1990s. Broadly speaking, inflation remained low due to
the effects of globalization, but political and institutional effects created different experiences
across the spectrum of EMEs. For example, while Asia enjoyed relatively low inflation, Latin
America experienced hyperinflation in the 1980s and 1990s, which was often paired with
sovereign debt defaults.
In general, EMEs are more sensitive to global financial conditions because their financial
markets are less developed, and they rely more on foreign exchange funding sources. The
level of reliance—and, therefore, the degree of impact—varies across individual EMEs. There
are four broad implications of foreign exchange dependency:
1.Region of stability implications. The region of stability for EMEs is much smaller than it is
for AEs. This means that the margin for error is also smaller. Stepping outside the
```
boundaries will produce a rapid (and potentially dramatic) response. The speed and
```
intensity of boundary breaches can force a rapid reversal in policy.
2.Symptoms of boundary breaches. Boundary breaches often lead to capital outflows and
currency depreciation. Exchange rate dependency can cause currency crises. Over time,
there has been a shift to more flexible exchange rate goals and inflation-targeting
frameworks.
3.Impact of AE monetary policy. Due to the exchange rate dependency of EMEs, monetary
```
policy in AEs (especially in the United States) can have a big impact. A U.S. policy rate
```
change can adjust exchange rates in an unfavorable way for EMEs and cause domestic
economic growth concerns. Flexible exchange rate goals for EMEs can potentially help
mitigate this impact.
4.Exposure to global financial conditions. As financial crises become more global in nature,
EMEs become more focused than ever on flexible exchange rate policies and inflation-
targeting regimes.
By focusing on flexible exchange rates and inflation-targeting policies, EMEs are enhancing
shock resilience and deploying countercyclical policies when economic times get tough. For
example, Latin America tightened monetary policy earlier and more forcefully during the
COVID-19 pandemic than its AE counterparts. These improvements allow policy constraints to
be relaxed, which widens the region of stability and potentially introduces other risks.
MODULE QUIZ 104.1
1. Which of the following statements is most likely a challenge associated with the region of
stability?
A. The region is the same in all countries.
B. The size of the region is persistent over time.
C. The shape of the region is persistent over time.
D. Regulation and foreign exchange rate interventions can both alter this region.
2. Which of the following statements is correct regarding the interaction between fiscal and
monetary policy?
A. Lower policy rates increase constraints on fiscal policy options.
B. Fiscal policy increases its impact as sovereign debt levels increase.
C. Monetary policy needs fewer extreme actions to have an impact when sovereign debt levels
are high.
D. Monetary policy needs more extreme actions to have an impact when sovereign debt levels
are high.
3. Which of the following statements is correct regarding the implications of foreign exchange rate
```
dependency for emerging market economies (EMEs)?
```
A. Boundary breaches lead to currency appreciation and capital outflows.
B. EMEs are trying to solve exchange rate issues with timelier monetary stimulus.
C. Monetary policy changes in advanced economies (AEs) can have a large ripple effect in EMEs.
D. Higher economic growth translates into a higher margin for error with the boundaries of the
region of stability.
MODULE 104.2: RISKS ASSOCIATED WITH HIGH PUBLIC DEBT
LO 104.c: Describe the risks that global economies face as a result of high public
debt levels, including the potential for these high debt levels, in combination with
other factors, to drive tension between fiscal policy and monetary policy.
There is a natural tension between fiscal and monetary policy with respect to managing the
balance between supporting price stability and mitigating financial stability risks. The three
primary challenges, which will be expanded on in this section, are as follows:
1. Public finances are strained by the intersection of record-high public debt, the pressure to
maintain spending, higher interest rate levels, and slowing future growth prospects.
2. High public debt loads can work against monetary policy tightening when combating
inflation.
3. The level of public debt introduces financial stability risks.
Intersection of Multiple Factors
Government spending, government debt loads, interest rates, and inflation are connected
events. Post-GFC, high public debt levels have resulted in decreasing sovereign credit ratings.
This decline has been more dramatic in AEs, but it is still present in EMEs. At the same time,
inflation has caused fiscal balances to look better than reality. Nominal prices and wages
respond fairly quickly to inflation, which has an impact on nominal tax revenues.
There is pressure to cut fiscal spending, but this is problematic because:
Rising costs naturally result in higher spending.
Inflation creates a need for higher federal wages and transfer payments.
Age-related expenditures are expected to increase by 4%–5% of GDP over the next 20
years.
The global push for sustainability is very costly for fiscal budgets.
An increase in geopolitical tensions will require an increase in defense spending.
If inflation levels remain stubbornly high, then higher policy rates will need to be maintained.
The following factors amplify the effect of policy rates on public borrowing:
1.Impact of central bank balance sheet expansion. Post-GFC, central bank purchases in major
AEs rose to 25%–50% of outstanding government debt. This central bank monetization
shortened the maturity of public debt. The shortened duration reduced borrowing costs and
lowered remittances from central banks to federal governments.
2.Impact on economic growth. Historically high fiscal debt burdens increase the cost of debt
service in annual federal budgets, which is estimated to grow to 6% of GDP. The problems
from high public debt could be further amplified if medium-term economic growth
```
disappoints. Structural factors (e.g., slower labor force growth and slowdowns in EMEs)
```
could play a role in lowering growth forecasts.
Two further points are useful for understanding this issue. First, theinterest-growth
```
differential is the difference between policy rates and economic (i.e., GDP) growth. When
```
rates are below the growth rate, as they were pre-pandemic, public finances can thrive—but
when rates rise above growth, the economy can slow, which produces a natural challenge for
```
public spending. Second, the monetary tightening (i.e., raising policy rates) needed to fight
```
persistent inflation will also raise the cost of federal borrowing and constrain economic
growth. History shows that higher inflation leads to higher public debt.
Countercyclical Effects
```
Monetary and fiscal policies are sometimes countercyclical (i.e., they work in opposite
```
```
directions). Fiscal deficits remain large in many countries. This is done to support aggregate
```
demand, but it causes higher inflation. The normal reaction to higher inflation is higher policy
```
rates (i.e., monetary policy contraction), but this can weaken the economy, which is
```
countercyclical to the aim of fiscal policy. This becomes more acute the longer that inflation
remains high.
The impact of fiscal stimulus on inflation is more pronounced for EMEs because of their
higher reliance on exchange rates. When exchange rates depreciate, the effect is higher
domestic prices, which naturally leads to ongoing higher inflation and slower economic
growth rates.
High debt levels can also prevent monetary policy officials from taking their desired actions.
Fiscal dominance exists when monetary policy is constrained due to fiscally imposed
constraints. This situation can arise due to the following reasons:
1.Political spending pressure. There is often pressure to maintain or increase spending in
support of fiscal priorities. This can place a floor on fiscal flexibility unless priorities are
adjusted.
```
2.Sovereign debt implications. Interest rate increases (i.e., monetary policy actions) could
```
spark a sovereign debt crisis under certain circumstances.
It is important to maintain strong institutional safeguards protecting the independence of
monetary policy so that fiscal policymakers do not hijack monetary policy to accomplish fiscal
priorities.
Financial Stability Risks
Sovereign debt vulnerability passes through to the financial system. This happens for the
following two reasons:
1.Direct financial system exposure. Sovereign debt helps maintain a well-functioning financial
system. Sovereign debt provides a store of value and facilitates economic transactions by
serving as collateral. It is widely seen as the most liquid asset and helps price risk for other
```
financial assets. Sovereign credit risk events (e.g., downgrades and defaults) can spill over
```
into the broad pricing of risk in an economy.
2.Government is the ultimate backstop. The government serves as the financial backstop for
its economy and the solvency of the financial system. The central bank often acts as the
lender of last resortand the market maker of last resort. Banks likely have higher credit
ratings with this government backstop than without it.
There is a potentially costly feedback loop that exists between banks and governments
because banks rely on the government for liquidity and sovereign bonds that are used as
collateral in transactions. A decrease in credit rating at one entity could spark an issue at the
other and then reinforce a downward spiral. This is most pronounced in EMEs.
High sovereign debt can increase the likelihood of sharp bond yield moves simply due to the
sheer size of the debt. When monetary policy tightens, long-term yields generally rise more in
countries with high debt burdens. The ultra-low interest rates enjoyed in the pre-pandemic
period incentivized governments to issue long-term debt to lock in low rates. The long-term
fixed-income assets are most volatile when policy rates change. The risk of sharp yield
adjustments can also result from liquidity events when investors are so fearful that they
would rather own cash than sovereign debt.
The financial sector is much more vulnerable following periods of loose fiscal and monetary
policies. If investors estimate that the boundaries of the region of stability are close to being
breached, then sovereign bond prices could be repriced rapidly and aggressively. This could
spill over into the entire financial system due to their close relationship.
Policy Implications
Because monetary and fiscal policy serve complementary roles in maintaining financial
stability, greater focus needs to be placed on staying within the region of stability. Monetary
policy should remain focused on price stability because the fix for high inflation can be very
costly for an economy. To support this goal, fiscal policy needs to be as conservative as
possible to allow headroom to handle crises while not putting monetary policymakers in
```
difficult situations; this temperate approach should be an ongoing goal. Relying on inflation to
```
lessen sovereign debt burdens is counterproductive because high inflation would severely
weaken the economy. Monetary policymakers need to tolerate inflation that is below target,
while remaining watchful for signs of overheating.
Central banks should maintain an ongoing policy of minimizing their balance sheets for the
following reasons:
1. It would limit the central bank’s footprint on the economy and avoid crowding out private
lenders.
2. It would reduce federal budget reliance on transfers from the central bank.
3. It would give the central bank more flexibility to temporarily expand its balance sheet to
deal with future crisis periods.
Viewpoints need to change regarding the structure of fiscal spending. High-quality public
spending that encourages productivity and growth gains is the highest goal. Investments in
items like infrastructure and education can help the economy receive the fuel that it needs.
Transfer payments and entitlement spending programs have a place, but they should not be
prioritized over growth-enabling strategies.
In terms of oversight, it is important that central banks remain independent. This helps to
```
prevent the politicization of monetary policy. There should also be some guardrails (e.g., fiscal
```
```
rules or oversight panels) to help ensure that fiscal spending remains focused on productivity
```
and growth-enhancing strategies that support a strong economy. Thoughtful, but not overly
restrictive, regulations can also play a role in the process of encouraging policy to stay within
the region of stability.
MODULE QUIZ 104.2
1. Which of the following statements is least accurate regarding how sovereign debt vulnerability
can pass through to the financial system?
A. Federal backstops hurt bank credit ratings.
B. Central banks serve as a lender of last resort.
C. Central banks serve as a market maker of last resort.
D. Sovereign debt often facilitates economic transactions by serving as collateral.
2. Which of the following statements is a reason why central banks should limit their balance sheet
expansion? Limiting the balance sheet will:
A. maintain flexibility to deal with crisis periods.
B. increase competition in private lending markets.
C. shorten the duration of outstanding federal debt.
D. increase federal budget reliance on transfers from the central bank.
KEY CONCEPTS
LO 104.a
Fiscal and monetary policies are two overlapping functions of a central government. They
operate through separate channels:
Fiscal policy channels. Fiscal policy has a direct impact on the economy through federal
spending, tax policy, the production of some services, and transfer payments to individuals
and businesses.
Monetary policy channels. Monetary policy manages the money supply through open
market transactions and policy interest rates, which spill over into bond yields, borrowing
costs, asset prices, and exchange rates.
Policies that both produce stable inflation and stable economic growth are said to operate in
the region of stability. This region changes in shape and size over time, and its boundaries
can easily become blurry.
LO 104.b
```
Among advanced economies (AEs), the pre-pandemic period can be subdivided into two time
```
```
blocks:
```
The 1960s to mid-1980s. Policymakers believed that they could fine-tune the economy
through fiscal and monetary policy. Fiscal policy produced high inflation, which was fought
with high policy rates. There was a surge in public debt during this period.
The mid-1980s to the pandemic. Financial liberalization and globalization were both very
prominent forces. Lower inflation was the result, and that led to a prolonged period of low
interest rates, which induced both higher public borrowing and financial recessions.
A few factors contribute to the trajectory of monetary and fiscal stimulus. First, when
sovereign debt levels are high, fiscal stimulus has less stimulative effect, and more extreme
monetary efforts are needed to have an impact. Second, there is a self-reinforcing interaction
between fiscal and monetary policy.
```
Due to foreign exchange rate dependency, emerging market economies (EMEs) are more
```
sensitive to global financial conditions than AEs. Implications of this foreign exchange rate
dependency include the following:
1. The region of stability is smaller for EMEs than AEs.
2. Boundary breaches amplify currency depreciation and capital outflows.
3. Monetary policy in AEs can have a ripple effect in EMEs.
4. EMEs are shifting to flexible exchange rate regimes.
LO 104.c
Record-high sovereign debt levels strain public finances. This is compounded by the pressure
for government spending, the impact on inflation, the resulting higher interest rates, and the
follow-on financial stability risks.
Higher policy rates impact public borrowing and the economy in several ways. First, central
bank balance sheet expansion can shorten the duration of outstanding federal debt because
central banks often purchase debt from the long end of the curve to bring down rates.
Second, central bank balance sheet expansion can reduce remittances to the federal
government because the higher-yielding bonds are removed from the market. Third, higher
debt service costs and crowding out private borrowers can reduce economic growth rates.
The interest-growth differential is an important metric.
Fiscal dominance occurs when monetary policy is constrained by fiscal policy actions. The
pressure on the government to spend can lead to inflation, which requires higher policy rates.
However, the higher policy rates should slow the economy and work against fiscal stimulus
goals.
Periods of loose fiscal and monetary policies create higher risks for the financial system. Both
policy tools should be saved for the times when a crisis occurs naturally and assistance is
needed. Otherwise, these tools could tip the economy into difficulty by pushing the
boundaries of the region of stability.

or foreign exchange rates can also alter the region. (LO 104.a)

extreme monetary policy movements. Lower policy rates reduce fiscal constraints and
```

```

for EMEs than AEs, which creates a smaller margin for error. Boundary breaches amplify
currency depreciation and capital outflows. EMEs are shifting to flexible exchange rate
```
regimes to combat their foreign exchange rate dependency. (LO 104.b)
```

last resort. One result of this backstop is that banks will have higher credit ratings than
```
they otherwise would enjoy. (LO 104.c)

sheet will likely prevent crowding out private credit markets, lengthen the duration of
government debts, reduce transfer payments from the central bank to the federal

```

The following is a review of the Current Issues in Financial Markets principles designed to address the learning objectives set
```
