READING 105
REGULATING THE CRYPTO ECOSYSTEM: THE
CASE OF UNBACKED CRYPTO ASSETS
Study Session 15
EXAM FOCUS
This reading highlights the importance of developing a robust and comprehensive regulatory
framework for the evolving cryptocurrency sector, with an emphasis on continuous oversight
and domestic and international collaboration. For the exam, understand how regulation can
improve market integrity and trust as well as which participants are the key focus of
```
regulations (including crypto asset exchanges, wallet providers, and centralized entities).
```
Also, be familiar with the opportunities and risks related to crypto technologies, how crypto
assets can lead to systemic risks, and why prudential regulation focuses on a targeted
approach rather than broad restrictions. This reading also explains the critical role of global
cooperation in mitigating risks and fostering a uniform regulatory landscape.
MODULE 105.1: CRYPTO ASSET CATEGORIES AND COMPONENTS
Crypto Asset Categories
LO 105.a: Define and describe crypto assets, including the categories broadly used
by global financial regulators to classify them.
Crypto assets are a form of digital assets that use cryptography to secure information and
```
rely on technologies like distributed ledger technology (DLT) to record transactions. DLT
```
broadly refers to a sequenced and standardized record of an activity that is cryptographically
secured on a network that is typically decentralized. It is calleddistributed because the record
```
is made available (i.e., distributed) simultaneously to all network participants. The best-
```
known form of DLT is blockchain, which consists of a chain of blocks. Each block contains
the records of multiple transactions and is linked to the previous block. Records are generally
immutable, meaning they cannot be altered.
Crypto assets can be backed by another asset or unbacked. Unbacked crypto assets do
not rely on the backing of another asset, and their value is determined through supply and
demand. They are not considered money because they are generally not used as a medium
of exchange, store of value, or unit of account. Unbacked crypto assets typically exhibit high
volatility and have low intrinsic value, and they are often used for speculative activities.
There is no single internationally accepted classification of crypto assets. However, there are
six broad categories based on taxonomy and risks, as shown in Figure 105.1.
Figure 105.1: Categories of Crypto Assets
The remainder of this reading will focus on unbacked crypto assets, security tokens, utility
tokens, and stablecoins.
Crypto Ecosystem Components
LO 105.b: Evaluate the key components within the crypto ecosystem, the
potential risks generated by these components, and potential regulatory
responses to address those risks.
Key components of the crypto ecosystem include the various entities within it, the risks posed
```
by the activities of these entities, and crypto regulation. Entities are categorized as (1)
```
```
issuers; (2) crypto asset service providers; (3) wallet providers; (4) validators, miners, and
```
```
underlying technology; and (5) regulated financial institutions.
```
Issuers
Unbacked crypto issuance is often decentralized, which allows it to move away from central
control and provides anonymity, liquidity, and broad access to participants. However, these
same features of decentralization create risks that are difficult to address and adequately
monitor by regulatory authorities. Disclosure of the issuers of unbacked crypto assets is
limited, and determining the intended purpose of the assets or even the technology used can
be challenging. Further, failure risk can happen at any node, and networks are susceptible to
operational risk and cyberattacks.
Crypto asset issuance and distribution also create risks. Crypto assets can be issued through
```
pre-mining (i.e., batch sale of tokens or coins ahead of public distribution, usually to a small
```
```
group), continuous mining, or a hybrid approach. Distribution includes pre-token sale, initial
```
```
coin offerings (ICOs), mining, airdrops (i.e., an issuer sends tokens/coins to certain entities
```
```
for marketing), and forks. Pre-mining and ICOs expose participants to price manipulation and
```
pump-and-dump schemes.
Crypto asset marketing materials, including white papers that contain information on its
developers, objectives, and technical data, are often incomplete or inaccurate and may
deliberately overstate benefits and understate risks. Regulatory response to these risks
should focus on improving the quality of white paper information, the notification process,
and marketing disclosures, using suitability tests. Regulatory elements include the following:
White papers. The language used in white papers and disclosures needs to be clear,
accurate, and easy to understand. It should provide the necessary details about the crypto
asset, including why the asset was issued, the technology used, and potential risks.
Notification process. Regulators may want to be notified when a white paper is available in
order to understand the crypto asset parameters and monitor risks. They may choose to
```
only require notification of major developments (e.g., building a framework) or when the
```
```
issuance is small or restricted (e.g., NFTs).
```
Suitability. Regulation can also require crypto asset issuers to conduct suitability tests to
assess whether the crypto asset is appropriate for retail users.
Marketing. Regulatory focus should also ensure fair and accurate marketing by issuers.
Marketing materials should be clear and simple and should contain the information
available in the white paper. Some jurisdictions require the regulator to approve marketing
materials for crypto assets, while in others the marketing of riskier assets like unregulated
crypto assets is prohibited.
Consumer education. Regulatory initiatives should incorporate consumer education efforts
as well as financial and technology literacy programs.
Crypto Asset Exchanges
Crypto asset exchanges play an important role in the digital currency ecosystem by
facilitating the buying and selling of cryptocurrencies, often providing services beyond those
of traditional securities exchanges. The largest exchanges are typically centralized
intermediaries, which foster trust in a centralized counterparty but hinder innovation in the
decentralized space. Unlike securities exchanges, crypto exchanges often allow direct access
to retail users, trade from their own inventory to provide liquidity, and offer custody services.
In particular, while custody services can expedite off-chain settlements, they expose users to
cyberattacks as well as credit and counterparty risk that can result in losses.
The unique features of exchanges, including trading their own inventory and combining
exchange and custody services, expose them to operational and cyber risks. Despite
improvements in operational and cyber resilience, successful cyber hacks have resulted in the
bankruptcy of some crypto asset providers and caused substantial financial losses for users
who were unable to access their crypto assets during stressed markets.
Efforts by exchanges to mitigate these risks include buying cyber insurance, using cold
```
wallets (i.e., offline storage) for tokens, and establishing compensation funds. These
```
```
measures have varying levels of effectiveness (given the variability in the level and terms of
```
```
cyber coverage), the known difficulty by users of converting crypto assets to fiat currency,
```
and the need to have online connectivity to access crypto assets held in cold wallets.
Additionally, the crypto market is relatively new, usually illiquid, and has less sophisticated
intermediaries, resulting in market integrity risks due to market manipulation from various
trading schemes. Exchanges often also have very large crypto asset holdings, giving them
```
control when dealing with networks that use a proof-of-stake (PoS) consensus mechanism.
```
Enforcing regulations against market manipulation is difficult because transactions are usually
anonymous and done on decentralized platforms across multiple jurisdictions.
Regulatory frameworks for crypto asset exchanges vary globally due to the small size of local
markets and the differences in regulatory priorities, and many jurisdictions have not yet
developed regulations for crypto asset exchanges. Regulation often focuses only on lower-risk
security tokens rather than unbacked security tokens. Exchanges can therefore circumvent
regulatory oversight by only listing unbacked security tokens.
```
The International Organization of Securities Commissions (IOSCO) has outlined a regulatory
```
framework for crypto asset platforms. This framework focuses on the following regulatory
```
requirements:
```
Prudent governance of platform operators, promoting rules for control functions,
operations, and capital
Regulated access to crypto platforms, protecting users and ensuring orderly trading
Robust controls and processes to protect against key risks, including cyber hacks
Strengthened market integrity, helping to reduce the risk of manipulation and conflicts of
interest
Improved transparency to enhance trading efficiency, integrity, and information
```
Anti-money laundering (AML)/combating the financing of terrorism (CFT) rules, promoting
```
compliance with domestic and international rules
Clear rules relating to product offering criteria that help authorities determine which assets
should be regulated
Mitigating conflicts of interest through strong governance frameworks
Better regulation of off-chain transactions
Wallet Providers
Digital wallets store the private keys needed to access crypto assets owned by users. Losing
a wallet means losing the private key and therefore the assets. Users can hold the wallets
```
themselves (i.e., self-custody) or use a third-party custodian (i.e., custodial wallets), often a
```
crypto asset exchange or another service provider. Custodial wallets reduce the risk of loss
but introduce other risks, including cyberattacks and losing control of the assets.
```
Wallets can be “hot” (stored online) or “cold” (stored offline). Most users and exchanges
```
prefer cold wallets for long-term asset storage, which protect against cyber risks but are
susceptible to physical loss or damage, and even theft in rare cases. Hot wallets facilitate
faster transactions but are subject to cyberattacks, especially since third parties often store
the private keys of many users in wallets, making them attractive to hackers.
Wallet security is critical for safeguarding and safekeeping of funds and protecting the
security of the system. Regulation should align with best practices in cryptography and focus
on key protection and lifecycle management controls. Regulatory frameworks for crypto
assets focus on the following factors:
Segregation and safekeeping. Wallet providers should segregate user funds and crypto
assets from the provider’s own funds and assets. Reusing or lending client assets should
require user consent.
Operational and cyber resilience. Providers need robust cybersecurity frameworks to
safeguard custodied assets. Effective incident management and prompt reporting of
operational or cyber incidents can reduce risks.
Record keeping. Wallet providers rely on DLT to improve processes. Regulation should
ensure that these processes are secure, resilient, timely, and accurate.
AML/CFT compliance. Wallet providers should comply with AML/CFT standards.
Reporting and regulation. Authorities may impose reporting and prudential regulations on
```
wallet providers, covering risk management; client asset protection; and minimum capital
```
and liquidity requirements.
Safeguards. Regulation should require that wallet providers maintain cyber insurance for a
designated proportion of their holdings.
Wind-down arrangements. Effective regulation of wind-down arrangements and regulating
third-party asset providers protect customers from fraud and reduce financial sector
contagion risks. However, private keys stored by third-party custodians can complicate legal
issues in bankruptcy due to the commingling of client and custodian funds.
Conflicts of interest. Stringent governance procedures can help reduce conflicts of interest,
especially when wallet providers are part of a group.
Validators, Miners, and Technology
Using DLT in financial services helps reduce operational and cyber risks, with immutable
transactions and a clear audit trail. Different forms of DLT, including private/permissioned or
public/permissionless, are subject to different risks.
The closed nature of private blockchains reduces interoperability and can lead to
fragmentation, concentration, and financial stability risks. On the other hand, while public
blockchains are more protected against single points of failure, they are exposed to other
failure risks related to user behavior. Cross-network interoperability is also a problem given
that most crypto assets can only operate on their own blockchain networks and may lack
fungibility, even when they are offered on separate networks.
```
Regulation typically aims to be technology neutral; however, this may create friction with
```
other policy objectives and may hinder future technological innovation.
Regulated Financial Institutions
Financial institutions’ direct and indirect exposure to crypto assets can be significant,
exposing the financial system to increased risk. Regulating exposures focuses on mitigating
```
risks, including proposals by the Basel Committee on Banking Supervision (BCBS) to
```
```
categorize banks’ asset holdings as either (1) lower-risk or (2) higher-risk (traditional) assets.
```
Lower-risk assets would have similar or lower risk weights compared to traditional assets,
```
while higher-risk assets would have significantly higher risk weights (further details are
```
```
provided in LO 105.c).
```
Direct exposure reflects the high volatility and potentially significant losses to regulated
financial institutions related to unbacked crypto assets, although the number of financial
institutions and the size of exposure is currently limited. Regulatory requirements focus on a
conservative approach to managing risks, including capital deductions and using higher risk
weights.
Indirect exposure can arise through derivatives, loans to crypto users, or extending other
services like cyber insurance to wallet providers. Regulated financial institutions should
carefully monitor these exposures.
Regulated financial institutions also include broker-dealers, asset managers, and insurers. In
recent years, institutional exposure to crypto assets has grown by extending their services or
product offerings relating to unbacked crypto assets. Regulatory oversight should focus on
enhanced monitoring of these risks.
Crypto Asset Provider Functions and Market Infrastructure
In traditional financial markets, market infrastructure tasks and functions are performed by a
```
large number of specialized entities, each responsible for a particular function (e.g., record
```
```
keeping, clearing, settlement, and custody). In crypto markets, tasks are often performed
```
globally by the same crypto asset provider, increasing operational risk. This can lead to
concentrated risks that have the potential to create broad market disruptions.
Regulation should first determine whether a crypto asset provider is deemed “systemic” and
```
should be designated as a financial market infrastructure (FMI), because these entities could
```
cause significant market disruptions. In the case of stablecoins, for example, factors in
determining the designation could be based on the number of users, transaction volume, risk
profile, and the level of interconnectedness with the broader financial system. The
designation alone does not fully mitigate risks. This is because crypto users could choose to
replace one crypto exchange with another, or move transactions “on-chain,” thereby
circumventing regulation. As a result, close regulatory supervision and oversight of crypto
service providers is paramount.
MODULE QUIZ 105.1
1. Which of the following justifications best supports the argument that unbacked crypto assets are
not money? Unbacked crypto assets:
A. rely on distributed ledger technology (DLT), which is inherently volatile.
B. have value that is not stable enough to support traditional functions of money.
C. are unsuitable for use as money because the records of crypto assets are immutable.
D. are not transferable and, therefore, would not be considered a medium of exchange.
2. Which of the following rationales best explains why regulating crypto asset issuers presents a
challenge for regulatory authorities?
A. Crypto asset issuers often operate in jurisdictions with no existing crypto regulations.
B. The high volatility of unbacked crypto assets makes them inherently risky investments.
C. The decentralized nature of unbacked crypto issuance complicates regulatory monitoring and
enforcement.
D. The technology underlying crypto assets is too complex for current regulatory frameworks to
address effectively.
3. The main regulatory approach to mitigate risks associated with crypto asset exchanges involves:
A. requiring exchanges to perform suitability tests of exchange participants.
B. developing a global regulatory framework that all exchanges must follow.
C. requiring the use of cold wallets for all exchanges to protect against cyber threats.
D. focusing on robust governance, regulated access, and strengthened market integrity.
MODULE 105.2: CRYPTO ASSET REGULATIONS
LO 105.c: Identify and describe some of the global approaches to the regulation of
unbacked crypto assets, including the BCBS’ proposed treatment of banks’
exposures to crypto assets.
Global Approaches to Regulation
Regulating unbacked crypto assets and crypto activities is often done on a national setting,
which may be sufficient and necessary if global regulation does not exist. National
approaches often differ, with some regulators focusing on high-level guidance only, while
others focus on bespoke regulation to address specific activities, including transfer and
safekeeping of assets and AML/CFT efforts. Some examples of the different national
approaches are as follows:
European Union. EU regulation primarily focuses on stablecoins, onshoring, and licensing of
issuing entities.
Switzerland. Switzerland has more progressive crypto regulation than most jurisdictions.
Regulatory work has focused on guidelines for initial token offerings, clarifying token
trading rules, asset segregation, and enabling ledger-based securities on a blockchain.
United Kingdom. Initial guidance on crypto assets focused on regulating security tokens,
but not utility and exchange tokens. Proposed future guidance is broader to also include
stablecoins, market infrastructure, and cryptocurrencies.
Albania. Regulatory activity has focused on the licensing, monitoring, and supervision of
crypto asset service providers. In 2020, Albania legalized crypto assets for trading
purposes.
Nigeria. Central bank guidelines deem crypto assets not to be legal tender and prohibit
regulated financial institutions from dealing with or facilitating payments in crypto assets.
Japan. Japanese regulation is more bespoke related to crypto asset providers, focusing on
asset segregation, operational risk, cyber risk, audit, and capital requirements. Since 2020,
stablecoin regulation has also been introduced.
Despite these national approaches, crypto activities often involve global markets, which can
make effective regulation and supervision challenging. In the absence of harmonized
regulation, trading crypto assets on unregulated exchanges can bypass sanctions and capital
flow restrictions, and crypto service providers can shift their services to less regulated
```
jurisdictions. They can also use virtual private networks (VPNs) to obscure their locations.
```
Bypassing regulations is often successful, including in jurisdictions where stringent crypto
regulation exists, but monitoring and enforcement is ineffective due to limited resources. For
example, both Egypt and Nigeria have strict restrictions, yet crypto assets and activities are
widespread. Further, while crypto trading has been banned in China since 2021, evidence
suggests significant mining activities.
```
Several international financial bodies, including the International Monetary Fund (IMF) and
```
the BCBS, have called for stronger international cooperation among regulatory authorities to
address these risks. Regulation should primarily focus on the economic functions of crypto
assets and their technical design. These bodies advocate that a blanket ban on crypto
activities is likely ineffective and would hinder innovation. Restricting only certain aspects of
```
crypto assets is considered a more effective solution in the short term; for example,
```
```
restricting derivatives related to crypto assets (Japan and the UK) or restricting the use of
```
crypto assets as payments for goods and services may be possible.
Regulating Bank Exposure to Crypto Assets
The BCBS aims to help standardize banks’ holdings of crypto assets given the high-risk nature
```
of these investments. As mentioned, crypto asset holdings are categorized as either (1)
```
```
lower-risk or (2) higher-risk (traditional) assets. Lower-risk assets include tokenized assets
```
and stablecoins, with tokenized assets having similar risk weights as traditional assets, while
stablecoins may benefit from lower risk weighting. Higher-risk traditional assets, which
include cryptocurrencies and unbacked crypto assets, would have more conservative
requirements, with the BCBS proposing a 1,250% risk weight for maximum long and short
positions.
LO 105.d: Examine the considerations and steps introduced by the Bali Fintech
```
Agenda (BFA) for developing a regulatory framework for crypto assets.
```
```
The Bali Fintech Agenda (BFA) is a global regulatory and supervisory framework for
```
regulators, jointly developed by the IMF and the World Bank. It sets out the standards for
addressing fintech risks related to crypto assets and technologies. The BFA encourages
regulators to ensure there is legal certainty relating to crypto assets in order to build investor
confidence. This is especially important because in many jurisdictions, investors do not have
sufficient rights.
The BFA proposes that domestic regulatory frameworks related to crypto assets should be
built around the following considerations:
Monitoring. Regulators should monitor new developments in the crypto space, including
investor knowledge and behavior. Monitoring tools could include web searches and horizon
scanning for emerging technologies using innovation offices.
Prioritizing resources. Because regulators have limited available resources, the cost and
feasibility of their approach are important. For example, in countries with small or
undeveloped crypto markets, it may be sufficient to devote limited resources initially, while
monitoring new developments in the market and assessing whether more resources are
needed.
Scope. Along with prioritization, the scope of regulation needs to be considered. In
general, all entities with key functions relating to crypto assets should be regulated.
Greater focus should be given to regulation in jurisdictions where unbacked crypto assets
and stablecoins are supported by centralized entities like regulated financial institutions,
exchanges, and wallets, in order to limit potential contagion and systemic risk. In
```
jurisdictions where crypto assets are not widely used or remain niche (e.g., NFTs and utility
```
```
tokens), systemic risk is limited and, therefore, significant regulation is not necessary. In
```
these markets, regulation should focus on user education and consumer warnings.
Further, in some markets, like in the UK, crypto trading is mainly speculative, where
regulation can be better addressed by other entities like consumer protection agencies. If
crypto service providers are active in multiple jurisdictions globally, supervision and
regulation are most effective if there is cross-border cooperation between regulatory
entities. This would allow them to tailor the scope of activities and focus on the highest risk
areas.
Domestic collaboration. The various aspects of regulating crypto assets often involve
multiple domestic regulatory authorities, each looking to address specific risks.
Coordination between these entities is important and should be tailored to specific risks,
including determining responsibility for AML/CFT mandates and legal issues.
Continuous risk assessment. Regulators often aim for technological neutrality, striving
neither to favor nor to discriminate against various technologies. However, technology in
crypto markets often changes quickly and materially, so regulation may need to shift to
consistently address the greatest risks. For example, the introduction of DLT represented a
new technology for crypto assets that allowed users to trade fractional assets on a
decentralized platform with significantly greater liquidity than in traditional asset markets.
Networks using DLT also often have different objectives, with some focusing on speed and
efficiency, while others focus on transparency. To most effectively address the risks of these
new technologies, regulation should be tailored and customized to address unique risks.
Given that crypto asset providers often operate internationally, both domestic and
international cooperation between regulatory authorities is needed to reduce
inconsistencies in laws and regulations, and effectively sanction and prosecute crypto
cases. International standard-setting bodies like the IMF can play a significant role in
assisting with these cooperation efforts.
MODULE QUIZ 105.2
1. A general ban on crypto assets would likely be:
A. effective in the long run only.
B. effective in the short run only.
C. effective in both the short and long runs.
D. ineffective in both the short and long runs.
KEY CONCEPTS
LO 105.a
Crypto assets are digital assets that use cryptography and distributed ledger technology
```
(DLT) to record transactions. DLT is a sequenced and standardized record of an activity that
```
is cryptographically secured on a network. Blockchain is a type of DLT where a block is linked
to the previous block.
The six broad categories of crypto assets are as follows:
Unbacked crypto assets are not backed by other assets. They are a means of exchange but
do not meet all definitions of money.
```
Nonfungible tokens (NFTs) represent rights of product ownership.
```
Security tokens represent a blockchain-based ownership interest in an asset.
Utility tokens represent blockchain-based rights to products/services.
Stablecoins offer stable price value backed by other currencies or assets.
```
Central bank digital currencies (CBDCs) are issued by central banks as a digital form of
```
money.
LO 105.b
Entities within the crypto ecosystem include issuers, crypto asset exchanges, wallet providers,
validators/miners, and regulated financial institutions, each subject to unique risks and
regulatory challenges.
The issuance of unbacked crypto assets is typically decentralized, creating risks related to
limited disclosure, operational risks, and susceptibility to cyberattacks. Regulatory responses
focus on improving white paper quality, notification requirements, suitability, marketing
disclosures, and consumer education.
Crypto asset exchanges offer services like direct retail access and custody, but they face
operational and cyber risks. The IOSCO offers a regulatory framework focused on
governance, various controls, integrity, and transparency.
Regulation for wallet providers focuses on mitigating risks related primarily to third-party
custodial wallets and “hot” wallets stored online. Areas of regulation include
segregation/safekeeping, operational and cyber resilience, record keeping, AML/CFT
compliance, reporting/regulation, write-down arrangements, and conflicts of interest.
Regulation related to DLT in financial services aims to address risks related to interoperability
issues, fragmentation, and financial stability, but risks vary between private/permissioned and
public/permissionless blockchains.
Regulated financial institutions have both direct and indirect exposure to crypto assets, which
necessitates a conservative regulatory approach to managing risks, including capital
deductions and higher risk weights, and monitoring derivatives and loan exposures.
LO 105.c
Regulating crypto assets in a national setting may be sufficient in the absence of harmonized
global regulation. Domestic regulation can be bespoke or broad. Examples of different
```
regulatory focus include stablecoins and tokens (EU, UK) and operational aspects (Japan,
```
```
Albania). Jurisdictions can be crypto friendly (Switzerland) or restrictive (Nigeria).
```
Activities relating to crypto assets can bypass domestic rules, especially where monitoring
and enforcement is lacking. As a result, stronger international cooperation among regulatory
authorities is needed to address risks and systemic concerns.
Instead of broad bans on crypto assets, regulation should mainly focus on clarifying rules
relating to the economic functions of crypto assets and technical designs.
```
The BCBS proposes to categorize banks’ asset holdings as either (1) lower-risk or (2) higher-
```
```
risk (traditional) assets. Lower-risk assets would have similar or lower risk weights than
```
traditional assets, while higher-risk assets would have significantly higher risk weights.
LO 105.d
```
The Bali Fintech Agenda (BFA) is a global regulatory and supervisory framework for
```
regulators that addresses fintech risks, including crypto assets and technologies. It includes
the following considerations for crypto asset regulation:
Monitoring new developments and technologies in the crypto space
Prioritizing resources, with most resources allocated to jurisdictions that have a significant
crypto presence to contain systemic risk
Scope of regulation, to determine which aspects of crypto activities should be regulated
Domestic collaboration and coordination between various regulatory authorities to address
specific risks
Continuous risk assessment to identify changing risks and provide the strongest protection
to markets and consumers, often through cross-border collaboration

Module Quiz 105.1
1. B Unbacked crypto assets typically have high volatility and low intrinsic value (with value
```
determination based on supply and demand), and they are often used for speculative
```
activities. This makes them unsuitable to be considered money because they do not fully
meet all three definitions of money: medium of exchange, store of value, and unit of
account.
Choice A is incorrect because the volatility of unbacked crypto asset values is not
inherently due to DLT but rather to changes in market supply and demand. Choice C is
incorrect because the immutability of records is not related to their function as money
but rather to the security and trustworthiness of the transaction records. Choice D is
incorrect because unbacked crypto assets are transferable and, therefore, could meet
```
the definition of a medium of exchange. (LO 105.a)
```
2. C The decentralized nature of crypto asset issuance, especially of unbacked crypto assets,
provides anonymity, liquidity, and broad access to participants. However, these features
complicate adequate regulatory monitoring and oversight, because disclosure on the
issuers is usually limited, and evaluating the technology used or intended purpose of the
assets is often difficult.
Choice A is incorrect because the lack of existing regulations would create opportunities
for, rather than hinder, regulatory oversight. Choices B and D are incorrect because high
```
volatility or complex technology alone do not hinder regulatory oversight (many
```
```
regulated entities have high volatility and complex technologies). (LO 105.b)
```
3. D The framework to regulate crypto asset exchanges is outlined by the International
```
Organization of Securities Commissions (IOSCO). The framework focuses on regulatory
```
requirements related to prudent governance, various controls, improved integrity and
transparency, clear rules for product offerings, and mitigating conflicts of interest.
Choice A is incorrect because, unlike the framework for issuers that requires suitability
tests relating to the end user, the framework for exchanges does not contemplate
suitability tests for participants. Choice B is incorrect because variability in regulatory
approaches would likely be needed, even within a global framework, to account for
differences in prudential regulation approaches and the nature and size of local
exchanges. Choice C is incorrect because the framework does not impose a requirement
```
to use cold wallets, although it highlights the advantages. (LO 105.b)
```

obscure their locations using VPNs, or they could trade on unregulated exchanges to
bypass sanctions and capital flow restrictions. Crypto service providers could simply shift
their services to less regulated jurisdictions. However, instead of a general ban,
```
restricting some aspects of crypto assets could prove effective in the short run; for
```
example, restricting derivatives use related to crypto assets or restricting the use of
```
crypto assets as payments for goods and services may be possible. (LO 105.c)

The following is a review of the Current Issues in Financial Markets principles designed to address the learning objectives set
```
forth by GARP®. Cross-reference to GARP assigned reading—International Monetary Fund (IMF).
