READING 106
TOKENIZATION AND FINANCIAL MARKET
INEFFICIENCIES
Study Session 15
EXAM FOCUS
This reading examines how tokenization on digital ledgers can reduce market inefficiencies
while also introducing new risks. For the exam, understand the tokenization process and the
three core features of digital ledgers: trust, sharedness, and programmability. Also, be
familiar with the major market frictions, including those related to externalities, internalities,
and market power, and how tokenization can help mitigate these frictions by automating
processes, reducing reliance on intermediaries, and enabling faster settlement. In addition,
know that digital ledgers can also create or intensify risks, including the propagation of
financial shocks, increased operational vulnerabilities, and new forms of market power
concentration. Finally, understand the different tokenization models used to achieve trust,
sharedness, and programmability, as well as the conditions under which trust in these models
could fail.
MODULE 106.1: TOKENIZATION FOUNDATIONS, FEATURES, AND
MARKET FRICTIONS
LO 106.a: Explain the process of tokenization and describe the fundamental
features of tokenized assets and digital ledgers.
Tokens and Tokenization
Tokens are assets or representations of assets created on a digital ledger. Put simply, a
digital token functions like a digital certificate of ownership that can be transferred between
parties through a secure digital system. The process of creating these tokens is known as
tokenization. The key features of tokenized systems—including trust, sharedness, and
programmability—can vary by degree, and tokens may move across different ledgers to find
```
more suitable combinations of these features. Overall, tokenization is not a single technology;
```
rather, it is a collection of tools and approaches designed to make financial assets easier to
transfer, automate, and manage securely.
Tokenization can take two different forms. Native tokens are assets that are created directly
on a ledger and exist only within that system. Examples include cryptocurrencies such as
Bitcoin and Ether, as well as digital art. Non-native tokens are digital representations of
assets that exist outside the ledger. Virtually any asset can be tokenized in this way, from
financial instruments like equities, bonds, and commodities to real estate and physical
artwork. Tokens can also represent assets that are migrated from another ledger, where the
original asset is destroyed on the source ledger and reissued as a native token on the new
ledger.
Trust, Sharedness, and Programmability
For tokenization to function effectively, the digital ledger must allow users to own and
transfer assets, users must trust the system to record ownership and transactions accurately,
and the system must be programmable so that it can automate instructions such as paying
interest or enforcing trading rules. The effectiveness of tokenization depends on the following
three key features of a ledger:
1.Trust. This is necessary so that users can be confident that their assets cannot be moved
without their consent, and that transactions will be executed reliably and accurately.
2.Sharedness. This means that the ledger is accessible to all relevant parties, that asset
ownership can be transferred between them, and that ownership records are updated
directly on the ledger.
3.Programmability. This allows the ledger to incorporate smart contracts, which is
immutable code that automates instructions. These can be used to create assets, transfer
assets only when certain conditions are met, or ensure compliance with rules and
regulations. This capability makes it possible to bundle complex transactions together and
ensure they operate exactly as intended. For example, smart contracts could be used to
create coupon-paying transferable bonds or restrict transactions to qualified investors.
LO 106.b: Describe frictions and inefficiencies that can arise during different
phases of an asset’s lifecycle and explain how the different features of
tokenization can both increase and mitigate risks related to these frictions.
Market Frictions in Traditional Asset Markets
Participants in financial markets face several types of market frictions, which are a subset
of market inefficiencies. These frictions can arise at various stages of an asset’s lifecycle,
including issuance, trading, servicing, and redemption. Key frictions include the following:
Information asymmetries. This is where one party has more information than others
```
(e.g., a company owner during an IPO).
```
Search frictions. This means there is difficulty finding suitable trading partners.
Transaction costs. These are fees, trading costs, and opportunity costs, such as the time
spent completing a transaction.
Counterparty risk. This is the risk that one party fails to meet its obligations. This risk is
```
especially significant in derivatives, where exposures can arise both at initiation (e.g., when
```
```
paying a fee or premium) and throughout the life of the contract (e.g., when the buyer has
```
```
a future right to transact).
```
The presence of specialized intermediaries helps reduce these frictions. For example,
investment banks mitigate information asymmetries by conducting extensive due diligence on
companies issuing assets and by underwriting new stocks or bonds. Central securities
```
depositories (CSDs) and central clearinghouses (CCHs) manage settlement and clearing,
```
reducing counterparty risk by ensuring that both sides of a trade have sufficient funds or
```
assets to meet their obligations. Central counterparties (CCPs), a subcategory of central
```
```
clearinghouses (CCHs), add an extra layer of risk mitigation by interposing themselves as the
```
counterparty to all trading parties in a derivatives contract and, in the event of default,
covering nonpayment or nondelivery using the defaulting party’s margin and collateral and—if
needed—the CCP’s guarantee fund.
Transacting in financial markets also gives rise to externalities, internalities, and market
power issues.
Externalities occur when the actions of one participant affect others. These can be
positive or negative, though even positive externalities can lead to socially inefficient
outcomes. For example,network effects are a positive externality in which liquidity
improves as more participants join a market, yet the number of participants may still
remain below the socially optimal level. Negative externalities includemoral hazard, where
```
intermediaries take excessive risk because they expect government guarantees or bailouts;
```
in such cases, the intermediary keeps the profits but shares the losses with others.
Internalities arise when participants take actions that are not in their own best interest,
such as a retail investor trading without fully understanding the risks involved.
```
Market power refers to the ability to influence prices or terms (e.g., when a financial
```
institution charges above-market fees because barriers to switching make it difficult for
```
users to move elsewhere).
```
Mitigating Market Frictions Through Tokenization
Tokenization can reduce certain frictions more effectively and at lower cost than traditional
```
asset markets throughout an asset’s lifecycle, including (1) issuance, (2) trading, and (3)
```
servicing and redemption.
Asset Issuance
Issuing financial assets in traditional markets, including equities and bonds, requires
intermediaries to create records and manage transactions. These intermediaries—such as
```
registrars (which keep the formal register of security holders), banks, and trust companies—
```
help lower costs through economies of scale given large transaction volumes, but they also
charge fees for their services. With tokenization, a shared and programmable ledger can link
assets directly to owners’ accounts, eliminating the need for separate recordkeeping and
reducing transaction costs. It also removes the need for stand-alone custodial services,
resulting in further cost savings.
The sharedness and programmability features of tokenization also generate cost savings and
improve efficiency in asset issuance. Smart contracts can automate complex processes, such
as auctions for new shares, by executing predefined rules once specified conditions are met.
This reduces the time and resources required to manage these events. Programmability also
allows key data to be embedded directly within the token, making it easier for investors to
access asset and company information. This is particularly valuable for smaller issuances,
where such information is often limited in traditional markets. For example, when BNP Paribas
issued a bond in tokenized form in 2022, the term sheet and environmental, social, and
```
governance (ESG) data were embedded in the token, increasing transparency for investors.
```
Asset Trading
Asset trading in traditional markets involves several frictions, including counterparty risk,
nonsimultaneous settlement, and transaction costs. Although CSDs and CCHs help mitigate
counterparty risk, they charge fees to cover their operational costs. Tokenization enables
simultaneous or even instantaneous settlement through smart contracts, which automate
trades and reduce costs by eliminating the need for intermediaries. For example, a self-
executing smart contract will only complete a trade if both the buyer’s cash and the seller’s
securities are locked in, helping lower transaction costs and accelerate settlement. Smart
contracts can also reduce counterparty risk in multistep transactions such as cross-currency
trades.
Tokenized assets on a digital ledger can also facilitate instantaneous trade settlement,
meaning transactions are settled as soon as parties agree on a trade. In traditional markets,
this is not possible because CCHs require time to coordinate and update multiple ledgers.
Instantaneous settlement for tokenized assets reduces costs and transaction times and can
improve liquidity and capital allocation.
A shared and programmable ledger can also reduce search frictions, particularly in over-the-
```
counter (OTC) markets where information and liquidity are limited. By giving users direct
```
access to a single shared ledger, tokenization makes it easier to find trading counterparties
and helps lower costs such as bid-ask spreads, which reduces the need for broker-dealers.
Nevertheless, even with shared and programmable ledgers, tokenization cannot fully
eliminate counterparty risk when a party fails to meet its obligations between contract
initiation and execution. To mitigate this risk, tokenized systems could incorporate a CCP or
require that collateral be locked up for the duration of the contract. However, locking up
assets as collateral creates opportunity costs and may discourage investors from using
tokenized trades.
Asset Servicing and Redemption
Servicing and redeeming assets in traditional markets creates transaction cost frictions related
to collecting and transmitting interest, principal, and dividend payments as well as
maintaining up-to-date records of asset owners. Tokenization using a shared and
programmable ledger can automate these payments through smart contracts, linking assets
directly to owners and reducing servicing costs. For example, a programmable ledger can
specify when payments are due and how much to pay per asset token, and can even embed
dividend distribution decisions.
MODULE QUIZ 106.1
1. A financial institution is evaluating whether to place a new asset onto a digital ledger. The
```
institution wants the asset to (1) exist solely on the ledger, (2) be transferable only with the
```
```
owner’s explicit authorization, and (3) automatically execute coupon payments without manual
```
intervention. According to the definition of tokenization, which of the following combinations best
satisfies these requirements? The asset must be:
A. issued as a native token.
B. issued as a non-native token backed by an off-ledger asset.
C. migrated from another ledger, with the original version destroyed.
D. represented as a non-native token whose transfers rely on a centralized administrator.
2. The programmability feature of tokenized ledgers would be least effective at mitigating
counterparty risk under which of the following scenarios?
A. Automating interest payments on bonds through smart contracts.
B. Enabling simultaneous settlement of trades using smart contracts.
C. Managing cross-currency trades that involve multiple trading steps.
D. Managing derivatives trades without requiring collateral to be locked up.
MODULE 106.2: EXTERNALITIES, INTERNALITIES, MARKET
POWER, AND TOKENIZATION MODELS
LO 106.c: Explain how the use of tokenization and digital ledgers can impact
financial market externalities, including those related to the transmission of
shocks across the financial system, investments in market infrastructure, and
network and knowledge effects.
Tokenization and tokenized ledgers can significantly improve interconnectedness, trading
liquidity, and transaction speeds. However, as mentioned previously, they can also introduce
new risks related to externalities, internalities, and market power.
Shock Transmission Externalities
In traditional markets, high leverage, low funding liquidity, and strong interconnectedness
among institutions make it easier for shocks to spread throughout the financial system,
increasing risks to overall stability. Highly leveraged institutions are more likely to take on
greater risk, altering their funding mix toward debt relative to equity. This allows them to
retain the upside from equity returns while shifting potential losses from failure or bankruptcy
onto other institutions or the public through bailouts. When institutions are closely
interconnected, a shock affecting one institution can trigger a domino effect, causing losses
for many others and amplifying financial stress across the system.
Tokenization can increase the impact of shocks, especially as transaction speeds and leverage
increase. Faster transaction speeds also make it more difficult for policymakers to contain the
costs associated with externalities. In addition, tokenization can lower the issuance cost of
debt compared with conventional bonds, making borrowing more attractive and potentially
encouraging higher leverage. This increases the risk of financial instability if institutions
acquire excessive debt.
Tokenization can also affect financial institutions’ funding liquidity by shifting retail deposits.
Because tokenization makes it easier for retail investors to place funds into financial assets
outside of traditional bank deposits, banks may experience declining retail deposit levels and
become more reliant on wholesale funding. Access to wholesale funding can dry up quickly
during periods of market stress, increasing the risk of defaults and triggering spillover effects
across the financial system.
The increased interconnectedness of shared and programmable ledgers also makes it easier
for investors to access a broader set of complex and linked assets, including those tied to
other assets or pegged to different currencies. Complex financial products may involve
rehypothecation, where collateral is reused across multiple transactions. As more investors
are able to move funds quickly and at low cost across various assets and markets, shocks can
spread more rapidly. For example, a sudden drop in the value of one asset could trigger rapid
selloffs in multiple markets, amplifying the initial shock and making it more difficult for
regulators to respond and contain risks.
Programmable ledgers also facilitate automated trading with much faster execution speeds.
Smart contracts using rules-based algorithms can execute trades instantly or nearly instantly
and can bundle complex transactions together, which may lead to rapid market movements.
Automated trading can sometimes contribute to flash crashes, where prices drop sharply in a
short period because a chain of self-executing smart contracts triggers large volumes of
trades.
Market Infrastructure Investment Externalities
As more users rely on and transact through digital ledgers, interconnectedness increases and
transaction speeds improve. However, this also raises the costs associated with market
infrastructure investment, because operators need to increase spending to mitigate the risks
of operational failures and cyberattacks. If private operators underinvest in operational
security, the resulting risks can affect all participants. For example, a single point of failure in
a dominant ledger could disrupt the entire market, underscoring the need for stronger
security measures.
Tokenization increases the importance of market infrastructure investment because the
potential impact of failures grows with the number of users and interconnected transactions.
Risks include human and software errors, fraud, and system failures. Concentration risk is
also a concern, because a widely used digital ledger can become a target for cyberattacks,
potentially causing broader disruption across the financial system.
Network Externalities
Network externalities, or network effects, refer to the value an agent gains from joining a
ledger. A larger number of users increases market liquidity and strengthens incentives for
innovation, which in turn makes the ledger more attractive to new participants. The
sharedness feature of tokenization is closely connected to liquidity. For example, moving an
OTC market onto a digital ledger can improve liquidity by standardizing contracts, centralizing
price information, and reducing search frictions. As liquidity grows, transaction costs fall and
bid-ask spreads narrow, which encourages even more users to join and reinforces the positive
cycle.
Tokenized ledgers also support asset fractionalization, which allows retail investors to
purchase fractional shares and diversify their portfolios more easily. Although some
intermediaries already offer fractional shares of certain equities, tokenization enables
fractionalization across a wider range of assets, including equities and bonds. When tokens
are issued directly by the asset issuer, the process can reduce or even eliminate intermediary
risks, improving market access particularly for smaller investors.
The impact on liquidity and the strength of network externalities depend on the
interoperability of ledgers. If tokenization results in multiple noninteroperable ledgers, market
liquidity can decline because users only have access to segmented pools of assets.
Fragmentation across competing platforms can also increase transaction costs and search
frictions, reducing the benefits of network effects. This risk is heightened when private
entities establish ledgers without coordination, or without regulatory requirements for
interoperability, which leads to lower sharedness and reduced market efficiency.
Knowledge Externalities
When innovations or new knowledge benefit market participants beyond the original creator,
positive knowledge externalities arise. These externalities grow as the number of platform
users increases. Because tokenization supports open code development, it allows innovators
to build on existing smart contracts and applications at low or no cost, creating wider benefits
for users.
However, this can reduce incentives for further innovation unless the shared user base is
large enough to encourage companies to continue investing in new technologies. Open code
access also lowers the incentive for significant research and development because code can
be quickly copied and improved by others. Although licensing and patenting can help protect
innovations, they also limit the benefits of open-source development.
LO 106.d: Explain how the proliferation of distributed ledger technologies can
impact retail investors and brokerage firms.
```
Distributed ledger technology (DLT), including shared ledgers that support tokenized
```
assets, can significantly affect both retail investors and brokerage firms by changing how
assets are traded, transferred, and serviced.
Retail Investor Internalities
Tokenized ledgers that allow retail investors to trade directly can amplify internalities,
particularly when investors make decisions that are not in their own best interest. These
internalities arise when investors trade securities whose risks they do not fully understand,
especially when they bypass financial advisors to avoid fees. This can result in insufficient
diversification and expose investors to large and sudden losses from risky or complex assets
and derivatives, especially in the absence of strong consumer financial protections.
Market Power Inefficiencies
Market power inefficiencies occur when a dominant entity can charge above-market prices or
fees due to limited competition. In traditional markets, brokers charge fees to cover costs and
generate profit, and retail investors are often discouraged from switching brokers because the
process is time-consuming and cumbersome. For example, transferring assetsin kind,
meaning moving the securities themselves rather than selling them and transferring cash,
may require going through a clearinghouse in the United States, which adds delays and
complexity. As a result, brokers can continue charging higher fees without losing clients.
On a digital ledger, changing brokers and transferring assets can be instantaneous because
transactions and ownership records are immediately verifiable. These features also eliminate
the need for a clearinghouse, which reduces costs. Additional cost reductions may arise if
brokers lower their fees in response to greater competitive pressure, or if increased market
participation enables them to achieve economies of scale and operate more efficiently.
Direct trading by retail investors, which allows them to purchase assets directly from issuers,
would put additional pressure on brokers to lower their fees. However, in most countries,
trading is still required to be conducted through licensed brokers.
Privately owned ledgers can also create market power inefficiencies. Private owners may
charge high fees, known asmonopoly rents, with fees rising as the ledger becomes more
widely shared and dominant. They can also monetize privileged data by offering credit, selling
the data, or charging higher fees to the most active traders. A private owner might initially
provide free access to attract users when the ledger is new but then begin charging fees once
the ledger gains popularity and secures a strong market position.
LO 106.e: Compare different models and methods that are currently available to
achieve sharedness, programmability, and trust on a ledger, and describe the
conditions under which trust can fail in such models.
Tokenization Models
There are three main models for achievingsharedness in tokenization. Each model differs in
how it establishes trust and programmability.
1. Single ledger models place all parties or their intermediaries on one system, and all
assets are created directly on that system.
2. Common ledger models allow representations of non-native assets that exist on other
ledgers, with the original assets placed in escrow and represented through escrow
certificates.
3. Compatible ledger models use standardized technology that allows different ledgers to
run the same programs and coordinate transfers, making them interoperable.
Programmability in digital ledgers means that the code and the database are integrated
within the system. This differs from traditional setups, where external users must send
instructions to a separate database operator. Internal programmability, where the code and
the database operate together, ensures that transactions are coherent and efficient. This
makes it easier to bundle or link smart contracts.
Trust in digital ledgers can be established through single-operator systems, permissionless
DLT, or permissioned DLT.
1. Single-operator systems are centralized, with one entity responsible for managing
records and executing and confirming transactions. Trust is based on the operator’s
credibility, supervision, and oversight.
2. Permissionless DLT relies on a decentralized network of nodes to verify and validate
transactions, with no single party able to alter the ledger. Anyone can join the network,
transact, and participate in validation, and validators are typically incentivized through
rewards.
3. Permissioned DLT combines elements of both approaches, allowing only authorized
participants—approved by a central entity or consortium—to access or validate
transactions.
Trust Failures in Tokenization Models
```
All three models (single-operator systems, permissionless DLT, and permissioned DLT) are
```
designed to establish trust among participants. Each model offers different tradeoffs with
regard to openness, security, and control. The level of trust in a ledger depends on its design
and on the mechanisms in place to prevent abuse, operational failures, or other technical
problems.
Because a permissionless DLT allows anyone to participate and validate transactions, it is
```
vulnerable to attacks that compromise the consensus mechanism. In a proof-of-work (PoW)
```
system, this can occur if an attacker gains control of more than 50% of the computing power
used to validate transactions. Trust can also be undermined if users manipulate transaction
fees offered by others, allowing them to position their own trades ahead of others in the
```
validation queue. This enables front running, including sandwich attacks (in which an attacker
```
```
places transactions before and after a victim’s transaction to generate profit). The absence of
```
a central authority also leaves external information more vulnerable to cyberattacks. In
addition, enforcing regulatory compliance is more challenging due to the decentralized
structure of permissionless ledgers.
As noted previously, a permissioned DLT is a hybrid between a single-operator system and a
permissionless DLT, so either type of design features could create trust failures. A
permissioned DLT limits participation to users certified by a central authority or consortium.
Although this structure can improve security and simplify regulation, trust failure may occur if
authorized participants collude to modify the ledger or its programming for their own benefit.
However, these participants generally have an incentive to maintain their reputations to earn
future profits, which helps limit such behavior. Regulatory oversight and penalties for
noncompliance also help discourage abuses of trust.
The risks of trust failure in a fully centralized single-operator system are similar to those in a
permissioned DLT. However, because trust in a single-operator system does not rely on
consensus among participants and instead depends entirely on the credibility of the operator
and the strength of legal protections, the risks are even higher.
MODULE QUIZ 106.2
1. Tokenization could unintentionally amplify financial instability in a highly interconnected market
primarily because tokenization:
A. lowers the cost of issuing debt.
B. enables instantaneous settlement of trades.
C. removes the need for broker intermediation.
D. allows retail investors to buy fractional shares for diversification.
2. Which of the following risks is most likely to arise when tokenized ledgers are fragmented and
lack interoperability?
A. Amplified transmission of financial shocks.
B. Reduced programmability and limited automation.
C. Higher transaction costs despite increased liquidity.
D. Reduced network externalities accompanied by higher search frictions.
3. After a period of free access, the private operator of a now-dominant tokenized ledger begins
charging users a fee. Which of the following concepts best illustrates this change?
A. Internalities.
B. Knowledge externalities.
C. Market power inefficiencies.
D. Positive network externalities.
4. Which of the following trust models in tokenized ledgers is most vulnerable to collusion among
authorized participants?
A. Permissioned DLT.
B. Compatible ledger.
C. Permissionless DLT.
D. Single-operator system.
KEY CONCEPTS
LO 106.a
Tokens are digital assets or representations of assets recorded on a digital ledger that is
trusted, shared, and programmable. Tokenization is the process of creating these tokens.
```
Tokens may be native, meaning they exist only on the ledger (e.g., Bitcoin, Ether, digital art),
```
or non-native, meaning they represent assets that exist outside the ledger, such as real
estate, commodities, equities, or bonds. Tokenization functions effectively only when the
underlying ledger provides three essential features:
1. Trust: Ownership and transactions are recorded accurately and cannot be altered without
the owner’s consent.
2. Sharedness: All relevant parties can access the ledger, hold assets recorded on it, and
transfer ownership.
3. Programmability: Smart contracts can automate instructions and embed financial logic
directly into assets.
LO 106.b
Traditional financial markets face frictions such as information asymmetries, search problems,
transaction costs, and counterparty risk, which can arise at any stage of an asset’s lifecycle.
Specialized intermediaries such as CSDs, CCHs, and CCPs help reduce these frictions through
due diligence, settlement, and clearing, but they charge fees that add to overall transaction
costs.
Market activity can also generate externalities that affect other participants, internalities
where investors make decisions that are not in their own best interest, and market power
that allows institutions to influence prices or terms.
Tokenization can mitigate many of these inefficiencies by using shared and programmable
ledgers to automate processes and reduce the need for intermediaries. Asset issuance,
trading, and servicing/redeeming can be streamlined through smart contracts that lower costs
and improve efficiency. However, tokenization cannot fully eliminate counterparty risk during
the life of a contract. Addressing this remaining risk may require involving a CCP or locking up
collateral, which can create opportunity costs and discourage investor participation.
LO 106.c
Tokenization and digital ledgers can amplify the transmission of shocks across the financial
system by increasing interconnectedness, speeding up transactions, and encouraging higher
leverage. Lower debt issuance costs make borrowing more attractive—and in a highly
interconnected system, disruptions can spread quickly and increase financial stability risks.
Tokenization also allows retail investors to shift funds out of traditional bank deposits, which
can force banks to rely more on wholesale funding. Wholesale funding is riskier and can
disappear rapidly during market stress, creating additional vulnerabilities.
Programmable ledgers enable automated trading and greater access to complex,
interconnected products. During periods of market stress, rapid automated execution and fast
asset movements can trigger selloffs across multiple markets, potentially leading to flash
crashes or sharp price swings.
The widespread use of shared ledgers also increases the importance of investing in secure
market infrastructure, because operational failures or cyberattacks could affect all users.
Underinvestment by private operators can create systemic risks.
Network externalities play a significant role in tokenized markets: as more users join a ledger,
liquidity improves and innovation becomes easier, increasing the value of participation.
However, fragmented or noninteroperable ledgers weaken these benefits by reducing liquidity,
increasing search frictions and transaction costs, and lowering overall market efficiency.
LO 106.d
Direct trading by retail investors on tokenized ledgers can increase internalities when
investors make decisions that are not in their own best interest. This can occur when
individuals trade assets—particularly, complex products and derivatives—without fully
understanding the risks involved.
Tokenization can also reduce the market power held by brokers. In traditional markets,
investors face delays and costs when switching brokers, which allows brokers to maintain
high fees. On tokenized ledgers, switching brokers and transferring assets can be
instantaneous, which lowers costs and may encourage brokers to reduce fees. However,
privately owned ledgers can introduce new market power inefficiencies. Owners of dominant
platforms may charge high fees or monetize privileged data, and these inefficiencies can
grow as the platform attracts more users.
LO 106.e
Token models require sharedness, programmability, and trust. Sharedness can be achieved
through single ledgers that centralize all transactions on one system, common ledgers that
create representations of non-native assets through escrow, and compatible ledgers that use
standardized technology to enable interoperability across different ledgers. Programmability
ensures that the code and database are integrated, allowing smart contracts to execute
directly on the ledger and improving efficiency. Trust can be established through single-
operator systems, permissionless DLT, or permissioned DLT.
Each tokenization model faces its own trust risks. Single-operator systems rely entirely on the
operator’s credibility and the strength of legal protections. Permissionless DLT is vulnerable to
cyberattacks, manipulation of transaction ordering or fees, and attacks on the consensus
mechanism if an attacker gains sufficient computing power. Permissioned DLT can fail if
enough authorized participants collude to alter the ledger for their own benefit.
ANSWER KEY FOR MODULE QUIZZES
Module Quiz 106.1
1. A A native token is created directly on the ledger and exists only within that system, which
satisfies the requirement that the asset exist solely on the ledger. For tokenization to
function, the ledger must also provide three essential features: trust, sharedness, and
```
programmability. (LO 106.a)
```
2. D Counterparty risk in derivatives arises not only at contract initiation but also throughout
the life of the contract if either party fails to meet its obligations. While programmability
allows for instantaneous settlement at initiation, it does not address ongoing
counterparty exposure unless additional safeguards are in place. Effective mitigation
during the contract period typically requires a CCP or collateral that remains locked up
for the duration of the agreement. When collateral is not required, programmability
alone cannot manage this exposure. However, collateral lockup can create opportunity
```
costs that may reduce investor participation in tokenized derivatives. (LO 106.b)
```
Module Quiz 106.2
1. A By lowering the cost of issuing debt, tokenization can encourage greater borrowing and
lead institutions to take on more leverage. In a highly interconnected financial system,
higher leverage increases the vulnerability to shocks, allowing disturbances to spread
```
more quickly and amplifying overall financial instability. (LO 106.c)
```
2. D Fragmented and noninteroperable ledgers divide users across separate platforms,
reducing liquidity and weakening network externalities. This fragmentation increases
search friction and can raise transaction costs. While fragmentation reduces liquidity, it
```
does not amplify shock transmission; it is unrelated to programmability and automation,
```
```
which depend on ledger design rather than interoperability. (LO 106.c)
```
3. C Market power inefficiencies arise when a dominant entity is able to charge above-market
prices or fees due to limited competition. After the ledger becomes widely adopted and
gains market dominance, the private operator can exploit this position by introducing
fees, often referred to as monopoly rents. This behavior reflects the operator’s ability to
```
leverage its market power once users are locked into the platform. (LO 106.d)
```
4. A In a permissioned DLT, only authorized participants can validate transactions and update
the ledger. Trust failure can occur if enough of these certified participants collude to
alter the ledger or its programming for their own benefit. A single-operator system is
vulnerable to operator failure rather than collusion. Permissionless DLTs typically involve
many independent validators, making collusion difficult, although they face other risks
such as attacks on the consensus mechanism. The interoperability of compatible ledgers
```
does not determine their trust characteristics. (LO 106.e)
```
The following is a review of the Current Issues in Financial Markets principles designed to address the learning objectives set
forth by GARP®. Cross-reference to GARP assigned reading—Martinez.
READING 107
DIGITAL RESILIENCE AND FINANCIAL
```
STABILITY: THE QUEST FOR POLICY TOOLS IN
```
THE FINANCIAL SECTOR
Study Session 15
EXAM FOCUS
Digital resilience is crucial for maintaining financial stability as financial institutions move
toward an almost completely digital system. For the exam, understand the complexities
involved with the extensive and concentrated use of cloud computing by financial institutions.
There has been some progress in increasing cyber resilience regarding the mitigation of risks
posed by third-party service providers. However, challenges in fundamental measurement
principles and standardization still exist. In addition, understand the role of macroprudential
measures such as circuit breakers and cooperation arrangements for dealing with cyber
threats across the banking industry.
MODULE 107.1: CYBER THREATS AND DIGITAL RESILIENCE
LO 107.a: Describe characteristics of cyber risks and information/communication
```
technology (ICT) risks faced by financial institutions.
```
Cyber and ICT Risks
```
Cyber risk refers to the loss of confidentiality, integrity, and availability (CIA) of data and
```
data systems that result from a cyberattack. Information and communications
```
technology (ICT) risk refers to disruptions pertaining to information and communication
```
systems that may have similar effects as cyber risk—but, strictly speaking, do not stem from
cyberattacks.
The overwhelming level of digitalization at financial institutions, combined with the essential
nature of their services, places financial institutions in a vulnerable position regarding cyber
and ICT risks. The financial sector is responsible for managing an immense amount of funds
together with the expectation of providing uninterrupted transaction services and maintaining
confidentiality. This suggests a greater risk of cyberattacks and ICT breakdowns.
Based on a general increase in serious cybersecurity incidents, some financial institutions
have ranked cyber risk as more significant than market risk. To date, there have been major
examples of confidentiality breaches of client information, theft of funds, and denial-of-
```
service (DoS) attacks experienced by financial institutions worldwide. Reliable estimates of
```
losses arising from cyber risks are generally not possible to compute due to a lack of data. As
a result, the development of cyber insurance has been significantly hindered.
Cloud Computing
```
Cloud computing can be thought of as the provision of computer resources (e.g., storage,
```
```
servers, software) through the internet (i.e., “the cloud”). The cloud operates on a shared
```
system with multiple users, and its benefits include lower costs and greater operating
flexibility. Cloud service providers are expected to have extremely robust controls in place.
Financial institutions have strongly embraced cloud computing in recent years, which has
```
resulted in a corresponding decrease in cyber risks; however, it has also led to an increase in
```
ICT risks. The main issue is that the use of cloud computing moves some, but not all, of an
organization’s cyber risks to cloud service providers because the organization’s providers and
customers might not always have adequate controls.
The significant adoption of cloud computing by financial institutions has resulted in the
integration of cyber and ICT risks to an extent that there could be systemic implications
```
(discussed in the next section). Furthermore, cloud computing has needed to evolve (e.g.,
```
```
multiclouds) to deal with concentration risks, for example. The use of multiclouds limits the
```
amount of damage that could occur from a cyberattack because the computing assets are
spread out over multiple clouds, rather than just one. All major banks have adopted the use
```
of multiclouds (compared to less than half for asset management companies and insurance
```
```
companies). However, such evolution comes with unintended additional risks, such as weaker
```
stewardship over the distributed computing processes.
ICT risks in the form of physical outages have become a key concern, with about 80% of
cloud customers suffering at least one outage within the prior three-year period. During the
same period, roughly 20% of those outages were considered significant enough to noticeably
disrupt operations. Furthermore, climate change and rising energy costs may be causing
longer and more frequent outages. In short, cloud computing use is high—despite its
numerous and significant drawbacks.
Interactions Between Cyber Risks and Financial Stability
LO 107.b: Assess the interactions between cyber and ICT risks and financial risks
and explain how cyber and ICT risk events at financial institutions can lead to
systemic financial risk.
Cyber vulnerabilities that are triggered can cause negative financial impacts such as illiquidity,
excessive leverage, and loss of customer trust. The most important consideration is how
technology risks can lead to the realization of financial risks.
The modern-day design of cyber and ICT connections is highly integrated, which opens the
potential for significant contagion effects. Financial contagion can occur extremely rapidly
through the financial industry with the onset of just one key triggering event. The effects of
```
such systemic risks on the CIA triad (i.e., confidentiality, integrity, and availability) are wide-
```
ranging. For example, severity seems to be lower for data confidentiality, but higher for data
integrity and data/systems availability for operational use. Although the quantification of
systemic risks is important, it is also necessary to look at the qualitative effects of systemic
risks in terms of their impact on the CIA triad.
Because contagion effects are nonlinear in nature, it is challenging to predict the probability
of key events with reasonable accuracy. The solution is to use agent-based models, which
are computer simulations of actions and interactions among agents. These models quantify
the impacts of a cyber incident in terms of the CIA effects and how they affect the liquidity
and asset values of the financial institutions in question. However, such models are still in
their infancy stage, but their basic principles could be applied to developing cyber and ICT
stress tests.
Agent-based models are useful for tracing the source of systemic risks, as what appears to
happen in real life between technology and finance is not always completely accurate. For
example, a cyber incident could trigger a run on bank deposits but still fail to result in actual
withdrawals if the bank’s systems are disrupted and cannot process transactions. In that
regard, it would appear on the surface that technology was able to suppress the effects of
the contagion—but this is clearly misleading because bank customers have other alternatives
available, such as withdrawing funds they have on deposit with other banks.
Virtually all financial institutions are digitalized now, and they have become integrated and
more reliant on each other due to cloud computing, networks, and shared software.
Therefore, it is necessary for each participating institution to be reliable enough to quickly
recover from a cyber incident to ensure the continuity of key functions in the general financial
system. In that regard, given the importance of ICT functions in the financial system, each
```
institution must be “tested” to ensure it is sufficiently reliable (which does not necessarily
```
```
mean 100% reliability).
```
The ultimate result of cyber and ICT risks, combined with the vulnerabilities of financial
institutions, is the overwhelming loss of confidence of customers. This combination has been
seen frequently in the past with runs on bank deposits stemming from customer panic or
financial fundamentals. Building up liquidity buffers helps to mitigate the effects of bank runs
caused by cyber shocks in theory, although history has shown that there has been a high
level of liquidity in the financial system for a reasonable amount of time, thereby not
requiring such buffers to be used. A more accurate assessment of the impact of a loss of
confidence does require the use of agent-based models.
Financial Stability Policies and Tools
LO 107.c: Describe potential macroprudential tools and policy measures that can
be used to address cyber risks and ICT risks and explain challenges to the
adoption of each one.
Digital resilience covers both technological and financial aspects and involves micro-
oriented tools and framework policies. Micro-oriented tools deal with general operational
guidance, while framework policies deal with fundamental rules pertaining to network and
system operations. Due to the significant threats to digital resilience in recent years,
framework policies have seen major revisions to strengthen the defenses against new cyber
and ICT risks.
Policy Measures and Micro-Oriented Tools
```
The International Telecommunications Union (ITU) covers a wide range of legal,
```
technical, and other measures to promote cyber resilience. However, due to the complexity
involved, it is necessary to have certification and standardization of solutions as a starting
point for building cybersecurity defenses. With the many entities involved with cyber
resilience, there needs to be a well-coordinated effort to maximize efficiency and minimize
```
redundancy; therefore, the result has been the consolidation of numerous cybersecurity
```
entities when dealing with cybersecurity issues on a national level.
Compared to past rules, recent rules pertaining to operational resilience are more forward-
looking and principles-based approaches. This is necessary to evolve with the growing
complexity of the cyber resilience world. However, a principles-based approach is more useful
when working with the risk for a given entity as opposed to systemic risk, which involves
multiple entities.
Policies have long been in place for incident reporting and information sharing in the
```
European Union (EU)—but, in taking it a step further, the European Systemic Risk Board
```
```
(ESRB) has suggested the formation of a European-wide structure for coordinating incident
```
reporting.
```
Also in the EU, the Digital Operational Resilience Act (DORA) has provisions to respond
```
to ICT third-party risks associated with cloud computing. In addition, it includes provisions
```
dealing with penetration stress testing (i.e., cyberattack simulation), which is a useful exercise
```
to keep entities conscious of security issues on an ongoing basis.
Macroprudential Tools
Circuit Breakers
Circuit breakers are essentially a temporary pause on normal rules and regulations, and
they have been used more in the banking industry in recent years. In the context of major
cyber incidents impacting the banking industry, it makes sense to have circuit breakers to
minimize the negative impacts of customer loss of confidence and the resulting bank run on
deposits. Therefore, circuit breakers supplement the controls already in place regarding bank
deposits. Circuit breakers are analogous to a temporary stoppage to analyze the fallout from
a major attack and to determine the upcoming steps for recovery.
The challenge here is for the bank to prove that a given cyberattack is sufficiently serious to
be considered aforce majeure event that will provide some recovery time to the bank after
the attack. It could be argued that proper cyber controls and other preventative measures
```
could have been put in place to avoid the negative effects of a cyberattack; therefore, it is
```
not a force majeure event. At the same time, a compelling argument for the use of circuit
breakers is their ability to control and minimize the negative effects of a potential bank run
after a significant cyberattack on a bank.
Cooperative Arrangements
Cooperation among members may seem like an obvious solution for small- and medium-sized
organizations, but it has taken time to occur in the financial sector where most organizations
are large. With the development of information and knowledge-sharing resources within the
financial sector, there is evidence of growing cooperation among organizations to develop
more robust defenses against cyberattacks.
Within the United States, there has been progress in unifying the various cybersecurity
standards and in transitioning from tick-the-box-style compliance requirements to a
judgment-based system. The challenge remains to bring the level of standardization and
thorough reporting to an international level, although cooperation within the realm of incident
reporting has been on the rise in Europe as well as internationally.
ICT buffers to combat systemic risk are a good example of cooperation and allow for a
much quicker recovery time for those banks that have suffered a major cyberattack. ICT
buffers have been used in the past in the United States with its tri-party repo system. In the
```
United States, there is a collectively owned entity (Sheltered Harbor, LLC) whereby any co-
```
owner bank that suffers a serious cyberattack could allow its customers to transact on their
accounts at another co-owner bank. With consistent data formats and operating procedures
for storage and restoration of data, the risk to a co-owner bank from a cyberattack is
substantially reduced.
Structural Measures
The reach of macroprudential regulation has inevitably spread to systemically important
```
technological institutions (SITIs), given the numerous externalities arising from SITIs as well
```
as the significant dependence of the financial sector on SITIs. Alternatives to macroprudential
regulation would include more thorough evaluations of critical providers, strongly encouraging
multicloud services, and using private clouds.
Additionally, regulators could engage in “forced diversification” of firms to incentivize them to
use multiclouds or multiple types of software. Penalties for concentrated software use could
```
be used as negative motivators; however, penalties also introduce the issue of enforceability.
```
MODULE QUIZ 107.1
1. Which of the following statements regarding cloud storage and cloud computing is most
accurate?
A. All major banks have adopted the use of multiclouds.
B. The increased use of cloud computing in recent years by banks has resulted in decreases in
both cyber and ICT risks.
C. The advance of cloud storage and computing has allowed ICT risks and cybersecurity risks to
be treated independently for analytical purposes.
D. A cloud services audit firm has recently reported that about 50% of cloud customers have
experienced a physical outage in the past three years.
2. Which of the following statements regarding the interaction between cyber risks and financial
stability is most accurate?
A. Agent-based models are useful for tracing the source of systemic risks.
B. The severity of shocks tends to be higher for data confidentiality compared to data integrity.
C. The most important consideration is how financial risks can lead to the realization of
technology risks.
D. Shocks to the CIA triad can trigger financial sector vulnerabilities that are focused on a few
main areas, including profitability.
3. The concept of a bank being able to recover very quickly from a serious cyberattack by allowing
its customers to access their accounts from another bank is best described as which of the
following macroprudential tools?
A. Circuit breaker.
B. Structural measures.
C. Forced diversification.
D. Cooperative arrangement.
4. Which of the following statements regarding the use of circuit breakers is most accurate? Circuit
```
breakers:
```
A. have been used extensively in the banking industry in the past.
B. are used to deal with the effects of a loss of confidence by bank customers.
C. are an improvement; therefore, they are a valid substitute to the controls in place regarding
bank deposits.
D. are a long-term pause on normal rules and regulations to allow a bank sufficient time to
recover from a serious cyberattack.
KEY CONCEPTS
LO 107.a
```
Cyber risk refers to the loss of confidentiality, integrity, and availability (CIA) of data and data
```
systems that result from a cyberattack. ICT risk refers to disruptions pertaining to information
and communication systems that may have similar effects as cyber risk, but do not stem from
cyberattacks.
Financial institutions have strongly embraced cloud computing in recent years, which has
resulted in a corresponding decrease in cyber risks but also an increase in ICT risks. The
main issue is that the use of cloud computing moves some, but not all, of an organization’s
cyber risks to cloud service providers because the organization’s providers and customers
might not have adequate controls.
The significant adoption of cloud computing by financial institutions has resulted in the
integration of cyber and ICT risks to an extent that there could be systemic implications.
LO 107.b
Cyber vulnerabilities that are triggered can cause negative financial impacts such as illiquidity,
excessive leverage, and loss of customer trust. The most important consideration is how
technology risks can lead to the realization of financial risks.
The modern-day design of cyber and ICT connections is highly integrated, which opens the
potential for significant contagion effects. Financial contagion can occur extremely fast
through the financial industry with the onset of just one key triggering event.
Because contagion effects are nonlinear in nature, it is challenging to predict the probability
of key events with reasonable accuracy. The solution is to consider agent-based models to
quantify the impacts of a cyber incident in terms of the CIA effects and how they affect the
liquidity and asset values of the financial institutions in question.
LO 107.c
Regarding policy measures and micro-oriented tools, it is necessary to have certification and
standardization of solutions as a starting point for building cybersecurity defenses. Given the
many entities involved with cyber resilience, the result has been the consolidation of
numerous cybersecurity entities. Compared to past rules, recent rules pertaining to
operational resilience are more forward-looking and principles-based approaches. Other policy
measures deal with coordinated incident reporting and information sharing.
Regarding macroprudential tools, circuit breakers are essentially a temporary pause on
normal rules and regulations. In the context of major cyber incidents impacting the banking
industry, it makes sense to have circuit breakers to minimize the negative impacts of
customer loss of confidence and the resulting bank run on deposits. Another macroprudential
tool is cooperation, and one aspect may involve unifying the various cybersecurity standards
and transitioning from tick-the-box-style compliance requirements to a judgment-based
system. ICT buffers to combat systemic risk are a good example of cooperation and allow for
a much quicker recovery time for those banks that have suffered a major cyberattack. Finally,
the reach of macroprudential regulation has inevitably spread to systemically important
```
technological institutions (SITIs) given the numerous externalities arising from SITIs as well
```
as the significant dependence of the financial sector on SITIs.
ANSWER KEY FOR MODULE QUIZ
Module Quiz 107.1
1. A Within the financial sector, all major banks have adopted multicloud computing. With
more banks embracing cloud computing in recent years, it has resulted in a
corresponding decrease in cyber risks but also anincrease in ICT risks. The advance of
cloud storage and computing has resulted in acommingling of ICT and cybersecurity
risks with potential systemic impact. A cloud services audit firm has recently reported
```
that about80% (not 50%) of cloud customers have experienced a physical outage in
```
```
the past three years. (LO 107.a)
```
2. A Agent-based models are useful for tracing the source of systemic risks, as what appears
to happen in real life between technology and finance is not always completely accurate.
However, such models are still in their infancy stage, but their basic principles could be
applied to developing cyber and ICT stress tests.
There tends to be alower severity of shocks to data confidentiality and a higher severity
to data integrity and availability of data and systems. The most important consideration
is howtechnology risks can lead to the realization offinancial risks. Shocks to the CIA
triad can trigger financial sector vulnerabilities that are focused on a few main areas
```
such asliquidity, leverage, andtrust. (LO 107.b)
```
3. D In the United States, there is a collectively owned entity (Sheltered Harbor, LLC)
whereby any co-owner bank that suffers a serious cyberattack could allow its customers
to transact on their accounts at another co-owner bank. With consistent data formats
and operating procedures for storage and restoration of data, the risk to a co-owner
bank from a cyberattack is substantially reduced. This is a good example of a
```
cooperative arrangement. (LO 107.c)
```
4. B In the context of major cyber incidents impacting the banking industry, it makes sense
to have circuit breakers to minimize the negative impacts of customer loss of confidence
and the resulting bank run on deposits.
Circuit breakers have not been commonly used in the banking industry until recent
years. Circuit breakers are acomplement to the controls in place regarding bank
```
deposits. Circuit breakers are atemporary (and, therefore, likely short-term) pause on
```
normal rules and regulations to allow a bank sufficient time to recover from a serious
```
cyberattack. (LO 107.c)
```

USING THE CUMULATIVE Z-TABLE
Probability Example
```
Assume that the annual earnings per share (EPS) for a large sample of firms is normally
```
distributed with a mean of $5.00 and a standard deviation of $1.50. What is the approximate
probability of an observed EPS value falling between $3.00 and $7.25?
For z-value of 1.50: Use the row headed 1.5 and the column headed 0 to find the value
0.9332. This represents the area under the curve to the left of the critical value 1.50.
For z-value of –1.33: Use the row headed 1.3 and the column headed 3 to find the value
0.9082. This represents the area under the curve to the left of the critical value +1.33. The
area to the left of –1.33 is 1 − 0.9082 = 0.0918.
The area between these critical values is 0.9332 − 0.0918 = 0.8414, or 84.14%.
Hypothesis Testing—One-Tailed Test Example
A sample of a stock’s returns on 36 nonconsecutive days results in a mean return of 2.0%.
Assume the population standard deviation is 20.0%. Can we say with 95% confidence that
the mean return is greater than 0%?
The significance level = 1.0 − 0.95 = 0.05, or 5%.
Since this is a one-tailed test with an alpha of 0.05, we need to find the value 0.95 in the
cumulativez-table. The closest value is 0.9505, with a corresponding criticalz-value of 1.65.
Since the test statistic is less than the critical value, we fail to reject H0.
Hypothesis Testing—Two-Tailed Test Example
Using the previous assumptions, suppose that the analyst now wants to determine with 99%
confidence that the stock’s return is not equal to 0.0%.
Since this is a two-tailed test with an alpha of 0.01, there is a 0.005 rejection region in both
```
tails. Thus, we need to find the value 0.995 (1.0 − 0.005) in the table. The closest value is
```
0.9951, which corresponds to a criticalz-value of 2.58. Since the test statistic is less than the
critical value, we fail to reject H0 and conclude that the stock’s return equals 0.0%.
